The Maxim 'Writing is Like Speech' in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Explanation of the maxim and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'writing is like speech' to determine the legal effect of a written offer in a contract, and identify the conditions for valid written communication between absent parties.

Core Idea
Writing between absent parties is like speech between present ones — but effect for contracts begins upon receipt, not writing.

The Hanafi school recognizes that when parties are not face-to-face, written communication substitutes for oral speech. However, not all writing is equal. This lesson unpacks the conditions that make writing legally effective and the critical timing difference between contracts (requiring mutual consent) and unilateral acts like divorce or emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Identify the ruling: 'al-kitāb ka al-khiṭāb — writing is like speech'. Understand that this makes written communication between absent parties equivalent to oral communication between present parties.
This is a rasm rule (governing maxim) cited from the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah.
2
Apply the Conditions for Valid Writing
The writing must be mustabayyinah (legible, on a stable medium) and marsūmah (following customary formalities like names, signatures, paper). Check that these are present.
Writing in the air or on water is invalid; bone slabs or leaves may not meet marsūmah today.
3
Determine the Timing of Legal Effect for Contracts
For contracts requiring mutual consent, the offer and acceptance take effect only upon receipt and reading of the writing by the other party, not at the moment of writing.
This ensures that the offeree is aware of the offer before being bound.
4
Distinguish Contracts from Unilateral Dispositions
For acts like talaq (divorce) or i'tāq (emancipation), which do not depend on the other party's consent, writing alone with the conditions suffices; the effect begins at writing, not receipt.
This is an istithnā' (exception) to the general rule for contracts.
5
Apply to a Modern Scenario
Given a written offer sent by email: check conditions (legible, includes names), then determine that the contract becomes effective when the email is received and read (for contracts). For a written divorce sent by mail, it takes effect when the letter is written and sent (assuming conditions met).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً : الكتابة : فالكتابة بين الغائبين كالنطق بين الحاضرين وعلى هذا الاعتبار وضعت القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: الكتاب كالخطاب (المجلة / ٦٩). ويشترط في الكتابة لكي تعتبر أن تكون مُسْتَبينة، ومرسومة. - ومعنى كونها مستبينة أن تكون مكتوبة على شيء تظهر وتثبت عليه. فلا تعتبر الكتابة في الهواء؛ أو على سطح الماء. - ومعنى كونها مرسومة أن تكون مكتوبة بالطريقة المعتادة بين الناس في مراسم زمانهم وتقاليده، وذلك بأن تكون مصدرة باسم المرسل والمرسل إليه، أو مختومة أو موقعة من المرسل المتكلم، وأن تكون في زماننا على قرطاس لا على نحو ألواح العظام وجلود الحيوان وأوراق الشجر مما كان يكتب عليه في الماضي القديم. والرأي الفقهي في الإيجاب والقبول بالكتابة أن حكمها لا يبدأ منذ كتابة العبارة، بل عقب وصول الكتاب وقراءته. فعندئذ تأخذ العبارة مفعولها، فيعتبر الموجب موجباً والقابل قابلاً. وهذا في العقود، بخلاف التصرفات التي لا تتوقف على علم الطرف الآخر ورضاه كالطلاق، والإعتاق فإنها إذا كتبت كتابة مستوفية الشرائط، أي مستبينة مرسومة بأن كتب أحد إلى زوجته كتاباً يقول لها فيه : أنت
First: Writing. Writing between absent parties is like speech between those present, and upon this basis the legal maxim was laid down: writing is like address (Majallah, article 69). For writing to be considered, it must be legible (mustabayyinah) and inscribed (marsūmah). The meaning of legible is that it is written on something on which it appears and is established. So writing in the air or on the surface of water is not considered. The meaning of inscribed is that it is written in the customary manner among people in the conventions and traditions of their time, such that it is headed with the name of the sender and the recipient, or stamped or signed by the speaking sender, and that in our time it is on paper, not on bone slabs, animal skins, or tree leaves as in the ancient past. The juristic opinion regarding offer and acceptance by writing is that its ruling does not begin from the time of writing the expression, but rather after the arrival of the writing and its reading. Then the expression takes effect, so the offeror is considered an offeror and the acceptor an acceptor. This applies to contracts, as opposed to dispositions that do not depend on the other party's knowledge and consent, such as divorce and emancipation, for if they are written with writing that fulfills the conditions, i.e., legible and inscribed, as when one writes to his wife a letter saying to her: 'You are...'
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage explains the rasm rule for written contracts. The key maxim is 'writing is like address' (al-kitāb ka al-khiṭāb), meaning a written offer/acceptance between absent parties is equivalent to oral speech between present parties. Two conditions are required: mustabayyinah (legible, on a stable medium) and marsūmah (following customary format: with names, signature/seal, on paper in modern times). The legal effect begins only upon receipt and reading, not at the time of writing. This is for contracts requiring mutual consent. For unilateral acts like divorce or emancipation, writing alone is sufficient if conditions are met, because they do not require the other party's acceptance. The passage distinguishes between writing a contract (which creates obligations) and writing a deed (which merely documents).

Key Terms
مستبينة (mustabayyinah): Legible and stable; written on a medium where it appears and persists.
مرسومة (marsūmah): Inscribed following the customary formalities of the time (e.g., names, signature, paper).
خطاب (khiṭāb): Oral address; the spoken word in the presence of both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a written offer in a contract become legally effective according to this passage?
A) At the moment of writing.
B) Upon receipt and reading of the writing.
C) Upon signing by both parties.
D) Upon verbal confirmation.

Key Takeaway
Writing is like speech, but for contracts the effect is upon receipt and reading; for unilateral acts like divorce, writing alone suffices.

الكتاب كالخطاب: Writing as a Means of Contract Formation
Explains the Hanafi maxim 'الكتاب كالخطاب' with conditions and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'الكتاب كالخطاب' to distinguish between contracts and unilateral acts, identify the conditions for valid written legal acts, and construct a fatwa for written transactions.

Core Idea
Writing between absent parties is like speech between present — but takes effect upon receipt in contracts, and immediately in divorce/manumission.

This lesson explores the Hanafi legal maxim that writing between absent parties is equivalent to speech between present parties. The passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains the maxim's conditions and its differential application to bilateral contracts versus unilateral acts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis dealing with written communications in marriage, divorce, and commerce.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim and Its Conditions
Recall the maxim 'الكتاب كالخطاب' and its two conditions: mustabinah (written on a stable surface) and marsumah (conventional format for the time).
These conditions ensure the writing is reliable and recognizable.
2
Apply to Bilateral Contracts
Apply the maxim to offer and acceptance in contracts: the effect begins upon receipt and reading, not at the moment of writing.
This is because contracts require mutual knowledge and consent.
3
Apply to Unilateral Acts
Apply to divorce and manumission: these take effect immediately upon writing, as they do not depend on the other party's knowledge or consent.
Exception: if the act is conditioned on receipt, effect is delayed.
4
Identify the Exception
Note the exception for conditional divorce: if the writing says 'If this letter reaches you, you are divorced,' it takes effect upon receipt, like contracts.
This is governed by the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٧/٣٠ - أولاً : الكتابة :
فالكتابة بين الغائبين كالنطق بين الحاضرين وعلى هذا الاعتبار وضعت القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: الكتاب كالخطاب (المجلة / ٦٩) (١) . ويشترط في الكتابة لكي تعتبر أن تكون مُسْتَبينة، ومرسومة. - ومعنى كونها مستبينة أن تكون مكتوبة على شيء تظهر وتثبت عليه . فلا تعتبر الكتابة في الهواء؛ أو على سطح الماء.
- ومعنى كونها مرسومة أن تكون مكتوبة بالطريقة المعتادة بين الناس في مراسم زمانهم وتقاليده، وذلك بأن تكون مصدرة باسم المرسل والمرسل إليه (۲) ، أو مختومة أو موقعة من المرسل المتكلم، وأن تكون في زماننا على قرطاس لا على نحو ألواح العظام وجلود الحيوان وأوراق الشجر مما كان يكتب عليه في الماضي القديم.
والرأي الفقهي في الإيجاب والقبول بالكتابة أن حكمها لا يبدأ منذ كتابة العبارة، بل عقب وصول الكتاب وقراءته. فعندئذ تأخذ العبارة مفعولها، فيعتبر الموجب موجباً والقابل قابلاً.
وهذا في العقود، بخلاف التصرفات التي لا تتوقف على علم الطرف الآخر ورضاه كالطلاق ،والإعتاق فإنها إذا كتبت كتابة مستوفية الشرائط ، أي مستبينة مرسومة بأن كتب أحد إلى زوجته كتاباً يقول لها فيه : أنت طالق، فإنها تأخذ حكمها فور الكتابة. فيقع الطلاق حالاً، ولا يتأخر إلى وصول الكتاب وقراءته (۱).
(۱) المراد بالكتاب في هذا المقام الخطاب الذي تكتب فيه عبارة الإيجاب أو القبول موجهة من أحد العاقدين إلى الآخر، بأن يكتب إليه مثلاً: إني اشتريت منك كذا بكذا، فيكتب إليه الآخر قد قبلت أو قد بعتك ما طلبت بما ذكرت من الثمن ونحو ذلك؛ فيكون هذا كما لو تشافها بالإيجاب والقبول نطقاً (ر: الأشباه، ج٢ الفن ۳ ص / ١٩٦ ، وفتح القدير ٤٦١/٥ - ٤٦٢). وليس المراد كتابة الصك الذي يكتب المتعاقدان فيه العقد. ويوقعانه، فإن كتابة الصك ليست مما نحن بصدده، لأن الصك ليس من قبيل إنشاء العقد بالكتابة، بل هو إقرار خطي بوقوع العقد أي إن الصك هو وثيقة مثبتة، وليس عبارة منشئة . (۲) يلحظ هنا أنه في زماننا يكتفي بتصدير الكتابة باسم المرسل إليه فقط توقيعها أو ختمها باسم المرسل في نهايتها، فإنه المعتاد اليوم.
(1) أما إذا كانت الكتابة قد علق فيها الطلاق على بلوغ الكتاب بأن كتب الرجل إلى زوجته إذا وصلك كتابي هذا فأنت طالق، فلا يقع الطلاق عندئذ إلا بعد وصوله، كما في العقود. وانظر في أحكام الكتابة وشرائطها (رد المحتار ٤٢٨/٢ ، والأشباه لابن نجيم مع حاشية الحموي ج ۲، الفن الثالث، ص/١٩٦ - ١٩٧).
Chapter Six: Theory of contracts and related matters in fiqh. 7/30 - First: Writing: Writing between absent persons is like speech between present persons, and on this basis the legal maxim was established: Writing is like speech (al-Majalla / 69). It is required for writing to be considered valid that it be stable and conventional. - Meaning of being stable: that it be written on something on which it appears and remains. So writing in the air or on the surface of water is not valid. - Meaning of being conventional: that it be written in the customary way among people in the customs and traditions of their time, that is, by being headed with the name of the sender and addressee, or sealed or signed by the speaking sender, and that in our time it be on paper, not on bone tablets, animal skins, or tree leaves as was done in the distant past. The fiqh opinion regarding offer and acceptance by writing is that its effect does not begin from the time of writing the expression, but rather upon the arrival of the writing and its reading. Then the expression takes effect, and the offeror is considered an offeror and the acceptor is considered an acceptor. This is in contracts, unlike acts that do not depend on the knowledge and consent of the other party, such as divorce and manumission. For if they are written in writing that fulfills the conditions, i.e., stable and conventional, such as if one writes to his wife a letter saying to her: 'You are divorced,' then they take effect immediately upon writing. The divorce occurs immediately and is not delayed until the arrival of the writing and its reading. (1) What is meant by 'kitab' in this context is the letter in which the expression of offer or acceptance is written addressed from one of the contracting parties to the other, such as if he writes to him: 'I have bought from you such-and-such for such-and-such,' then the other writes back: 'I have accepted' or 'I have sold you what you requested for the price mentioned,' etc.; so this is as if they exchanged offer and acceptance orally (see al-Ashbah, vol. 2, section 3, p. 196, and Fath al-Qadir 5/461-462). What is not meant is the writing of the instrument (sakk) in which the two contracting parties write the contract and sign it, for the writing of the instrument is not what we are dealing with, because the instrument is not a type of creating the contract by writing, but rather it is a written acknowledgment of the occurrence of the contract; i.e., the instrument is a confirming document, not a constitutive expression. (2) It is noted here that in our time, it suffices to head the writing with the name of the addressee only and to sign or seal it with the name of the sender at its end, as that is the current custom. (1) As for if the divorce is conditioned in the writing upon receipt of the writing, such as if a man writes to his wife: 'If this my letter reaches you, you are divorced,' then the divorce does not occur except after its arrival, as in contracts. See rulings on writing and its conditions (Radd al-Muhtar 2/428, al-Ashbah by Ibn Nujaym with Hashiyat al-Hamawi, vol. 2, section 3, pp. 196-197).
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal rule that writing between absent parties is equivalent to speech between present parties. The rule is sourced from the Majalla (article 69). The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains two conditions for valid writing: it must be stable (مستبينة), meaning written on a surface that retains it, and it must be conventional (مرسومة), meaning written in the customary manner of the time, including addressing, signing, or stamping. The legal effect of a written offer and acceptance in contracts begins not at the time of writing but upon receipt and reading. However, for unilateral acts like divorce and manumission that do not require the knowledge or consent of the other party, the effect is immediate upon writing. An exception is if the divorce is made conditional on receipt of the writing, in which case it only takes effect upon receipt, similar to contracts. The footnote clarifies that 'kitab' here refers to the written message containing the offer or acceptance, not a formal contract document (sakk) which is merely evidence.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-kitab): الخطاب الذي تكتب فيه عبارة الإيجاب أو القبول موجهة من أحد العاقدين إلى الآخر
مستبينة (mustabinah): أن تكون مكتوبة على شيء تظهر وتثبت عليه
مرسومة (marsumah): أن تكون مكتوبة بالطريقة المعتادة بين الناس في مراسم زمانهم وتقاليده

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule 'الكتاب كالخطاب', when does a written contract (إيجاب وقبول) take effect?
A) At the moment of writing
B) Upon receipt and reading of the written communication
C) When the writer signs
D) Only if witnessed

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'الكتاب كالخطاب' applies with conditions of stability and conventionality, and its effect depends on the nature of the act: receipt for contracts, immediate for unilateral, condition fulfillment for conditional.

The Gesture of the Mute as a Substitute for Speech
Explains the rule that a mute's gesture replaces speech under conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-isharat al-ma'hudah lil-akhras ka al-bayan bil-lisan' to determine the validity of a mute's legal acts, and identify when the exception (if the mute can write) applies.

Core Idea
The accustomed gesture of a mute is like speech, but only if the mute cannot write.

This lesson presents a key maxim from the Majalla (Article 70) used in Hanafi fatwa for mute persons. We will examine the conditions under which a mute's gesture is accepted as a valid expression of intent, and the critical exception when the mute is literate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
State the Arabic text of the maxim and its English translation: الإشارة المعهودة للأخرس كالبيان باللسان — 'The accustomed gesture of the mute is like verbal expression.'
This is a sughra (minor) maxim governing a specific scenario.
2
Determine the scope
Identify which acts are covered: all verbal transactional acts - contracts (sale, marriage), transfers (will, endowment), relinquishments (divorce, release), and others (claims, acknowledgment, oaths).
The maxim applies to any oral legal act where speech normally would be required.
3
Identify the condition
The maxim applies only if the mute cannot write and has an accustomed, understood gesture. If the mute can write, the maxim does not apply.
This is the dabt (restrictive sub-maxim): if the mute is literate, gesture is not accepted; writing must be used.
4
Apply the exception (dabt)
State the ruling for a literate mute: his gestures are invalid; his acts must be expressed in writing because writing is equivalent to speech in clarity.
The basis is that gesture is inferior to writing in indication.
5
Handle temporary muteness
If the muteness is temporary (tongue-tied), and the person cannot write but has habitual gestures, his acts are suspended until death. If he dies in that state, they become effective retroactively.
This preserves legal validity while awaiting resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨/٣٠ - ثانياً : الإشارة :
وأما إشارة الأخرس فإنها تقوم مقام النطق باللسان باتفاق الفقهاء، إذا كان لا يعرف الكتابة في جميع تصرفاته التعاملية القولية التي يكون له فيها إشارة معهودة مفهومة جرت عليها عادته سواء منها ما كان عقداً كالبيع والشراء والنكاح، أو كان إنشاء منفرداً في التمليكات كالوصية لجهة خيرية والوقف، أو في الإسقاطات كالطلاق والإبراء وإسقاط الشفعة، أو في غير ذلك كالدعوى والإقرار وحلف اليمين.
وعلى هذا الأساس وضعت القاعدة القائلة :
الإشارة المعهودة للأخرس كالبيان باللسان (م (۷۰) .
أما إذا كان الأخرس كاتباً فالرأي الراجح فقهاً أنه لا تصح تصرفاته إلا بالكتابة لأنها في الدلالة كالنطق من كل وجه. أما الإشارة فهي دون الكتابة دلالة، فلا تقبل مع إمكان الكتابة.
وإذا كان الخرس غير أصلي بل طارئاً، وهي حالة معتقل اللسان فالرأي المرجّح عند فقهائنا أنه إذا كان لا يعرف الكتابة وتكونت له إشارات معهودة اعتادها للإفصاح عن مراده تقبل إشارته هذه في تصرفاته إذا دامت عقلة لسانه إلى الموت.
وعلى هذا تكون تصرفاته بإشارته المعهودة موقوفة إلى موته : فإذا مات على عُقلته نفذت تصرفاته مستندة إلى تاريخ صدورها، أي انها يكون لها أثر رجعي.
8/30 - Secondly: Gesture: As for the gesture of the mute, it stands in place of speech by the tongue by agreement of the jurists, if he does not know writing, in all his verbal transactional acts for which he has an accustomed understood gesture that has become his habit, whether they are contracts like sale, purchase, and marriage, or independent creations in transfers like a will for a charitable purpose and endowment, or in relinquishments like divorce, release, and waiver of pre-emption, or in other things like claim, acknowledgment, and taking an oath. On this basis the maxim was laid down: The accustomed gesture of the mute is like verbal expression (M. 70). However, if the mute is literate, the authoritative opinion in fiqh is that his acts are not valid except by writing, because it is like speech in indication from every aspect. As for gesture, it is inferior to writing in indication, so it is not accepted when writing is possible. And if the muteness is not original but temporary, i.e., the state of a tongue-tied person, the preferred opinion among our jurists is that if he does not know writing and has formed accustomed gestures that he habitually uses to express his intent, his gesture is accepted in his acts if his tongue-tiedness continues until death. On this basis, his acts by his accustomed gesture are suspended until his death: so if he dies in his tongue-tied state, his acts are executed retroactively to the date of issuance, i.e., they have retroactive effect.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents the rule that the accustomed gesture of a mute person is like verbal expression (from Majalla m.70). It applies when the mute cannot write. The gesture must be understood and habitual. It is valid for all types of oral transactions: contracts, wills, gifts, divorce, release, etc. If the mute can write, the relied-upon opinion is that his gestures are not accepted because writing is equivalent to speech in clarity; gesture is inferior. This is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim). If the muteness is temporary (e.g., due to a speech impediment) and the person cannot write but has habitual gestures, his gestures are accepted on a provisional basis: they are suspended until death. If he dies in that state, his acts take effect retrospectively. This preserves legal validity while awaiting resolution.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): الشخص الذي لا يستطيع النطق
الإشارة المعهودة (al-isharat al-ma'hudah): الإشارة التي جرت عادة الأخرس عليها وتكون مفهومة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mute person who knows how to write uses gestures to express a sale, according to the relied-upon Hanafi opinion, is the sale valid?
A) Yes, because gesture is like speech
B) No, because writing is possible and takes precedence
C) Yes, but only upon death
D) No, because gestures are never valid

Key Takeaway
The accustomed gesture of a mute who cannot write is equivalent to speech for all oral legal acts, but if the mute is literate, writing must be used.

Contract Formation by Conduct (Ta'ati) in Hanafi Law
Explaining the concept of التعاطي as a valid method of contract formation without verbal expression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'A contract is concluded by mutual taking and giving (Ta'ati)' to identify valid contracts formed without verbal expression, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i restriction.

Core Idea
A contract is valid by mutual giving and taking alone; conduct suffices without words.

In everyday transactions, from buying a loaf of bread to subscribing to a utility service, contracts are often concluded without a formal offer and acceptance. Hanafi jurisprudence recognizes this reality through the concept of التعاطي (Ta'ati), or mutual conduct. This lesson explores the legal maxim that a contract is formed by taking and giving, its scope, and its application to both cheap and expensive items, as well as modern contracts of adhesion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qawida
Read the passage and locate the general rule: contracts can be formed by mutual taking and giving (Ta'ati) without verbal expressions. Understand that this is a recognized method of contract formation in Hanafi law.
2
Understand the Scope
Note that Ta'ati applies to all types of property, cheap or expensive. The passage explicitly states that it covers both base and precious properties. This contrasts with the later Shafi'i view restricting it to cheap goods.
3
Apply to Examples (Furuat)
Work through the examples given in the passage: buying goods with a price tag, paying price and receiving later, 'urbun (earnest money), and modern contracts like utility subscriptions. For each, confirm that the contract is valid by Ta'ati.
The passage says one-sided giving is sufficient (e.g., buyer pays, seller promises delivery).
4
Analyze the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
The footnote mentions that Shafi'i originally disallowed Ta'ati, later allowed for cheap goods. Compare this with the Hanafi position. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: in Hanafi, expensive items are also covered; in Shafi'i, they are not.
Maliki and Hanbali agree with Hanafi.
5
Consider Modern Application (Iftaa)
Recognize that modern contracts of adhesion (عقود الإذعان) are considered a form of Ta'ati according to Hanafi law. The mufta bihi position is that such contracts are valid without verbal offer and acceptance.
This is based on the need and prevalence in contemporary life.

(۱) اجتهاد الشافعية في الأصل أنه لا يسوغ البيع بالتعاطي دون لفظ، ثم جوزه متأخروهم لشيوعه والحاجة إليه في خسيس الأموال أي رخيصها، دون نفيسها. والاجتهادان المالكي والحنبلي كالاجتهاد الحنفي في التعاطي. وقد نقل عن مالك أنه قال: ينعقد البيع بما يعتقده الناس بيعاً». وقال موفق الدين بن قدامة الحنبلي في الاستدلال الشرعي للتعاطي في كتابه «المغني) ما نصه :
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. Among the jurists are those who consider it sufficient to have a continuous loss of speech for a year, after which he becomes like a congenital mute, his customary gesture is accepted, and it has immediate effect. This is more appropriate and more repelling of hardship, for the first opinion involves great hardship, for the life of a tongue-tied person may extend for decades after his loss of speech, so his transactions should not be suspended throughout that time, since a customary gesture has been formed for him. (See Radd al-Muhtar 2/425). And the juristic view on the gesture of a mute and a tongue-tied person will be clarified in our discussion on the aforementioned maxim in the third section of this part. 9/30 - Third: Indication (al-Dilalah): As for indication, it means that the formation (of the contract) is derived from an act that is appropriate to follow from the contract and expresses its execution, or derived from a situation that necessitates its formation. Under the formation of a contract by indication, there are two forms: Ta'ati (taking and giving) and Lisan al-Hal (language of the situation). 10/30 - A: Ta'ati: As for Ta'ati, it is taking and giving, i.e., an actual exchange indicating the exchange of wills and mutual consent without uttering offer or acceptance (see Majalla article 175). For example, if a person finds goods in a shop with their price written on them, or they are of known price, or he asks the seller about its price and he tells him; then he takes it and hands the seller its price, and he accepts it willingly without verbal offer and acceptance, then the sale is concluded between them by this taking and giving, because they take the place of offer and acceptance in indicating the exchange of definitive wills in a commutative contract. For the conclusion of a contract by Ta'ati, it is sufficient that the giving be from one side, i.e., that the receipt occurs on one of the two considerations only in a commutative contract, according to the preponderant juristic opinion. For example, if a buyer asks about the price of something and then pays the price to the owner, and he accepts it and promises to deliver the sold item later, then the sale is concluded, and each is bound even if the price changes afterward (see Majalla article 175, Radd al-Muhtar 11/4). Considered as Ta'ati is the seller's receipt of 'urbun (earnest money) from the buyer without uttering offer and acceptance, so the sale is concluded thereby, and neither may retract afterward, because the 'urbun is considered part of the price. Today, one may consider as a type of Ta'ati the customary method in contracts called in modern legal language 'contracts of adhesion', such as subscription to electricity, water, gas, telephone, and the like. They are concluded by submitting a written application and the relevant institution (company, municipality, or government department) making the necessary installations and delivering the required benefits. Similarly today, a person boarding buses, buying tickets for train travel or cinema entry, and the like, as well as sending a magazine or newspaper to a subscriber. Ta'ati applies to both base and precious properties, i.e., both cheap and expensive. Just as it is permissible in bread, coal, firewood, stones, and the like of cheap trivial items, it also applies to pearls and precious stones of valuable expensive items. So if a person sees a necklace of jewels with a jeweler and understands its price, then hands it over and the jeweler hands over the necklace, the sale is concluded between them without words. (1) The ijtihad of the Shafi'is originally was that sale by Ta'ati without words is not permissible. Then their later scholars permitted it due to its prevalence and need, for base (cheap) properties only, not for precious ones. The Maliki and Hanbali ijtihads are like the Hanafi ijtihad regarding Ta'ati. It is reported from Malik that he said: 'The sale is concluded by whatever people consider a sale.' And Muwaffaq al-Din Ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali said in his legal reasoning for Ta'ati in his book al-Mughni, the text of which is: ...
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa discusses contract formation by conduct, specifically the concept of التعاطي (Ta'ati). The passage first touches on the mute person (مُعْتَقَل اللسان) whose speech is lost for a year, then moves to the main topic: الدلالة (indication) as a means of forming contracts without verbal expression. Two forms are mentioned: التعاطي and لسان الحال. The focus here is on التعاطي, defined as an actual exchange of goods and price that indicates mutual consent, replacing the need for verbal offer and acceptance. The author provides examples: buying from a shop with a price tag, or asking the price and then handing over money. The principle is that the act itself suffices; verbalization is not required. Importantly, the passage states that it is enough for the give-and-take to be from one side (e.g., buyer pays, seller promises delivery) – the contract is still concluded and binding. This is the راجح (preponderant) view. The concept also applies to 'urbun (earnest money): when the seller takes a deposit, the sale becomes binding. The author then brings forward modern applications: subscription contracts for utilities, public transport, tickets, etc. A key point is that Ta'ati applies to both cheap and expensive items – unlike the later Shafi'i view that restricts it to cheap goods. The footnote at the end clarifies madhhab positions: originally Shafi'is required verbal offer and acceptance, but later scholars allowed Ta'ati for cheap items; Malikis and Hanbalis agree with the Hanafi view. This passage illustrates the Hanafi principle that the essence of a contract is mutual consent, and any clear indication of that consent, including conduct, suffices.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'āṭī): An actual exchange of giving and taking that indicates mutual consent in a contract, without verbal offer and acceptance.
الدلالة (al-dilālah): Indication; the formation of a contract inferred from an act that normally follows from the contract or from a situation.
عقد الإذعان (ʻaqd al-idhʻān): Contract of adhesion; a standard-form contract offered by a dominant party on a take-it-or-leave-it basis.
العربون (al-ʻurbūn): Earnest money; a deposit paid to secure a sale, considered part of the price.
لسان الحال (lisān al-ḥāl): Language of the situation; a non-verbal indication arising from circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view as explained in the passage, is it valid to buy a loaf of bread by taking it and handing money to the baker without saying anything?
A) No, a verbal offer and acceptance is required.
B) Yes, this is a valid sale by Ta'ati.
C) Only if the bread is expensive.
D) Only if the baker knows the price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contract is formed by mutual conduct (Ta'ati) without verbal offer and acceptance, and this applies to all goods, cheap or expensive.

The Five Real Contracts in Hanafi Law
Defining the five real contracts in Hanafi law that require delivery of the subject matter for completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five real contracts in Hanafi law and apply the maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' to determine when a gratuitous contract is binding.

Core Idea
A donation is not completed except by possession: five contracts require delivery of the subject matter.

In Hanafi law, most contracts are complete upon offer and acceptance. However, five specific contracts—gift, loan for use, deposit, loan of money, and pledge—require delivery of the object to be fully binding. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this exception and the core maxim that governs it.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
The maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' means that a gratuitous contract (tabarru') is not completed without possession. Identify that it applies to all five contracts listed.
2
List the five real contracts
Memorize the five: hibah (gift), i'arah (loan of use), wadi'ah (deposit), qard (loan of consumables), and rahn (pledge). Note that they are all tabarru' in nature.
Other schools may list different contracts.
3
Explain the legal rationale (illa)
The jurists explain that the condition of qabd is because these contracts are gratuitous. Since there is no exchange, delivery is needed to confirm the donor's intent and prevent disputes.
4
Apply the maxim to each contract
For each contract, state the effect of delivery: e.g., in hibah, the gift is not binding until the recipient takes possession; in rahn, the creditor cannot demand delivery before possession.
Before delivery, the contract is concluded (mun'aqid) but not complete (tāmm).
5
Identify the legal effects before and after delivery
Before delivery: the contract has no legal effect (no obligation to deliver). After delivery: the contract becomes binding and enforceable. Use the example of rahn to illustrate.

Source Text

المطلب الثالث: العقود العينية 13/30 - اتضح مما تقدَّم أنّ العقد في الشرع الإسلامي يتم وتثبت بمجرد الإيجاب والقبول . غير أنّ هناك طائفة من العقود لا تعتبر تامة إلا إذا حصل تسليم العين التي هي موضوع العقد ولا يكفي فيها الإيجاب والقبول وتسمى هذه العقود بلغة الحقوق اليوم العقود العينية أي التي يتوقف فيها تمام الالتزام على تسليم العين، وهي خمسة عقود: الهبة والإعارة، والإيداع، والقرض، والرهن. ويعلل الفقهاء اشتراط القبض لتمامها بأنها تبرع. وعلى هذا الأساس وضعت القاعدة القائلة: «لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض (م/57). فالعقد اللفظي في هذه المواضيع يعتبر قبل القبض عديم الأثر: ففي الرهن مثلاً لا يستطيع الدائن المرتهن إجبار المدين على تسليم (1) سنناقش في ملحق هذا الفصل رأياً للدكتور شفيق شحاتة حول ما سماه «اللفظية» في الفقه الإسلامي.
Third Subsection: Real Contracts 13/30 - It has become clear from what preceded that the contract in Islamic law is completed and established by mere offer and acceptance. However, there is a group of contracts that are not considered complete unless delivery of the specific object (ayn) that is the subject of the contract occurs, and offer and acceptance are not sufficient. These contracts are called today in the language of law 'real contracts', i.e., those in which the full obligation is dependent upon delivery of the object. They are five contracts: gift (hibah), loan of benefit (i'arah), deposit (wadi'ah), loan of money (qard), and pledge (rahn). The jurists explain the stipulation of possession (qabd) for their completion by the fact that they are donations (tabarru'). Upon this basis, the maxim was established: 'A donation is not completed except by possession' (Majallah Article 57). Thus the verbal contract in these matters is considered before possession to have no effect. For example, in a pledge, the creditor (mortgagee) cannot force the debtor to deliver... (1) We will discuss in the appendix to this chapter an opinion of Dr. Shafiq Shahata regarding what he called 'verbality' in Islamic jurisprudence.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a specific category of contracts called 'real contracts' (uqud 'ayniyyah). The author explains that while the general rule is that a contract is concluded by offer and acceptance alone, there are five exceptions: hibah (gift), i'arah (loan of a non-consumable for use), wadi'ah (deposit for safekeeping), qard (loan of consumable items), and rahn (pledge/mortgage). These contracts are considered 'tabarru' (gratuitous/charitable) in nature, and for them, delivery (qabd) of the subject matter is necessary for the contract to be fully binding and effective. The author cites the Hanafi legal maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' (a donation is not completed except by possession). He illustrates with the example of rahn: before the pledge is delivered, the creditor cannot compel the debtor to hand it over. This emphasizes that in these cases, the verbal contract alone creates no obligation to deliver; delivery is a condition for the contract's completion, not an effect of it. This is a key distinction in Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
العقود العينية (al-ʿuqūd al-ʿayniyyah): Real contracts: those in which delivery of the specific object is required for the contract to be complete and binding.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Physical or constructive possession/delivery of the subject matter of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal reason (illa) given by Hanafi jurists for requiring delivery in the five real contracts?
A) They are onerous contracts (mu'awadat)
B) They are gratuitous contracts (tabarru')
C) They require witnessing
D) They involve risk (gharar)

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' governs five real contracts; without delivery, they have no legal effect.

Real Contracts and the Maxim of Delivery for Gratuitous Contracts
Defining real contracts and establishing the maxim of delivery for gratuitous contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' to identify when a gratuitous contract becomes binding and distinguish the exception for bequests.

Core Idea
Gratuitous contracts require delivery to be binding; offer and acceptance alone are insufficient.

We have established that contracts in Islamic law are generally concluded by offer and acceptance. However, there is a special category of contracts known as real contracts, which require physical delivery of the object for full legal effect. This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on these contracts, centered on the maxim 'A gratuitous contract is not completed except by delivery.' We will examine the five contracts that fall under this rule, the rationale behind it, and the exception for bequests, equipping you to correctly assess the binding nature of such transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of contracts
Recognize that the passage distinguishes between contracts concluded by mere offer and acceptance and those requiring delivery. The latter are called 'real contracts'.
Real contracts are a minority; most contracts are 'consensual' in Hanafi law.
2
State the maxim and its basis
The maxim is 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض'. Understand that the justification is that these contracts are gratuitous (tabarru'), so the law gives the donor the chance to reconsider before delivery.
The maxim is codified in Article 57 of the Majalla.
3
List the five real contracts
Memorize the five contracts: hiba (gift), i'ara (loan for use), wadi'a (deposit), qard (loan for consumption), and rahn (pledge).
Other schools may include different contracts; this list is specific to the Hanafi school.
4
Work through the furuat
For each of the five contracts, apply the maxim: before delivery, the beneficiary has no right to demand the object; after delivery, obligations become enforceable.
The passage gives explicit examples for pledge and gift; we extend to all five.
5
Identify the istithna (exception)
Bequest (wasiyya) is exempt: it becomes binding upon death and acceptance without delivery because delivery is impossible from the deceased.
This exception is based on leniency to facilitate charitable acts.

Chapter (30): The Form of the Contract and What Pertains to It. However, the utterance in contracts is not intended for its own sake formalistically; rather, it is the best expression of will in a clear manner. And when jurists examine a contract and mention the utterances suitable for its creation, they often state that these utterances are mentioned only by way of example, and that the contracting parties may use other expressions that convey the intended meaning. Even if the agreement of two wills emerges through a means other than utterance, the contract is concluded by implication, as in mu'āṭāh (mutual giving) and the language of the situation, as previously indicated (1). Its rulings. Third Topic: Real Contracts. 13/30 - It has become clear from what preceded that the contract in Islamic law is concluded and established by mere offer and acceptance. However, there is a group of contracts that are not considered complete unless delivery of the specific object that is the subject of the contract takes place; offer and acceptance alone are insufficient. In modern legal language, these are called real contracts, i.e., those in which the full obligation depends on delivery of the object. They are five contracts: gift (hiba), loan for use (i'āra), deposit (wadī'a), loan for consumption (qarḍ), and pledge (rahn). The jurists justify the condition of possesion (qabḍ) for their completion by the fact that they are gratuitous (tabarru'). Upon this basis, the maxim was formulated: 'A gratuitous contract is not completed except by delivery' (Majalla Art. 57). Thus, the verbal contract in these matters is considered devoid of effect before delivery. For example, in pledge, the creditor-pledgee cannot force the debtor to deliver the pledge by virtue of the contract if he does not surrender it voluntarily. But if he delivers it, the obligation is fulfilled; if the pledgor then reclaims it without the consent of the pledgee, the latter may compel him to return it. Similarly, the donee cannot force the donor to deliver the gift. Execution in these real contracts is what generates the effects of the contract; the will is not considered manifested by utterance alone without this execution. However, there is an exception to this theory of real contracts: the contract of bequest (waṣiyya). It is suspended until after death. Upon the death of the testator and the acceptance of the legatee or his non-rejection after death, the bequest is complete and the property becomes his ownership without need for delivery, because delivery from the person creating the bequest is no longer conceivable after death. The law of bequest is based on leniency and as an exception from the analogical rules in many of its provisions, to facilitate acts of piety and charity.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the concept of 'real contracts' (العقود العينية) in Hanafi law. It begins by clarifying that verbal expression in contracts is not an end in itself; rather, it serves as the clearest indicator of intention. Jurists often give example wordings for contracts but allow contracting parties to use any expression that conveys the intended meaning. Even without words, a contract can be concluded by implication, such as mutual giving (mu'āṭāh) or the language of the situation. The passage then defines real contracts: contracts that are not completed merely by offer and acceptance but also require physical delivery (qabḍ) of the subject matter. Five contracts fall under this category: gift (hiba), loan for use (i'āra), deposit (wadī'a), loan for consumption (qarḍ), and pledge (rahn). The reason for requiring delivery is that these are gratuitous contracts (tabarru'), meaning they involve a transfer without consideration. The key maxim is 'A gratuitous contract is not completed except by delivery' (م/٥٧). Before delivery, the contract has no legal effect. For example, in a pledge, the creditor cannot force the debtor to deliver the pledge; if delivered, the debtor cannot reclaim it without the creditor's consent. Similarly, a donee cannot compel the donor to hand over the gift. The passage emphasizes that execution (delivery) is what generates the legal effects, not mere verbal agreement. An exception is made for bequests (waṣiyya): because the testator dies before delivery, the bequest becomes effective upon death and acceptance, without need for delivery. This exception is based on leniency and to facilitate charitable acts. This maxim is central to understanding Hanafi contract law, distinguishing between commutative contracts (which are binding immediately) and gratuitous contracts (which require delivery).

Key Terms
عقد عيني ('aqd 'ayniyy): A contract that requires delivery of the specific object for its completion; offer and acceptance alone are insufficient.
تبرع (tabarru'): A gratuitous act or contract involving transfer of property without compensation.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical delivery or taking possession of the subject matter of a contract.
وصية (waṣiyya): A bequest; a disposition of property to take effect after the testator's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض', which of the following contracts is NOT completed merely by offer and acceptance?
A) Sale (بيع)
B) Gift (هبة)
C) Lease (إجارة)
D) Marriage (نكاح)

Key Takeaway
Gratuitous contracts (hiba, i'ara, wadi'a, qard, rahn) do not become binding until the subject matter is delivered; bequest is a special exception.

Third Condition: Contract Not Prohibited Under Nullity
Explaining the third general condition for contract formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'every contract with no one to ratify is void' to identify when a contract is batil and state the mufta bihi ruling for a minor's disposition.

Core Idea
A contract prohibited under pain of nullity is void ab initio and cannot be ratified; only a new contract suffices.

In the previous sections, we examined conditions related to the subject matter and parties. Now we turn to the third condition: that the contract not be prohibited by a legal text under threat of nullity. This condition is rooted in the Hanafi legal maxim that a contract without any potential ratifier at the time of issuance is void. Understanding this maxim and its exceptions is crucial for any mufti dealing with contracts involving minors, fraud, or unauthorized agents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition source
Determine whether the contract is prohibited by a text (nass) explicitly or by inference, under threat of batil. Examples include contracts with minor's property, gross fraud, or prohibited methods.
The prohibition must be under pain of nullity, not mere dislike.
2
Check for legal authority (wilayah shar'iyyah)
Is there any person who has the right to ratify or execute the contract at the time of issuance? If not, the contract is batil.
Legal authority means someone can conclude the contract even if not the actual party.
3
Distinguish from personal authority (wilayah shakhsiyyah)
If the contracting party lacks personal authority (e.g., fuduli) but someone else can ratify later, the contract is suspended, not void.
Absence of personal authority does not cause voidness; it causes suspension.
4
Apply the qawida
Use the maxim to rule on scenarios: gift from minor's property, sale with gross fraud. Note that batil cannot be ratified; a new contract is required.
Remember the istithna' for fuduli contracts: they are suspended, not void.

Source Text

الفصل (٣١) الشرائط العامة لانعقاد العقود
و كذلك لا ينعقد رهن الأموال التي يتسارع إليها الفساد كالخضروات الطازجة، والجمد (أي الجليد، لأنَّ حكم الرهن احتباس المرهون لأجل استيفاء الدين منه عند عدم الأداء في ميعاده، وهذه الأموال لا تقبل الحفظ والاحتباس مع بقاء منافعها .
٤/٣١ - ثالثاً : أن لا يكون العقد ممنوعاً بمقتضى نص شرعي تحت طائلة البطلان :
وذلك لأن العقد الممنوع شرعاً تحت طائلة البطلان يعتبر في نظر الفقهاء غير مشروع من أصله منافياً للنظام الشرعي العام.
فلا تنعقد الهبة من مال الصغير القاصر، ولا بيعه بغين فاحش، سواء أكان الواهب والبائع هو الصغير أو وليه أو وصيه أو القاضي نفسه بمقتضى سلطته العامة عند عدم الولي الخاص.
وذلك لأنّ التبرع من مال الصغير لا يملكه أحد، والبيع بغبن فاحش كالتبرع . فكان هذا العقد مما ليس لأحد سلطة عقده بحسب موضوعه، فيصدر باطلاً حتى لو بلغ الصغير فأجازه لا تعتبر إجازته، لأنَّ الباطل لا يجاز (كما سنرى في بحث البطلان من نظرية المؤيدات)، بل عليه أن يجدد عقده بعد بلوغه إن شاء. والقاعدة الفقهية في ذلك : إن كل عقد يصدر، ولا يوجد من يملك حق إجازته وإنفاذه عند صدوره يكون باطلاً (البدائع ١٤٩/٥ - ١٥٠).
والفقهاء يعبرون في تعليل بطلان هذا النوع من العقود بأن الولاية الشرعية منتفية فيه. ويقصدون بالولاية الشرعية في هذا المقام أن يكون هناك من يملك شرعاً سلطة إبرام هذا العقد ولو كان غير الشخص الذي تولى عقده فعلاً. فإذا لم تكن هذه الولاية بهذا المعنى متوافرة كان العقد باطلاً غير منعقد.
وهذا بخلاف الولاية الشخصية وهي أن يكون شخص العاقد ذا سلطة شرعية، بأن يكون مالكاً أو نائباً عن المالك كالوكيل والوصي، فإن هذه الولاية الشخصية ليست شريطة انعقاد بل ينعقد عقد الفضولي عن غيره، ولكن يكون نفاذه متوقفاً على إجازة صاحب حق التصرف الشرعي.
ويلحظ في هذا المقام أنَّ العقد الممنوع شرعاً تحت طائلة البطلان قد يعود سبب المنع فيه إلى طريقة العقد كبيع الملامسة، والمنابذة، وإلقاء الحجر، مما منعه النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في الماضي (ر : ف ٦/٢٧) ؛ أو يعود إلى محل العقد كبيع المخدرات للاستعمال المحرم، وكالاستئجار على فعل المعاصي أو ارتكاب الجرائم؛ أو يعود إلى موضوع العقد كالتبرع من مال القاصر. فكل هذه العقود باطلة مهما كان سبب المنع الشرعي فيها بعد أن يكون المنع تحت طائلة البطلان صراحة في النص أو استنتاجاً أو قياساً . وقد يعود البطلان لسبب ذي صلة بالمحل المعقود عليه أو بالعاقد، كبيع صيد الحرم وبيع صيد الشخص المحرم بحج أو عمرة ولو (١)
(١) وقد كان مثل ذلك لدينا في الأحكام القانونية، منذ العهد العثماني، العقود المنشئة للحقوق العينية على العقار من بيع أو رهن وغيرهما. فقد أوجب القانون العثماني إجراء جميع هذه العقود العقارية في دائرة المكتب العقاري وتسجيلها في سجله الرسمي تحت طائلة البطلان فلا تعتبر العقود العقارية إذا عقدت خارج السجل العقاري ولو كتب بها صك، ولا يلزم الناكل عن تنفيذها وتسجيلها لبطلانها .. وعلى هذا درج الاجتهاد القضائي لدينا في فهم نصوص قانون الملكية العقارية ذي الرقم / ٣٣٣٩ الصادر في عهد الانتداب الفرنسي سنة / ١٩٣٠م. غير أن الهيئة الجديدة لمحكمة التمييز السورية (محكمة النقض) قد عدلت أخيراً عن الاجتهاد السابق في فهم نصوص هذا القانون الأخير فقررت أن جميع هذه العقود العقارية تعتبر صحيحة في ذاتها ولو عقدت سراً، ويصح الادعاء بها وتلزم عاقدها قضاء بالتسجيل، ولكن لا تنتقل الملكية بهذا العقد بين العاقدين، ولا يسري مفعول العقد علي غيرهما إلا من تاريخ التسجيل. ر: القرار التمييزي الصادر في ١٨ ربيع الأول/ ١٣٦٨هـ = ١٧ كانون الثاني / ١٩٤٩م، تحت رقم // أساس حقوق / ٤٦). وهذا ما أخذ به وقرره أيضاً القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر لدينا في سنة / ١٩٤٩م خلال الطبعة الثالثة من هذا المدخل الفقهي.
Chapter (31): General Conditions for Contract Formation
Likewise, a pledge (rahn) of goods that quickly spoil, such as fresh vegetables and ice, is not valid, because the ruling of pledge is the retention of the pledged item for the purpose of recovering the debt from it upon non-payment at its due date, and these goods do not accept preservation and retention while their benefits remain.
4/31 - Third: That the contract not be prohibited by a legal text under the threat of nullity.
This is because a contract prohibited by law under threat of nullity is considered by the jurists as fundamentally illegitimate, contrary to the general legal order.
Thus, a gift from the property of a minor who is under guardianship is not valid, nor is his sale with gross fraud, whether the donor or seller is the minor himself, his guardian, his executor, or even the judge himself by virtue of his general authority in the absence of a specific guardian.
This is because donating from a minor's property is not owned by anyone, and a sale with gross fraud is like a donation. Thus, this contract is one that no one has the authority to conclude based on its subject matter, so it issues as void, and even if the minor reaches majority and ratifies it, his ratification is not considered, because the void cannot be ratified (as we will see in the discussion of nullity in the theory of confirmations). Rather, he must renew his contract after reaching majority if he wishes. The legal maxim in this regard is: Every contract that issues, and there is no one who has the right to ratify and execute it at the time of its issuance, is void (al-Bada'i' 5/149-150).
The jurists express the cause of nullity of this type of contract as the absence of legal authority (wilayah shar'iyyah) therein. They mean by legal authority in this context that there exists someone who legally possesses the power to conclude this contract, even if it is not the person who actually undertook the contract. So if this authority in this sense is not present, the contract is void and not formed.
This is contrary to personal authority (wilayah shakhsiyyah), which is that the contracting person himself possesses legal authority, such as being an owner or a representative of the owner like an agent or executor. For personal authority is not a condition for formation; rather, the contract of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) on behalf of another is formed, but its enforcement is contingent upon the ratification of the person who holds the right of legal disposition.
It is observed in this regard that the contract prohibited by law under threat of nullity may be due to the method of the contract, such as the sale of mulamasah, munabadhah, and throwing a stone, which the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited in the past; or it may be due to the object of the contract, such as selling narcotics for prohibited use, and hiring for committing sins or crimes; or it may be due to the subject matter of the contract, such as donating from a minor's property. All these contracts are void regardless of the cause of the legal prohibition, provided that the prohibition is under threat of nullity explicitly in the text, by inference, or by analogy. And nullity may be due to a cause related to the object of the contract or the contracting party, such as selling game from the Haram and selling game by a person in ihram for Hajj or Umrah, even if...
(1) There was something similar in our legal provisions since the Ottoman era: the contracts creating real rights over immovable property, such as sale, pledge, etc. The Ottoman law required all such real estate contracts to be conducted at the Land Registry Office and registered in its official record under threat of nullity, so real estate contracts are not considered if concluded outside the land registry even if a deed is written, and the reluctant party is not compelled to execute and register them due to their nullity. On this basis, our judicial ijtihad proceeded in understanding the texts of the Real Property Law No. 3339 issued during the French Mandate in 1930. However, the new body of the Syrian Court of Cassation (Court of Cessation) recently modified the previous ijtihad in understanding the texts of this latter law, ruling that all these real estate contracts are valid in themselves even if concluded secretly, and they may be claimed and the contracting party is judicially compelled to register, but ownership does not transfer by this contract between the contracting parties, nor does the effect of the contract affect others except from the date of registration. See the cassation decision issued on 18 Rabi' al-Awwal 1368 AH = 17 January 1949, under file number: /46/ (Basic Rights). And this is what was adopted and decided by the Syrian Civil Code issued in our country in 1949 during the third edition of this al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis of the third general condition for the valid formation of contracts: that the contract must not be prohibited by a legal text under penalty of nullity. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins with an example from the previous condition about perishable goods, then moves to the core discussion. He explains that a contract prohibited under threat of nullity is fundamentally illegitimate and contrary to the general legal order. Key examples include: a gift from a minor's property, or a sale by a minor with gross fraud (ghubn fahish). Such contracts are void ab initio, and even if the minor later reaches majority and ratifies, the ratification is ineffective; the contract must be renewed. The author cites the legal maxim from al-Bada'i': any contract that no one has the authority to ratify at the time of issuance is void. The cause is absence of legal authority (wilayah shar'iyyah), meaning there is no entity legally empowered to conclude that contract at that time. He distinguishes this from personal authority (wilayah shakhsiyyah): a contract by a fuduli (unauthorized agent) is formed but subject to ratification by the owner. The prohibition can arise from the method (e.g., prohibited sales like mulamasah), the object (e.g., narcotics for prohibited use), or the subject matter (e.g., donation from a minor's property). In all cases, if the prohibition is under threat of nullity, the contract is void. The long footnote discusses parallel rules in Ottoman and Syrian positive law regarding registration of real estate contracts, showing a historical development from strict nullity to a regime where unregistered contracts are valid but require registration for transfer of ownership. This is a modern example of how fiqh principles interact with codified law.

Key Terms
عقد (‘aqd): Legal transaction requiring offer and acceptance, subject to conditions of formation.
باطل (bāṭil): Void ab initio; produces no legal effects and cannot be ratified.
ولاية شرعية (wilāyah shar‘iyyah): Legal authority to conclude a contract, even if not possessed by the actual contracting party at the time.
ولاية شخصية (wilāyah shakhṣiyyah): Personal authority of the contracting party, e.g., being an owner or authorized representative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A contract prohibited under pain of nullity is void ab initio and cannot be ratified; only a new contract suffices.

Special Conditions and Benefit Requirement
Enumerating two general conditions: meeting special contractual conditions and the contract being beneficial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that a contract must be beneficial (mufīd) to determine the validity of contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A contract must serve a Shariah-recognized purpose; otherwise it is void.

In this lesson, we examine two crucial conditions for the formation of contracts in Hanafi law. First, each contract type has its own special conditions (like witnesses for marriage or delivery for real contracts). Second, every contract must be beneficial (mufīd), meaning it must produce a legitimate legal effect. Understanding these conditions helps distinguish valid from invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the two conditions
Identify the fourth and fifth conditions for contract formation from the passage: (1) fulfilling special conditions of formation, (2) the contract being beneficial.
These are general conditions applicable to all contracts.
2
Explain the fourth condition with examples
Each contract has its own special conditions (shurūṭ khāṣṣah). For instance, marriage requires witnesses; real contracts (ʿaqd ʿaynī) require delivery. Understand why these are contract-specific.
General conditions (like capacity) also apply, but special conditions are additional.
3
Explain the fifth condition (beneficial)
The contract must produce a recognized legal benefit (mufīd). It is not about profitability but about achieving a legitimate Shariah objective, such as securing a debt or transferring ownership.
If a contract cannot achieve its intended purpose, it is invalid.
4
Apply the benefit condition to the example
Analyze the example: a depositor takes a pledge from a depositary to secure a deposit. Since the deposit is a trust (amānah) and the depositary is not liable unless negligent, the pledge serves no purpose. Hence the pledge is not formed.
The pledge is not beneficial; it cannot secure a non-guaranteed trust.
5
Identify any exceptions
No exceptions are mentioned in this passage. The condition of benefit is a general principle with limited istithnāʾāt elsewhere (e.g., some gratuitous contracts). Here, the ruling is straightforward.
In other texts, exceptions like gifts before delivery may apply, but not relevant here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣١/ ٥ - رابعاً : أن يستوفي العقد شرائط انعقاده الخاصة به: بيَّنا أول هذا الفصل أنّ شرائط الانعقاد نوعان: عامة وخاصة (ر).(۱/۳۰ فيشترط بوجه عام لانعقاد أي عقد من العقود أن تتوافر فيه شرائط انعقاده الخاصة التي يتطلب الشرع توافرها فيه إن كان له شرائط خاصة به مطلوبة شرعاً فيه دون غيره من العقود. وذلك كالشهود مثلاً في عقد النكاح فإن الإشهاد شريطة فيه دون غيره. فلا ينعقد النكاح إلا إذا توافرت فيه - إلى جانب بقية الشرائط العامة - هذه الشريطة الخاصة به. وكذلك العقد العيني مثلاً، فإنه لا يتم انعقاده إلا إذا حصل علاوة على الإيجاب والقبول، تسليم الشيء محل العقد. وهلم جراً. ٦/٣١ - خامساً: كون العقد مفيداً : فلا ينعقد الرهن مثلاً في مقابل الأمانة كما لو أخذ المودع رهناً على الوديع لقاء الوديعة لأن الوديعة أمانة غير مضمونة على الوديع فإذا هلكت بلا تعد ولا تقصير منه في حفظها لا يحق لصاحبها المودع أن يستوفي قيمتها من الرهن فكان الرهن غير مفيد .(رد المحتار في الرهن ٣١٧/٥ وفي الوقف ٣/ ٣٦٧).
31/5 - Fourth: That the contract fulfills its own special conditions of formation. We stated at the beginning of this chapter that conditions of formation are of two types: general and special. So it is generally required for the formation of any contract that its special conditions be present, which the Law requires for it if it has special conditions demanded by the Law for it and not for other contracts. Such as witnesses for the marriage contract; for witnessing is a condition for it and not for others. The marriage contract is not formed unless, along with the rest of the general conditions, this special condition is present. Similarly, a real contract (contract in rem) is also not formed unless, in addition to offer and acceptance, delivery of the object of the contract takes place. And so on. 31/6 - Fifth: That the contract be beneficial. For example, a pledge (rahn) is not formed in exchange for a trust (amanah), as when a depositor takes a pledge from the depositary for the deposit, because the deposit is a trust not guaranteed by the depositary; if it perishes without his transgression or negligence in preserving it, the depositor is not entitled to recover its value from the pledge, so the pledge is not beneficial. (Radd al-Muhtar on Rahn 5/317, and on Waqf 3/367).
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents two general conditions for contract formation in Hanafi fiqh. The fourth condition: the contract must meet its own special conditions (shurūṭ al-in‘iqād al-khāṣṣah). For example, marriage requires witnesses; a real contract (e.g., a loan of fungibles or a gift of specific property in some cases) requires delivery. The fifth condition: the contract must be beneficial (mufīd). A contract that confers no legal benefit is invalid. The author gives the example of a pledge (rahn) taken to secure a deposit (wadī‘ah). Since the depositary is not liable for the deposit unless negligent, the pledge would serve no purpose; hence the pledge contract is not valid. This is from the Hanafi reliance (Radd al-Muhtar).

Key Terms
عقد عيني (‘aqd ‘aynī): A real contract that requires delivery of the object for completion, e.g., gift (hiba) before delivery.
مفيد (mufīd): Beneficial; the contract must produce a legal effect that is recognized in Shariah, such as securing a debt or transferring ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the depositary cannot be trusted
B) Because the deposit is a trust not guaranteed by the depositary, making the pledge useless
C) Because pledges are only allowed for loans
D) Because the pledge contract lacks witnesses

Key Takeaway
For a contract to be valid in Hanafi fiqh, it must fulfill its own special conditions and produce a recognized legal benefit.

Unity of the Contract Session: The Majlis al-'Aqd
Definition of the contract session and its legal effect in Hanafi sales law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-majlis yajma' al-mufarraqat' to determine when an offer lapses and when acceptance is valid within a contract session.

Core Idea
The session unites disparate times — the offer remains valid until the session ends, even if acceptance is delayed.

This lesson explores the seventh condition for contract validity in Hanafi law: the unity of the contract session (majlis al-'aqd). The session is treated as a temporal unit, allowing the acceptor time to deliberate without rushing. We will examine how this condition works, how it ends, and how it differs from the Shafi'i requirement of immediate acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Define majlis al-'aqd
Understand that the contract session is the temporal context of negotiation; it begins with the offer and continues as long as the parties are engaged.
The session is not necessarily physical; it can be via correspondence.
2
Apply the qawida
Use the Hanafi maxim 'al-majlis yajma' al-mufarraqat' (the session unites disparate times) to see that acceptance can be delayed within the session.
3
Identify the three ways the session ends
List the endings: (1) contract concluded, (2) parties separate, (3) one party shows disinterest (e.g., stands up, changes topic).
If the session ends before acceptance, the offer becomes void.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Compare the Hanafi flexibility with the Shafi'i requirement of immediate acceptance, noting the compensatory khiyar al-majlis in Shafi'i.
This difference has practical effects on contract formation.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
In Hanafi, the offeror can revoke during the session; in Shafi'i, acceptance must be immediate but both parties can rescind after the contract during the session.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi is the position presented here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سابعاً : اتحاد مجلس العقد : مجلس العقد هو الحال التي يكون فيها المتعاقدان مقبلين على التفاوض في العقد، فيلغو الإيجاب إذ انفض المجلس قبل القبول، ولا ينعقد العقد بالقبول بعد ذلك، بل يعتبر هذا القبول إيجاباً جديداً يبدأ به مجلس جديد. ونظرية مجلس العقد هذه تحدّد أجلاً للقبول يكون للقابل فيه التروّي، فيقبل في خلاله دون اضطرار إلى القبول فور الإيجاب. وتعليل ذلك أنَّ القبول في الأصل من الوجهة النظرية، يتصل بالإيجاب مباشرة وفوراً لينعقد العقد (۱) . حق ولكن لصعوبة ذلك جعل فقهاؤنا للعقد مجلساً تعتبر ساعاته وحدة زمانية. وعن هذا قال الفقهاء : إنَّ المجلس يجمع المتفرقات (ر: البدائع ١٣٧/٥، والهداية أول البيوع ٤٦٠/٥). ويحدد مجلس العقد بفترة تبدأ منذ الإيجاب، وتنتهي إما بالإتفاق وانعقاد العقد، وإما بافتراق الطرفين أو بإعراض أحدهما، كما لو قام معرضاً أو اشتغل بشأن آخر . (1) وهذا ما ذهب إليه الاجتهاد الشافعي، فاشترط فورية القبول بعد الإيجاب ليتحقق الارتباط الشرعي. وفي هذا ضيق وحرج، ولكنه يقابله أن الاجتهاد الشافعي قد أخذ بمبدأ خيار المجلس لكلا العاقدين بعد العقد في البيع وأشباهه من العقود اللازمة القابلة للفسخ فيثبت لكل من الطرفين حق فسخ العقد ما داما في اجتماعهما لم يفترقا عن المجلس كما سيأتي (ر: ف ٤٠/٢).
Seventh: Unity of the contract session. The contract session is the situation in which the contracting parties are engaged in negotiating the contract. So the offer is invalidated if the session breaks up before acceptance, and the contract is not concluded by acceptance after that; rather, this acceptance is considered a new offer that begins a new session. This theory of the contract session sets a time limit for acceptance in which the acceptor can deliberate, accepting during it without being forced to accept immediately after the offer. The justification is that acceptance, in principle from a theoretical perspective, should connect with the offer directly and immediately for the contract to be concluded. (1) But due to the difficulty of that, our jurists made for the contract a session whose hours are considered a temporal unit. Hence the jurists said: the session unites disparate things (see: al-Bada'i', 5/137; al-Hidaya, beginning of Buyu', 5/460). The contract session is determined as a period starting from the offer and ending either with agreement and conclusion of the contract, or with the parties separating, or with one of them turning away, such as if he stands up showing disinterest or occupies himself with another matter. (1) This is what the Shafi'i ijtihad held; they required immediacy of acceptance after the offer to achieve the legal connection. In this is narrowness and hardship, but it is counterbalanced by the fact that the Shafi'i ijtihad adopted the principle of the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis) for both contracting parties after the contract in sale and similar binding contracts that are subject to annulment, so each party has the right to rescind the contract as long as they are together and have not separated from the session, as will come (see: § 40/2).
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh condition for contract validity: the unity of the contract session (majlis al-'aqd). In Hanafi law, the offer and acceptance must occur within a single continuous session. If the session breaks (e.g., the parties separate or one turns away), the offer becomes void and a new offer must be made. The session is considered a temporal unit; as long as the parties remain in the negotiation context, the offer stays open. This allows the acceptor time to deliberate. The author contrasts this with the Shafi'i view, which requires immediate acceptance to connect with the offer. However, the Shafi'i school compensates by granting both parties the right of khiyar al-majlis (option to rescind) during the session after the contract. The Hanafi view avoids this rigidity by extending the offer's validity throughout the session. The session ends by: (1) conclusion of the contract, (2) separation of the parties, or (3) one party demonstrating disinterest (e.g., standing up or engaging in other business). This condition is crucial for contracts between absent parties via correspondence; each letter constitutes a separate session.

Key Terms
مجلس العقد (majlis al-'aqd): The temporal context of negotiation; a single session from offer to acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, when does the contract session end?
A) Only when the contract is concluded.
B) When the parties physically separate or one shows disinterest.
C) After a fixed period of time.
D) Only when the offeror revokes.

Key Takeaway
Unity of the contract session is a Hanafi condition that keeps the offer alive until the session ends by conclusion, separation, or disinterest, allowing deliberation.
