The Rule of I'rad: Both Parties Equally Terminate the Session
Rule with exceptions for contract session termination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that turning away (i'rad) by either the offeror or offeree terminates the contract session and apply the exceptions for will, agency, and endowment.

Core Idea
Turning away by either party ends the contract session; special rules apply for wills, agency, and endowment.

In Hanafi contract law, the formation of a contract requires continuity of the session from offer to acceptance. The rule is clear: if either party turns away from negotiation, the session ends and the offer becomes void. This lesson unpacks that rule and its important exceptions in wills, agency, and endowment, where the session is defined differently to accommodate absent parties.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida (legal maxim)
Read the rule: 'There is no difference between the turning away of the offeror and the offeree in terminating the session.' Understand that i'rad by either party voids the offer.
The offer alone is not binding; the offeror can revoke before acceptance.
2
Apply the rule to a standard case: letter to an absent person
When an offer is made by letter, the session begins when the absent person reads the letter. If he turns away before accepting, the offer is void. If he accepts in his session, the contract is concluded.
This illustrates that the session is tied to the state of negotiation, not physical presence.
3
Identify the first exception: will (wasiyyah)
The legatee's acceptance or rejection is only valid after the testator's death. Silence after death suffices as acceptance. If the legatee dies before accepting or rejecting, the bequeathed property passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan.
The will is suspended on death; the legatee's right arises posthumously.
4
Identify the second exception: agency (wakalah) and endowment (waqf)
When the agent or beneficiary is absent, the session is defined as the session of knowledge. If they do not reject upon learning of the agency or endowment, it becomes binding.
Knowledge substitutes for physical presence in these contexts.
5
Note the basis of the exceptions: istihsan
The exceptions are justified by istihsan (juristic preference), which considers the practical needs and objectives of the Shari'ah.
Istihsan overrides the strict application of the rule in these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۱) الشرائط العامة لانعقاد العقود
ولا فرق بين إعراض الموجب وإعراض القابل في قطع المجلس، لأن الإيجاب وحده لا يقيد الموجب فرجوعه قبل القبول مبطل للإيجاب (۱).
فإذا كان الإيجاب بالكتاب إلى غائب فمجلس العقد إنما يبدأ منذ قراءة الغائب للكتاب ; فإذا قبل في مجلسه تم العقد وإذا انقضى مجلسه بالإعراض بطل الإيجاب ( ر ) الهداية وشروحها ٤٦١/٥، والأشباه ج ٢ الفن الثالث ص / ١٩٦).
على أن لهذه القاعدة استثناءات :
أ - فإن قبول الموصى له أو رده يشترط وقوعه بعد وفاة الموصي; ولا يعتبر قبوله ولا رده قبل الوفاة.
ويكفي لاعتباره قابلاً بعد الوفاة أن يسكت فلا يرد الوصية. حتى لو مات بلا قبول ولا رد بعد وفاة الموصي كان المال الموصى به ميراثاً لورثة الموصى له استحساناً .
ب - وكذا في الوكالة والوقف إذا وكل غائباً ; أو وقف على غائب، فإنه يعتبر مجلس علم الوكيل أو الموقوف عليه ; فإذا لم يردا فيه الوكالة أو الوقف كانا مبرمين.
(۱) يتراءى من نصوص فقهاء المذهبين الحنفي والشافعي في تصوير مجلس العقد أنهم يعتبرون فيه عنصرين (أحدهما) مكان العاقدين و (الثاني) وضعهما، وهو حالة إقبال العاقدين على التفاوض فيختلف مجلس العقد بتبدل أحد هذين العنصرين :
أ - فإذا تبدل مكان العاقدين بين الإيجاب والقبول يبطل الإيجاب فلا ينعقد العقد بالقبول بعد ذلك ولو ظلا يتفاوضان.
ب - وإذا انقطعت حالة التفاوض بإعراض أحدهما بعد الإيجاب لم ينعقد العقد أيضاً بالقبول بعد ذلك ولو بقيا في مكانهما .
لكننا نرى أنه لا يوجد دليل شرعي على العنصر المكاني فاقتصرنا هنا في تصوير مجلس العقد على العنصر الثاني وهو حالة التفاوض لأنه هو الأقرب إلى سماحة الشريعة ومقاصدها، وهو الذي قد تشعر به نصوص فقهاء المذهب الحنبلي إذ يصرحون بأن : حالة المجلس كحالة العقد ( ر ): الشرح الكبير على المقنع ٤/٤).
Chapter (31): General conditions for the formation of contracts. There is no difference between the turning away of the offeror and the turning away of the offeree in terminating the session, because the offer alone does not bind the offeror; his withdrawal before acceptance invalidates the offer. If the offer is made by letter to an absent person, the contract session only begins from the time the absent person reads the letter; if he accepts in his session, the contract is concluded, and if his session ends by turning away, the offer becomes void. However, this rule has exceptions: (a) The acceptance or rejection of the legatee is conditional upon occurring after the death of the testator; his acceptance or rejection before death is not considered. It suffices for him to be considered as accepting after death that he remains silent and does not reject the will. Even if he dies without acceptance or rejection after the testator's death, the bequeathed property becomes inheritance for the legatee's heirs, by istihsan. (b) Similarly, in agency and endowment, if one appoints an absent agent or endows to an absent beneficiary, the session is considered to be the session of knowledge of the agent or beneficiary; if they do not reject the agency or endowment in it, they become binding. (1) It appears from the texts of Hanafi and Shafi'i jurists regarding the conceptualization of the contract session that they consider two elements in it: (first) the place of the contracting parties, and (second) their condition, which is the state of engagement in negotiation. Thus the contract session changes with the change of either of these two elements: (a) If the place of the contracting parties changes between offer and acceptance, the offer becomes void, and the contract is not concluded by subsequent acceptance even if they continue negotiating. (b) If the state of negotiation is interrupted by the turning away of one of them after the offer, the contract is also not concluded by subsequent acceptance even if they remain in their place. However, we see that there is no shar'i evidence for the spatial element, so we have restricted ourselves here in conceptualizing the contract session to the second element, which is the state of negotiation, because it is closer to the leniency and objectives of the Shari'ah, and it is what the texts of Hanbali jurists seem to suggest, as they explicitly state: 'The state of the session is like the state of the contract' (see: Al-Sharh al-Kabir 'ala al-Muqni', 4/4).
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a qawida (legal maxim) with its exceptions from Hanafi contract law. The rule states that turning away (i'rad) by either the offeror or offeree terminates the contract session (majlis al-'aqd), thereby invalidating the offer. This is because an offer alone does not bind the offeror; he may revoke before acceptance. The text then provides two exceptions: (1) In a will (wasiyyah), the legatee's acceptance or rejection is effective only after the testator's death; silence suffices as acceptance, and if the legatee dies without accepting or rejecting, the bequeathed property passes to his heirs by istihsan. (2) In agency (wakalah) and endowment (waqf) involving an absent person, the session is the session of knowledge—if the agent or beneficiary does not reject upon learning, the contract becomes binding. The footnote (1) discusses the elements of the contract session. Hanafi and Shafi'i jurists traditionally consider two elements: the place of the contracting parties and their state of negotiation. Changing either terminates the session. However, the author (Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa) argues that the spatial element has no shar'i basis and adopts only the state of negotiation, aligning with Hanbali jurisprudence. This commentary refines the classical Hanafi view, emphasizing the objectives of the Shari'ah.

Key Terms
إعراض (i'rad): Turning away or disengagement from negotiation, terminating the contract session.
مجلس العقد (majlis al-'aqd): The contract session, defined by the state of negotiation (and optionally the place) from offer to acceptance.
إيجاب (ijab): The offer made by one party to initiate a contract.
قبول (qabul): The acceptance by the offeree, completing the contract if made in the same session.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of departing from strict analogy for the sake of practical need or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a will (wasiyyah), when is the acceptance of the legatee considered effective according to the passage?
A) At the time the testator makes the will.
B) Immediately upon the testator's death, even if the legatee rejects.
C) Only after the testator's death, and silence is sufficient for acceptance.
D) At any time before the testator's death.

Key Takeaway
Turning away by either party ends the contract session, but exceptions in wills, agency, and endowment use alternative definitions of session (based on death or knowledge) to ensure practical legal outcomes.

Refining the Contract Session: The State of Negotiation Over Location
Author's refinement of the contract session definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two elements traditionally used to define the contract session (place and negotiation), and explain why the author adopts only the state of negotiation, citing the Hanbali view.

Core Idea
The contract session is defined by the state of negotiation, not the location, aligning with Hanbali leniency.

Classical Hanafi and Shafi'i jurists considered both the physical place and the state of negotiation as defining the contract session. Changing either would void the offer. However, Mustafa al-Zarqa critically re-examines this: he finds no shar'i evidence for the spatial element and restricts the session definition to the state of negotiation, citing Hanbali jurisprudence as supportive. This lesson explores that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two elements of the contract session from classical jurists
The footnote states that Hanafi and Shafi'i jurists consider two elements: (1) the place of the contracting parties, and (2) their state of engagement in negotiation. Changing either terminates the session.
The text gives examples: changing place voids the offer even if negotiation continues; breaking negotiation voids the offer even if place remains.
2
Understand the consequence of each element
If the place changes between offer and acceptance, the offer becomes void (even if they continue negotiating). If the state of negotiation breaks (by turning away), the offer is void (even if they remain in the same place).
This reveals that both elements are independently sufficient to end the session.
3
Analyze the author's critique of the spatial element
The author asserts: 'we see that there is no shar'i evidence for the spatial element.' He therefore limits the session definition to the state of negotiation.
The author's approach is based on the objectives of Shari'ah (leniency) and the texts of Hanbali jurists.
4
Recognize the Hanbali alternative
The author quotes the Hanbali maxim: 'The state of the session is like the state of the contract' (حالة المجلس كحالة العقد). This supports the idea that only the state of negotiation matters.
Hanbali jurisprudence does not require spatial continuity.
5
Apply the refined definition
In practice, if the parties change location but continue negotiating, the session remains intact according to the author's refined view. If either party turns away, the session ends.
This refinement simplifies the session concept and reduces invalidation of contracts.

Source Text

عنصر مكاني (unsur makani): The spatial element of the contract session; the physical place where the contracting parties are present.

Silence as Acceptance: Rules and Exceptions
States the principle that silence is not acceptance and lists exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'لا ينسب إلى ساكت قول' to determine when silence constitutes acceptance or forfeiture, and evaluate exceptions using custom (urf) and circumstantial evidence (qarina).

Core Idea
Silence is not acceptance unless custom or action speaks for it.

This lesson examines the Hanafi legal maxim that silence is not attributed to a person as a statement. While this is the default rule, there are important exceptions where silence does carry legal effect, such as in certain contracts and forfeitures. Understanding when silence counts and when it doesn’t is essential for a mufti evaluating tacit agreements and rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Base Qawida
Identify the maxim: لا ينسب إلى ساكت قول (No statement is attributed to a silent person). Understand its default implication: silence is not acceptance.
This is the general rule (asl).
2
Identify the Exceptions (Furuat)
List the six cases from the passage where silence is given legal effect. Note the supporting qarina or urf in each.
Each exception has an external indicator that overrides the default.
3
Analyze the Role of Qarina and Urf
For each exception, identify the specific action or custom that serves as evidence of consent. Distinguish between cases where action replaces speech (e.g., bai' al-ta'ati) and where custom does (e.g., virgin's silence).
The qarina must be strong enough to substitute for explicit speech.
4
Apply the Qawida to a New Scenario
Consider a case not explicitly in the furuat: a buyer receives goods and remains silent for a week without paying. Determine if silence indicates acceptance. Refer to the qawida and the requirement of a qarina.
Without a supporting action or custom, silence does not create an obligation.
5
Recognize Modern Restrictions
Note the footnote: positive legislation (e.g., rent control laws) can override the qawida. A mufti must consider applicable civil law before applying the maxim.
This exemplifies the interaction between fiqh and contemporary regulation.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
وعلى هذا فإنّ السكوت لا يعتبر في الأصل قبولاً، وكان من القواعد أنه لا ينسب إلى ساكت قول (المجلة / ٦٧) .
وإذا كان الفقهاء قد انتهوا إلى تصحيح التعاقد في بعض الأحوال دون ما لفظ فما ذلك منهم إلا بعد الاستيثاق من وجود عمل ظاهري أو قرينة تقوم مقام اللفظ؛ كما في بيع التعاطي الآنف الشرح، فإن فيه فعلاً تنفيذياً قام مقام اللفظ ؛ وكما في تجدد عقد الإجارة بالسكوت إذا عقد الطرفان الإيجار كل شهر بأجرة معينة، حيث ينعقد على شهر واحد ؛ ثم كلما دخل شهر جديد وهما ساكتان دون فسخ، تجددت الإجارة بينهما. ولو أنذر المؤجر المستأجر بزيادة الأجر اعتباراً من الشهر القادم فاستمر في السكنى حتى دخل الشهر وهو ساكت اعتبر قبولاً، ولزمته الأجرة الزائدة (م/ ٤٣٨ و ٤٩٤)(١).
وكذا سكوت الفتاة البكر إذا استأمرها - أي استأذنها - وليها في عقد تكاحها، إذ يعتبر سكوتها إذناً وتوكيلاً بالعقد استناداً إلى دلالة العادة والعرف، لأن المعتاد من أمثالها الاستحياء عن إظهار الرغبة لا عن الرفض. والقاعدة في السكوت أنه لا أثر له إلا في أنواع من الإسقاطات، كسقوط حق الشفعة بالسكوت عن الطلب، وسقوط خيار البلوغ بسكوت الفتاة عن فسخ النكاح، كما يعرف في مواضعه.
الخطأ، الأساس الشرعي في تمييز العمد هي [؟] ويجري فيها [؟] من [؟]
(١) هذا مقيد لدينا اليوم بقوانين تمديد عقود الإيجار التي تمنع المؤجر من حق التخلية أو زيادة الأجرة عن النسبة التي عينها القانون، فلا يُعتبر اليوم هذا السكوت قبولاً ملزماً .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. And on this basis, silence is not considered acceptance in principle, and it was from the rules that no statement is attributed to a silent person (Majalla / 67). And if the jurists have concluded to validate contracting in some cases without verbal expression, that is only after ascertaining the existence of an apparent action or a circumstantial evidence that takes the place of speech, as in the sale by exchange (bay' al-ta'ati) explained earlier, for it contains an executive act that took the place of speech; and as in the renewal of a lease contract by silence when the two parties contract the lease each month for a specified rent, where it is concluded for one month; then whenever a new month enters while both are silent without cancellation, the lease is renewed between them. And if the landlord notifies the tenant of an increase in rent as of the next month, and he continues to reside until the month enters while he is silent, it is considered acceptance, and the increased rent becomes binding upon him (M/438 and 494)(1). Similarly, the silence of a virgin girl when her guardian asks her permission – i.e., seeks her consent – for her marriage contract, for her silence is considered permission and authorization for the contract based on the indication of custom and usage, because the usual among her like is shyness from showing desire, not from refusal. And the rule regarding silence is that it has no effect except in types of forfeitures (isqatat), such as the forfeiture of the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) by silence in demanding, and the forfeiture of the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) by the girl's silence in annulling the marriage, as is known in its places. Error, the shari'i basis for distinguishing intent is [؟] and it applies in [؟] from [؟] (1) This is restricted today by laws extending lease contracts that prevent the landlord from the right of eviction or increasing rent more than the percentage determined by law, so today this silence is not considered binding acceptance.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a qawida with its furuat (applications). It opens by stating the well-established Hanafi rule that silence (sukut) is not legally considered acceptance (qabul) in principle. This is a default rule, as codified in the Majalla (Article 67): 'لا ينسب إلى ساكت قول' (No statement is attributed to a silent person). However, the author immediately introduces exceptions where silence does have legal effect, but only when supported by an apparent act (amal zahiri) or a strong circumstantial evidence (qarina) that takes the place of speech. The key is that the jurists require external indicia to overcome the maxim. The first example is bai' al-ta'ati (mutual exchange by action without verbal offer/acceptance), where the actual delivery and payment serve as evidence of consent. Second, renewal of a lease (ijara) by mutual silence when both parties continue after the first month: the silence of both without objection indicates renewal. Third, when a landlord announces a rent increase and the tenant remains silent while continuing occupancy, the tenant's silence is acceptance and the increased rent is binding. Fourth, the silence of a virgin (bikr) when her guardian asks for permission to marry her is deemed consent because custom (urf) dictates that modesty prevents explicit verbal acceptance. Finally, the passage notes that silence is only effective in certain forfeitures (isqatat): e.g., silence in claiming shuf'a leads to its loss, and the virgin's silence in not annulling the marriage after puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) results in its forfeiture. The footnote (1) provides a modern restriction: in today's legal systems with rent control laws, silence may not have the same binding effect. The unclear lines about 'error' appear to be a separate fragment, possibly a heading or note, but are not expanded here.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukut): Failure to speak or express objection; not considered a statement or acceptance in principle.
قرينة (qarina): Circumstantial evidence or indication that supplies meaning in place of explicit speech.
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice that is accepted as a source of legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the general rule regarding silence being considered acceptance in contracts?
A) Silence is always acceptance if the other party performs.
B) Silence is not acceptance in principle, except when supported by custom or circumstantial evidence.
C) Silence is acceptance only in marriage contracts.
D) Silence is never acceptance under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, silence is not acceptance unless supported by custom, action, or a specific legal exception for forfeitures.

Ruling on Collusion on the Consideration (Muwada'ah fi al-Badal)
Defining the rule and applications of collusion on consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim of muwada'ah fi al-badal to determine the effective consideration in contracts where the parties have a secret agreement on the price or dowry.

Core Idea
Secret collusion on the consideration does not void the contract; the hidden terms prevail.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all simulation invalidates a contract. This lesson examines the specific case where the collusion concerns only the amount of the consideration, not the contract itself. Drawing from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal, we will explore the rule, its practical applications in marriage and sale, and its exception in modern legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
State the rule: muwada'ah on the consideration does not invalidate the contract; it adjusts the apparent consideration to the secret one.
This maxim applies only when the contract itself is genuine.
2
Distinguish from muwada'ah on the origin
Contrast with collusion that fakes the entire contract – that renders the contract void. Here, only the price/dowry is simulated.
This distinction is crucial for correct application.
3
Apply to the furuat
Work through the two examples: marriage with an inflated mahr, and real estate sale with an understated price.
Apply the maxim: the hidden amount is what counts legally.
4
Note the istithna' (exception)
Recognise that in Syria, the pre-emption (shuf'ah) application is obsolete due to legal reforms. The maxim still applies where shuf'ah exists.
The exception is due to positive law, not a change in fiqh.
5
Relate to other Hanafi maxims
Link to the broader principle: contracts are judged by intent, not words. Also contrast with the maxim that muwada'ah on the origin voids the contract.
These related qawaid reinforce the reasoning.

وقد تكون المواضعة إنما تهدف إلى مقدار البدل المتعاقد عليه دون أصل العقد. وعندئذ لا تبطل المواضعة العقد، بل ينحصر تأثيرها في تعديل البدل الظاهر واعتبار ما جرت عليه المواضعة في السر.
فقد يتواضع الطرفان على بدل في السر أقل مما يتعاقدان عليه علناً،

عامل الرغبة في التساهل مع الرجل الكفء الخاطب.
وعامل الرغبة في سمعة غلاء المهر باعتبار أنه من مظاهر الشرف!!

وهذا في الحقيقة من فساد المقاييس الاجتماعية والعقلية فإن التساهل في المهور إذا كان مطلوباً شرعياً وعقلياً واجتماعياً لتسهيل الزواج وإزالة العراقيل من سبيله، فمن الواجب إعلانه ليقتدي الناس بعضهم ببعض في تخفيض مهور النساء، ويألفوه ولا يجدوا حرجاً في ترك تقاليدهم الفاسدة الفارغة ويصبح التباهي والتشرف بهذا التساهل في المهور لا في التغالي ليكون المنظور إليه إنما هو مزايا الزوج وفضائله لا كثرة ماله .
وإن عادة المواضعة هذه في مهور السمعة كثيراً ما تنشأ عنها مشكلات قضائية تربك المحاكم الإثباتات عند الاختلاف في حقيقة المهر بوفاة أحد الزوجين أو بوقوع الطلاق . . (۲) سبقت الإشارة إلى أن حق الشفعة قد ألغاه القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر خلال الطبعة الثالثة من هذا المدخل، فلم يبق لدينا مجال لتطبيق هذا الحكم (ر: ف ٧/٢٣ الحاشية).
Chapter (33): Simulation of Contracts. 333 - Collusion on the Consideration: Collusion may aim at the amount of the contracted consideration without the origin of the contract. In that case, the collusion does not invalidate the contract; rather, its effect is limited to adjusting the apparent consideration and considering what was colluded upon in secret. So the two parties may collude on a consideration in secret less than what they contract upon openly, (1) and this is frequent in marriage contracts seeking fame and reputation with a large dowry. And often people used to resort to that in the sale of real estate as a stratagem to prevent the pre-emptor from demanding to take it by pre-emption when he sees its price high. But this method has disappeared today from people's practices in our country since the issuance of the Real Property Law No. 3339/, whose article 251 stipulates that when the pre-emptor and the buyer of the real estate differ on the real price at which the purchase took place, the judge determines the value through experts (2). And the two contracting parties may collude on a consideration in secret more than they announce, and it occurs (1) This bad custom has become widespread in our country in marriage contracts: they contract on high dowries while agreeing on moderate secret dowries less than what they announce in the contract for ostentation and reputation. The families of the wives are driven to this by two factors: the desire to be lenient with the competent suitor, and the desire for the reputation of a high dowry as being a sign of honor! This is actually a corruption of social and intellectual standards. For leniency in dowries, if it is required legally, rationally, and socially to facilitate marriage and remove obstacles from its path, then it must be declared so that people follow each other in reducing women's dowries, get used to it, and not find it difficult to leave their corrupt empty traditions, and boasting and honor become in this leniency in dowries not in extravagance, so that what is considered is the husband's merits and virtues, not the abundance of his money. And this custom of collusion in reputation dowries often gives rise to judicial problems that confuse the courts regarding proof when there is a dispute over the real dowry upon the death of one of the spouses or upon divorce. (2) It was previously indicated that the right of pre-emption was abolished by the Syrian Civil Code issued during the third edition of this Introduction, so we have no room to apply this ruling (see: Section 7/23 footnote).
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the rule of 'collusion on the consideration' (al-muwada'ah fi al-badal). The author defines a specific type of simulated contract: where the parties agree secretly on a different price or consideration than what they declare publicly, but the contract itself is genuine. The ruling is that such collusion does not invalidate the contract; instead, it adjusts the apparent consideration to what was secretly agreed. This is a Hanafi position that gives effect to the true intention of the parties. The text then provides two practical applications. The first is in marriage contracts: parties may declare a high dowry for prestige but secretly agree on a lower one. The hidden dowry is the legally effective one. The author criticizes this practice as socially harmful. The second application is in real estate sales to circumvent the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah): parties might declare a low price but secretly agree on a higher one to deter the pre-emptor. However, the author notes that this stratagem is now obsolete in Syria due to legal reforms that abolished pre-emption and gave judges power to determine the real price. The passage also mentions that collusion can be on a secret higher price as well. In all cases, the hidden consideration prevails. This rule is a sub-maxim (dabt) of the general principle that simulation invalidates the contract only when it concerns the existence of the contract itself.

Key Terms
المواضعة (al-muwada'ah): A secret agreement between two parties that contradicts the apparent terms of the contract or declaration.
البدل (al-badal): The consideration or exchange in a contract, such as the price in a sale or the dowry in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi marriage contract, the parties publicly declare a dowry of $50,000 but secretly agree on $30,000. What is the effective dowry?
A) $50,000 (the declared amount)
B) $30,000 (the secret agreement)
C) The contract is void due to collusion
D) The judge chooses the average

Key Takeaway
Secret collusion on the amount of consideration does not void a Hanafi contract; the hidden terms are legally binding.

The Hanafi Rule on Collusion (Muwada‘a) Regarding Contract Price
Explaining legal rule on collusion regarding contract price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on muwada‘a regarding the price, apply it to new cases, and identify when the exception applies.

Core Idea
Collusion on price does not invalidate the contract; the secret price counts only if both parties explicitly declare the apparent price as fictitious.

In contractual transactions, parties sometimes agree on a secret price while stating a different price publicly. This is called muwada‘a on the consideration. The Hanafi school provides a clear rule to determine which price is legally binding, depending on whether the parties explicitly stated that the apparent price has no effect. This lesson unpacks that rule and its applications in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English
Read the qawida: if parties collude on a price but contract on another, they must declare the apparent price as without effect for the secret price to bind.
The maxim is sughra (minor) in the Hanafi legal framework.
2
Identify the condition for the secret price to be binding
The condition is an explicit declaration at the time of collusion that the apparent price is fictitious.
Without this declaration, the apparent price stands.
3
Apply the maxim to the three furuat given
Work through each furu': (1) secret lower price to avoid pre-emption, (2) secret higher price to reduce taxes, (3) secret lower dowry for reputation.
In each case, the secret price binds only if the parties explicitly declare the apparent price as ineffective.
4
Identify the istithna' and its rationale
The exception: if the parties do not expressly declare the apparent price as fictitious, the apparent price is followed even if there was a prior collusion. Reason: the collusion alone is not conclusive; they may have changed their minds.
The governing qawida is 'العبرة بالظاهر ما لم يصرح بخلافه' (consideration is given to the apparent unless explicitly stated otherwise).
5
Note the related qawida
The parallel qawida: العقود تنصرف على ما قصد به المتعاقدان (contracts are interpreted according to the intention of the contracting parties).
This reinforces that explicit declaration reveals intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۳) صورية العقود
۳۳۳ - :: المواضعة في البدل :
وقد تكون المواضعة إنما تهدف إلى مقدار البدل المتعاقد عليه دون أصل العقد. وعندئذ لا تبطل المواضعة العقد، بل ينحصر تأثيرها في تعديل البدل الظاهر واعتبار ما جرت عليه المواضعة في السر.
فقد يتواضع الطرفان على بدل في السر أقل مما يتعاقدان عليه علناً، وهذا كثير الوقوع في عقود الأنكحة ابتغاء الشهرة والسمعة بالمهر الكثير وكثيراً ما كان الناس يلجؤون إلى ذلك في بيع العقار احتيالاً لمنع صاحب الشفعة فيه من أن يطلب أخذه بالشفعة عندما يرى ثمنه غالياً. ولكن هذه الطريقة قد زالت اليوم من أساليب الناس في بلادنا منذ صدور قانون الملكية العقارية ذي الرقم ۳۳۳۹/ الذي تنص المادة / ٢٥١/ منه على أنه عند اختلاف الشفيع ومشتري العقار في الثمن الحقيقي الذي وقع به الشراء يتولى القاضي تحديد القيمة بواسطة خبراء .
وقد يتواضع المتعاقدان على بَدَل في السر أكثر مما يعلنان، ويحصل ذلك في بيع العقار بغية تخفيف رسوم الانتقال المطروحة على أساس البدل .
ففي جميع صور المواضعة على مقدار البدل يعتبر بدل السر المتواضع عليه إذا صَرَّح الطرفان عند المواضعة بأنّ البدل الذي سيذكر في العقد هو بدل لا عبرة له. صوري
وأما إذا لم يصرحا بذلك عند المواضعة فالعبرة للبدل المتعاقد عليه علناً، لأنه هو الأصل الظاهر الاعتبار، إذ كثيراً ما يتفاوض الطرفان على بدل، ثم يتغيّر رأيهما فيتفقان في العقد على خلافه، فلا يبطل اعتبار مقدار البدل الظاهر في العقد إلا إذا كانا قد صرحا في المواضعة بعدم اعتباره (ر: البدائع ۱۷۷/٥).
Chapter (33): Fictitiousness of Contracts
333 - Collusion regarding the consideration:
Collusion may aim at the amount of the contracted consideration without the contract itself. In that case, collusion does not invalidate the contract; rather, its effect is limited to modifying the apparent consideration and considering what was colluded upon in secret.
Thus, the two parties may collude on a price in secret that is less than what they contract publicly. This frequently occurs in marriage contracts seeking fame and reputation with a large dowry, and people often resorted to this in real estate sales fraudulently to prevent the pre-emptor from demanding to take it by pre-emption when he sees its price high. But this method has disappeared today from the practices of people in our country since the issuance of the Real Estate Law No. 3339/, whose Article 251 stipulates that when the pre-emptor and the buyer of the real estate disagree on the actual price at which the purchase occurred, the judge determines the value through experts.
And the two contracting parties may collude on a price in secret that is more than what they declare, and that happens in real estate sales with the aim of reducing transfer fees imposed on the basis of the price.
In all forms of collusion on the amount of the consideration, the secret price colluded upon is considered if the two parties declared at the time of collusion that the price that will be mentioned in the contract is a price with no effect, fictitious.
But if they did not declare that at the time of collusion, then consideration is given to the price contracted publicly, because it is the apparent origin to be considered, as often the parties negotiate on a price, then their opinion changes and they agree in the contract on something else, so the consideration of the apparent price amount in the contract is not invalidated unless they had declared in the collusion its non-consideration (see: Bada'i' 5/177).
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm discusses a specific type of fictitious contract: collusion (muwada‘a) regarding the consideration (badal) rather than the contract itself. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that such collusion does not nullify the contract; it only affects the apparent price. He gives two examples: (1) parties secretly agree on a lower price than the public one (common in marriages for a high dowry or in real estate to deter the pre-emptor), and (2) they secretly agree on a higher price than the public one (to reduce transfer taxes). The key rule is that the secret price is considered only if the parties explicitly state at the time of collusion that the apparent price is without effect. If they do not so state, the apparent price prevails because it is the original, and they may have changed their mind. This rule derives from the Hanafi school, as referenced from al-Bada’i‘. The author also notes that the first example (to avoid pre-emption) is now obsolete in Syria due to legal changes. This principle is a minor legal maxim (sughra) governing contracts where parties have two prices.

Key Terms
مواضعة (muwāḍa‘a): Collusion or mutual pretense between two parties regarding the consideration in a contract, where they agree on a secret price different from the publicly stated one.
بدل صوري (badal ṣūrī): A fictitious price that is stated in the contract but is not intended to be the actual price by agreement of both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule in the passage, when does the secret price in a muwada‘a contract become the binding consideration?
A) Whenever there is any prior collusion between the parties.
B) When the secret price is lower than the apparent price.
C) When the parties explicitly declare at the time of collusion that the apparent price has no effect.
D) When the court determines the real price through experts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, collusion on the price does not invalidate a contract; the secret price binds only if the parties explicitly declare the apparent price as fictitious.

Definition of Khilabah and Its Prophetic Basis
Defining khilabah with its prophetic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khilabah as a legal defect in contracts and identify its prophetic evidence.

Core Idea
Deception that induces false consent gives the deceived party a right to rescind.

Khilabah is the Islamic legal term for deception in contracts that vitiates consent. This lesson presents its precise definition as given by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, along with the hadith that forms the basis of this rule. Understanding this definition is essential for identifying contractual defects and issuing correct fatwas in sale and contract law.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the definition of khilabah
Learn the exact wording: that one party deceives the other by a delusive means (verbal or actual) inducing consent that would not otherwise be given.
The definition includes both speech and acts.
2
Identify the hadith evidence
The hadith of Habban ibn al-Ansari provides the basis: the Prophet instructed him to say 'la khilabah' and granted a three-day option.
This is the primary dalil for khiyar due to deception.
3
Understand the linguistic root
Khilabah linguistically means deception (khud'ah), from the root kh-l-b, as in 'cloud that promises rain but is empty'.
The author uses this to show the term's fit.
4
Recognize the author's terminological choice
Al-Zarqa prefers khilabah over tadlis as the general title for all forms of deception in contracts, because tadlis is more specific.
This is an ijithad in terminology for fatwa purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
/١/٣٥ - الخلابة في العقد هي: أن يخدع أحد العاقدين الآخر بوسيلة موهمة قولية أو فعلية تحمله على الرضا في العقد بما لم يكن ليرضى به لولاها. وأصل فكرتها الفقهية ما ورد في الحديث النبوي أن حَبَّان بن الأنصاري كان يُغبن في المبايعات فقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : «إذا بايعت فقل : لا خلابة ولي الخيار ثلاثة أيام». (1) الخلابة : (بكسر الخاء المعجمة) معناها في اللغة الخديعة وهي مصدر من خلبه يخلبه إذا خدعه بالقول اللطيف، ومنه قولهم : سحاب أو برق خُلب (بضم الخاء وتشديد اللام المفتوحة، وزان : سُكر) إذا كان يوهم أنه ممطر وهو فارغ (ر: النهاية لابن الأثير). وتلفت النظر سلفاً هنا إلى أننا اخترنا لفظ الخلابة عنواناً عاماً لجميع صور الخديعة التي يسميها علماء القانون في نظرية العقد، وبعض فقهاء الشريعة الإسلامية تدليساً كما سننبه عليه في آخر بحث الخلابة، مع بيان سبب ترجيحنا لفظها على لفظ التدليس.
35/1 - Khilabah in a contract is: that one of the two contracting parties deceives the other by a delusive means, verbal or actual, which induces him to consent to the contract in a way he would not have consented to without it. The basis of its juristic idea is what is mentioned in the prophetic hadith that Habban ibn al-Ansari used to be cheated in sales, so the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'When you make a sale, say: no deception, and you have the option for three days.' (1) Khilabah: (with kasra on the kha') its literal meaning is deception; it is a verbal noun from khallabahu, yukhallibuhu, if he deceives him with gentle speech. From this comes the saying: a cloud or lightning khlub (with damma on the kha' and shadda on the lam, like sukr) if it gives the impression of rain but is empty. (See Lisan al-Arab, al-Nihaya by Ibn al-Athir). I draw attention here in advance that I have chosen the word khilabah as a general title for all forms of deception which legal scholars call in contract theory, and some Islamic jurists call 'tadlis', as we shall point out at the end of the discussion of khilabah, with an explanation of why we prefer the term khilabah over tadlis.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines khilabah as deception in a contract that induces consent that would not have been given otherwise. The definition includes both verbal and actual deceptive means. The author traces the juristic concept to the hadith of Habban (or Hanzala?) where the Prophet instructed him to say 'la khilabah' and granted a three-day option. This hadith is the basis for the legal concept. The author also clarifies the linguistic meaning of khilabah as deception (khud'ah) and explains why he chooses this term over tadlis, which is used by some jurists and legal theorists. The lesson establishes that khilabah is a defect of consent that gives the deceived party a right to annul the contract (khiyar al-ghabn or khiyar al-tadlis). This is a foundational definition for fatwa in sale and contract law.

Key Terms
الخلابة (al-khilabah): Deception in a contract by any means that induces consent that would not otherwise occur.
الخديعة (al-khud'ah): Deception in general; the linguistic meaning of khilabah.
التدليس (al-tadlis): A form of deception where a defect is concealed or goods are misrepresented.

Khilabah is a defect of consent in contracts, defined as deception inducing agreement that would not otherwise occur, and is grounded in the Prophet's hadith granting an option to rescind.

Practical Deception in Description: Definition and Effect
Defines practical deception and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define practical deception in description (al-taghrir al-fi'li) and state its legal effect according to the majority of jurists, including the Hanafi position on khiyar.

Core Idea
Practical deception in description gives the deceived party the option to rescind the contract, even without financial disadvantage.

This lesson defines a key concept in the law of sales: practical deception (al-taghrir al-fi'li wa al-tazwir fi al-wasf). Unlike mere exaggeration (ghaban), this form of deception involves an act that creates a false impression about the object of sale. The author provides several examples and then explains the legal consequence: the deceived party has an option (khiyar) to annul the contract. This lesson will dissect the definition and its authoritative Hanafi underpinning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the author's definition of practical deception in description: 'tazwir wasf fi mahall al-'aqd yuwahim fi al-ma'qud 'alayh mizya masnu'a ghayr haqiqiyya.'
The definition emphasizes falsifying a description to create a false impression of a manufactured advantage.
2
Examine the examples
Review the examples given: deceptive arrangement of goods, manipulating water flow in a mill, adjusting a car's odometer, and the classic musarra animal case.
These illustrate acts (fi'l) that misrepresent a quality.
3
Extract the legal effect
The majority of jurists hold that this type of deception gives the deceived party an option (khiyar) to rescind the contract, even if no gross disadvantage (ghaban fahish) is present.
The rationale is that the missing quality was a motivating factor (wasf maqsud) at the time of contracting.
4
Note the Hanafi nuance
While the majority grants khiyar, Abu Hanifa restricts the remedy to compensation (arsh) for the decrease in value, not rescission. This will be explored further in Lesson 2.
This shows that not all deception results in contract rescission in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ب) التغرير في الوصف:
والتغرير الفعلي في الوصف يكون بتزوير وصف في محل العقد المتعاقد يوهم في المعقود عليه مزية مصطنعة غير حقيقية. وذلك كتوجيه البضاعة المعروضة للبيع بأن يضع الجيد منها في الأعلى ليكون هو المنظور كالوجه لها ويجعل الرديء في الأسفل وكذا إذا كان ماء الرحى (الطاحون المائي) قليلاً، فحبس بائعها الماء ثم أرسله عند عرضها على البيع ليوهم المشتري أن ماءها - وهو قوتها المحرّكة - غزير دفاق . ومن هذا القبيل اليوم في عصرنا تلاعب بائع السيارة المستعملة بعداد المسافة فيها وإرجاع أرقامه لإيهام المشتري قلة استعمالها.
ومن أشهر أمثلة التغرير في دواب الحلب التي تقتنى لأجل اللبن تضرية هذه الدواب عند بيعها وذلك بأن تكون الشاة أو البقرة أو الناقة قليلة اللبن أو ناضبته فيربط صاحبها أخلاف ضرعها مدة حتى يجتمع لبنها ويحفل ضرعها أي يمتلىء، ثم يعرضها للبيع إيهاماً بغزارة لبنها . فإذا حلبها المشتري أول مرة ظهر له بعدها نضوب اللبن. وهي معروفة عند الفقهاء بمسألة : الشاة المُصَرَّاة .
الأخلاف لضرع الناقة ونحوها كالحلمات لأثداء النساء، مفردها خلف (بكسر فسكون).
المُصَرَّاة: (بضم الميم وتشديد الراء المفتوحة): اسم مفعول من التصرية؛ وهي في الأصل حبس الماء بسد مجراه، ثم استعمل لحبس اللبن في الضرع.

الفصل (٣٥) عيوب الرضا - الخلابة
وقد نهى النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عن تصرية الإبل والغنم وثبت عنه أنه نهى عن: بيع المحفلات. وجمهور الفقهاء على أن هذا النوع من التغرير - أعني التغرير الفعلي في الوصف - يوجب للمغرور خياراً في إبطال العقد ولو لم يصحبه غبن، لأنه قد يكون مقصوده تلك الصفة الموهم بها في المعقود عليه؛ فهي صفة ملحوظة عند التعاقد، فيوجب فواتها الخيار للعاقد المغرور كفوات الصفة المشروطة (ر : المغني والشرح الكبير (٨٠/٤ - (۸۱)
روى البخاري ومسلم في البيوع قال صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا تصروا الإبل والغنم فمن ابتاعها فهو بخير النظرين بعد أن يحلبها، إن شاء أمسك وإن شاء ردها وصاعاً من تمر.
روى البخاري ومسلم في البيوع قال : من اشترى محفلة فردها فليرد معها صاعاً .
والمحفلة : (بفتح الفاء المشددة) اسم مفعول من التحفيل بمعنى التصرية والتجميع، ومنه يقال لمجامع الناس : محافل.
في قضية الشاة المصراة اختلاف وكلام طويل في فقه المذاهب الاجتهادية. فقد روي في الصحيحين عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: لا تصروا الإبل والغنم، فمن ابتاعها فإنه بخير النظرين بعد أن يحلبها : إن شاء أمسك، وإن شاء ردها وصاعاً من تمر.
وقد اختلفت الاجتهادات في ذلك :
- فمنهم من أوجب ردها مع صاع من تمر مطلقاً دون النظر إلى كمية اللبن المحلوب منها، عملاً بظاهر الحديث.
- وذهب أبو حنيفة إلى عدم ردها وإنما يرجع المشتري بنقصان قيمتها إن كانت أنقص مما اشتراها به.
- وذهب أبو يوسف من أصحاب أبي حنيفة في رواية عنه إلى أن المشتري يردها قيمة اللبن المحلوب لا مع صاع من تمر، لأنه قد يكون أقل أو أكثر قيمة. (ر: رد المحتار ٩٦/٤ - ٩٧).
فأبو يوسف يحمل ما ورد في الحديث من رد صاع تمر على أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لحظ تكافؤه مع اللبن المحلوب في تلك الحادثة والتمر هو المال الميسور إذ ذاك فقضى به ولكن الأصل الواجب في التعادل هو القيمة. ورأي أبي يوسف كما ترى في هذه القضية، هو أعدل الآراء وأوجهها فقهاً، وهو الذي يجب التعويل عليه وهو أحسن من قول إمامه أبي حنيفة بالرجوع بنقصان قيمة الدابة المصراة فقط دون فسخ العقد، إذ قد يكون غرض المشتري إنما هو الصفة الفائتة الموهم بوجودها في البيع.
(B) Deception in Description: Practical deception in description consists of falsifying a description in the subject matter of the contract, giving a false impression of a fabricated advantage in the object of sale. Examples include displaying goods by placing the good ones on top so they are visible and the defective ones below; or if the water of a mill is low, the seller holds back water and releases it when showing the mill for sale to make the buyer think the water (its driving force) is abundant. Similarly today, tampering with the odometer of a used car to roll back the mileage to give the buyer a false impression of low usage. Among the most famous examples of deception in milking animals bought for milk is ‘tasriya’ of these animals at sale: the sheep, cow, or camel has little or no milk, so the owner ties its teats for a while so the milk collects and the udder becomes full (i.e., distended), then offers it for sale giving a false impression of abundant milk. When the buyer milks it the first time, after that the milk dries up. This is known among jurists as the issue of the ‘musarra’ animal. (Footnote on ‘akhlaf’ and ‘musarra’ definitions.) Chapter 35: Defects of Consent – Deception (Khalaba). The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited tasriya of camels and sheep, and it is established that he prohibited the sale of ‘muhaffalat’. The majority of jurists hold that this type of deception – i.e., practical deception in description – gives the deceived party an option to annul the contract even if not accompanied by gross disadvantage (ghaban), because his intention may have been that specific simulated quality in the object; it is a quality considered at the time of contracting, so its absence gives the deceived party an option like the absence of a stipulated quality. (Ref: al-Mughni and al-Sharh al-Kabir 4/80-81). Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim in the Book of Sales: The Prophet said: “Do not practice tasriya on camels and sheep; whoever buys them after that has the choice after milking them: if he wishes he may keep them, and if he wishes he may return them with a sa’ of dates.” Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim: “Whoever buys a muhaffala and returns it, let him return with it a sa’.” The muhaffala is a passive participle from tahfil meaning tasriya and gathering. Regarding the issue of the musarra animal, there is disagreement and lengthy discussion in the fiqh of the ijtihad schools. It is narrated in the Sahihayn that the Prophet said: “Do not practice tasriya on camels and sheep; whoever buys them has the choice after milking them: if he wishes he may keep them, and if he wishes he may return them with a sa’ of dates.” The ijtihads differed: Some held that it must be returned with a sa’ of dates unconditionally, regardless of the quantity of milk milked, acting on the apparent meaning of the hadith. Abu Hanifa held that it should not be returned, and the buyer only claims the decrease in value if it is less than what he paid for it. Abu Yusuf, a companion of Abu Hanifa, held in one narration that the buyer returns the value of the milk milked, not a sa’ of dates, because it may be worth less or more. (Ref: Radd al-Muhtar 4/96-97). Abu Yusuf interprets the hadith’s ruling of returning a sa’ of dates by saying that the Prophet took into account its equivalence to the milk milked in that particular incident, and dates were the available currency at that time, so he ruled accordingly, but the underlying obligatory principle is compensation in value. Abu Yusuf’s opinion, as you see, is the most just and most sound juristically, and it is the one that must be relied upon, and it is better than the opinion of his imam Abu Hanifa of only claiming the decrease in value of the musarra animal without rescission of the contract, because the buyer’s purpose may have been the missing quality that was falsely suggested existed in the sale.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and ruling concerning practical deception (al-taghrir al-fi'li) in the description of the subject matter of a contract. The author defines it as falsifying a description to create a false impression of an advantageous quality. He gives several examples, including arranging goods deceptively, manipulating water flow in a mill, tampering with car odometers, and the classic case of the musarra animal (an animal whose milk is withheld in the udder to give a false impression of high milk yield).

The author then cites the Prophetic prohibition of tasriya (withholding milk) and notes that the majority of jurists grant the deceived buyer an option to rescind the contract because the missing quality was a motivating factor. He then delves into the Hanafi ikhtilaf on this issue:

1. Some scholars (apparent meaning of the hadith) require the buyer to return the animal with a sa' of dates unconditionally.
2. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer cannot rescind the contract; he may only claim compensation (arsh) for the decrease in value due to the defect.
3. Abu Yusuf (one of Imam Abu Hanifa's leading students) holds in one narration that the buyer returns the animal and compensates the seller with the value of the milk milked (instead of a fixed sa' of dates), because the underlying principle is to ensure equivalence; the sa' of dates was a specific ruling for that particular time and place, but the general rule is to give the value.

The author (al-Zarqa) clearly prefers Abu Yusuf's opinion, calling it the most just and most sound, and states that it should be relied upon in fatwa. This demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of choosing among the positions of the imams based on strength of evidence and suitability for the time.

For the mufti, this passage illustrates how to navigate ikhtilaf within the madhhab, weighing the apparent meaning of hadith against analogical reasoning (qiyas), and selecting the opinion that best achieves justice while remaining within the Hanafi framework.

Key Terms
التغرير الفعلي (al-taghrir al-fi'li): Practical deception consisting of an act (like tying the udder) that misrepresents a quality of the object of sale.
الشاة المصراة (al-shat al-musarra): An animal (sheep, cow, camel) whose milk has been withheld in the udder to give a false impression of high yield, then sold.
المحفلة (al-muhaffala): Synonym for musarra; a passive participle from tahfil meaning gathering or withholding milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal effect of practical deception in description (al-taghrir al-fi'li fi al-wasf) according to the majority of jurists?
A) The contract is automatically void (batil).
B) The deceived party has an option (khiyar) to annul the contract even without gross disadvantage (ghaban).
C) The deceived party may only claim compensation (arsh) for the decrease in value.
D) The contract is valid and no remedy is available.

Key Takeaway
Practical deception in description gives the deceived party a khiyar to rescind the contract, reflecting the principle that a false quality induces the contract.

The Musarra Animal: Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf and the Preferred Fatwa
Discusses Hanafi ikhtilaf on musarra and author's tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ikhtilaf on the musarra animal, compare Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's positions, and identify the mufta bihi opinion preferred by al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf's opinion that the buyer returns the animal and pays the value of the milk is the most just and must be relied upon in fatwa.

The case of the musarra animal (the animal with withheld milk) is a classic example of practical deception. While the hadith appears to give a straightforward remedy (return the animal with a sa' of dates), the Hanafi school has a nuanced discussion. In this lesson, we explore the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, their proofs, and the author's tarjih. This demonstrates how a mufti navigates ikhtilaf within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
State the hadith and its apparent ruling
The hadith states: 'Do not practice tasriya... whoever buys it has the choice after milking: if he wishes he may keep it, and if he wishes he may return it with a sa' of dates.'
The apparent meaning is that the buyer may rescind the contract and must return the animal along with a sa' of dates as compensation for the milk taken.
2
Outline the three positions
Some scholars follow the literal hadith (return with sa' unconditionally). Abu Hanifa holds the buyer cannot rescind; only claims decrease in value. Abu Yusuf holds the buyer may rescind and pays the value of the milk (not a fixed sa').
The third position is an intermediate view between literalism and compensation-only.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa uses qiyas: the defect is like any other defect, so only compensation is due. Abu Yusuf interprets the sa' as representing the value; the general principle is equivalence.
Abu Yusuf's proof appeals to the underlying objective (maqsad) of the hadith rather than its literal wording.
4
Examine the author's tarjih
Al-Zarqa declares Abu Yusuf's opinion as 'a'adal al-araa' (most just) and 'wajib al-ta'wil' (must be relied upon). He argues it is better than Abu Hanifa's because the buyer may have intended the quality of high yield.
The author provides a frank evaluation, preferring the view of the student over the master in this particular case.
5
Determine the practical fatwa
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) according to this text: the buyer has the option to rescind and must compensate with the value of the milk milked.
This shows how a later Hanafi scholar (al-Zarqa) exercised tarjih to update the fatwa to better serve justice.

Source Text

الفصل (٣٥) عيوب الرضا - الخلابة
وقد نهى النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عن تصرية الإبل والغنم وثبت عنه أنه نهى عن: بيع المحفلات. وجمهور الفقهاء على أن هذا النوع من التغرير - أعني التغرير الفعلي في الوصف - يوجب للمغرور خياراً في إبطال العقد ولو لم يصحبه غبن، لأنه قد يكون مقصوده تلك الصفة الموهم بها في المعقود عليه؛ فهي صفة ملحوظة عند التعاقد، فيوجب فواتها الخيار للعاقد المغرور كفوات الصفة المشروطة (ر : المغني والشرح الكبير (٨٠/٤ - (۸۱)
روى البخاري ومسلم في البيوع قال صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا تصروا الإبل والغنم فمن ابتاعها فهو بخير النظرين بعد أن يحلبها، إن شاء أمسك وإن شاء ردها وصاعاً من تمر.
روى البخاري ومسلم في البيوع قال : من اشترى محفلة فردها فليرد معها صاعاً .
والمحفلة : (بفتح الفاء المشددة) اسم مفعول من التحفيل بمعنى التصرية والتجميع، ومنه يقال لمجامع الناس : محافل.
في قضية الشاة المصراة اختلاف وكلام طويل في فقه المذاهب الاجتهادية. فقد روي في الصحيحين عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: لا تصروا الإبل والغنم، فمن ابتاعها فإنه بخير النظرين بعد أن يحلبها : إن شاء أمسك، وإن شاء ردها وصاعاً من تمر.
وقد اختلفت الاجتهادات في ذلك :
- فمنهم من أوجب ردها مع صاع من تمر مطلقاً دون النظر إلى كمية اللبن المحلوب منها، عملاً بظاهر الحديث.
- وذهب أبو حنيفة إلى عدم ردها وإنما يرجع المشتري بنقصان قيمتها إن كانت أنقص مما اشتراها به.
- وذهب أبو يوسف من أصحاب أبي حنيفة في رواية عنه إلى أن المشتري يردها قيمة اللبن المحلوب لا مع صاع من تمر، لأنه قد يكون أقل أو أكثر قيمة. (ر: رد المحتار ٩٦/٤ - ٩٧).
فأبو يوسف يحمل ما ورد في الحديث من رد صاع تمر على أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لحظ تكافؤه مع اللبن المحلوب في تلك الحادثة والتمر هو المال الميسور إذ ذاك فقضى به ولكن الأصل الواجب في التعادل هو القيمة. ورأي أبي يوسف كما ترى في هذه القضية، هو أعدل الآراء وأوجهها فقهاً، وهو الذي يجب التعويل عليه وهو أحسن من قول إمامه أبي حنيفة بالرجوع بنقصان قيمة الدابة المصراة فقط دون فسخ العقد، إذ قد يكون غرض المشتري إنما هو الصفة الفائتة الموهم بوجودها في البيع.
(B) Deception in Description: Practical deception in description consists of falsifying a description in the subject matter of the contract, giving a false impression of a fabricated advantage in the object of sale. Examples include displaying goods by placing the good ones on top so they are visible and the defective ones below; or if the water of a mill is low, the seller holds back water and releases it when showing the mill for sale to make the buyer think the water (its driving force) is abundant. Similarly today, tampering with the odometer of a used car to roll back the mileage to give the buyer a false impression of low usage. Among the most famous examples of deception in milking animals bought for milk is ‘tasriya’ of these animals at sale: the sheep, cow, or camel has little or no milk, so the owner ties its teats for a while so the milk collects and the udder becomes full (i.e., distended), then offers it for sale giving a false impression of abundant milk. When the buyer milks it the first time, after that the milk dries up. This is known among jurists as the issue of the ‘musarra’ animal. (Footnote on ‘akhlaf’ and ‘musarra’ definitions.) Chapter 35: Defects of Consent – Deception (Khalaba). The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited tasriya of camels and sheep, and it is established that he prohibited the sale of ‘muhaffalat’. The majority of jurists hold that this type of deception – i.e., practical deception in description – gives the deceived party an option to annul the contract even if not accompanied by gross disadvantage (ghaban), because his intention may have been that specific simulated quality in the object; it is a quality considered at the time of contracting, so its absence gives the deceived party an option like the absence of a stipulated quality. (Ref: al-Mughni and al-Sharh al-Kabir 4/80-81). Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim in the Book of Sales: The Prophet said: “Do not practice tasriya on camels and sheep; whoever buys them after that has the choice after milking them: if he wishes he may keep them, and if he wishes he may return them with a sa’ of dates.” Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim: “Whoever buys a muhaffala and returns it, let him return with it a sa’.” The muhaffala is a passive participle from tahfil meaning tasriya and gathering. Regarding the issue of the musarra animal, there is disagreement and lengthy discussion in the fiqh of the ijtihad schools. It is narrated in the Sahihayn that the Prophet said: “Do not practice tasriya on camels and sheep; whoever buys them has the choice after milking them: if he wishes he may keep them, and if he wishes he may return them with a sa’ of dates.” The ijtihads differed: Some held that it must be returned with a sa’ of dates unconditionally, regardless of the quantity of milk milked, acting on the apparent meaning of the hadith. Abu Hanifa held that it should not be returned, and the buyer only claims the decrease in value if it is less than what he paid for it. Abu Yusuf, a companion of Abu Hanifa, held in one narration that the buyer returns the value of the milk milked, not a sa’ of dates, because it may be worth less or more. (Ref: Radd al-Muhtar 4/96-97). Abu Yusuf interprets the hadith’s ruling of returning a sa’ of dates by saying that the Prophet took into account its equivalence to the milk milked in that particular incident, and dates were the available currency at that time, so he ruled accordingly, but the underlying obligatory principle is compensation in value. Abu Yusuf’s opinion, as you see, is the most just and most sound juristically, and it is the one that must be relied upon, and it is better than the opinion of his imam Abu Hanifa of only claiming the decrease in value of the musarra animal without rescission of the contract, because the buyer’s purpose may have been the missing quality that was falsely suggested existed in the sale.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and ruling concerning practical deception (al-taghrir al-fi'li) in the description of the subject matter of a contract. The author defines it as falsifying a description to create a false impression of an advantageous quality. He gives several examples, including arranging goods deceptively, manipulating water flow in a mill, tampering with car odometers, and the classic case of the musarra animal (an animal whose milk is withheld in the udder to give a false impression of high milk yield).

The author then cites the Prophetic prohibition of tasriya (withholding milk) and notes that the majority of jurists grant the deceived buyer an option to rescind the contract because the missing quality was a motivating factor. He then delves into the Hanafi ikhtilaf on this issue:

1. Some scholars (apparent meaning of the hadith) require the buyer to return the animal with a sa' of dates unconditionally.
2. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer cannot rescind the contract; he may only claim compensation (arsh) for the decrease in value due to the defect.
3. Abu Yusuf (one of Imam Abu Hanifa's leading students) holds in one narration that the buyer returns the animal and compensates the seller with the value of the milk milked (instead of a fixed sa' of dates), because the underlying principle is to ensure equivalence; the sa' of dates was a specific ruling for that particular time and place, but the general rule is to give the value.

The author (al-Zarqa) clearly prefers Abu Yusuf's opinion, calling it the most just and most sound, and states that it should be relied upon in fatwa. This demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of choosing among the positions of the imams based on strength of evidence and suitability for the time.

For the mufti, this passage illustrates how to navigate ikhtilaf within the madhhab, weighing the apparent meaning of hadith against analogical reasoning (qiyas), and selecting the opinion that best achieves justice while remaining within the Hanafi framework.

Key Terms
التغرير الفعلي (al-taghrir al-fi'li): Practical deception consisting of an act (like tying the udder) that misrepresents a quality of the object of sale.
الشاة المصراة (al-shat al-musarra): An animal (sheep, cow, camel) whose milk has been withheld in the udder to give a false impression of high yield, then sold.
المحفلة (al-muhaffala): Synonym for musarra; a passive participle from tahfil meaning gathering or withholding milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi scholar's opinion does al-Zarqa consider the most just and must be relied upon for fatwa?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) The literalists

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's opinion that the buyer may rescind and pay the value of the milk is the preferred fatwa, as it better serves justice than the founder's compensation-only view.

Deception by Tasriyah: Hanafi Iftaa on Milk Retention
Explains the ruling on selling animals with artificial milk retention and the buyer's option.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the fatwa hierarchy for the sale of a musarrah animal by identifying the mufta bihi position and justifying it with the governing qawida and tarjih.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi for tasriyah is returning the milk's value, not the animal or a fixed commodity.

This passage examines the legal remedies for buying an animal whose udder was tied to deceive about milk yield. The hadith gives a choice to return the animal with a sa' of dates, but Hanafi imams differ on the exact remedy. Understanding this ikhtilaf and the tarjih is essential for issuing fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Define the problem: a buyer purchases an animal with artificially retained milk, discovering the deception after milking. What remedy is available?
The issue is one of deception (al-khilabah) and its effect on a sale contract.
2
State the hadith proof
Recall the Prophetic command: 'Whoever buys a muhaffalah ... if he wishes he may return it and a sa' of dates.' This is the primary textual evidence.
The hadith is authentic (muttafaq alayh) but open to interpretation.
3
Map the ikhtilaf
List the three positions: (1) Majority – obligatory return with dates; (2) Abu Hanifa – no rescission, only price reduction; (3) Abu Yusuf – return value of milk.
Abu Yusuf's position is a narration within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyse the tarjih
Understand why the author prefers Abu Yusuf's view: it aligns with the qawida that equivalence should be based on monetary value (al-asl fi al-ta'adul al-qeemah).
The author calls Abu Yusuf's view 'the most just and sound'.
5
Formulate the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa: the buyer may keep the animal and claim the value of the expected milk, or rescind and return the animal while also returning the value of milk already taken.
This is the position of fatawa mutakhkhirin (later Hanafi fatwa manuals).

Chapter (35): Defects of Consent – Deception (al-Khilabah). The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade the tasriyah of camels and sheep (1), and it is established that he forbade the sale of al-muhaffalat (2). The majority of jurists hold that this type of deception – meaning actual deception in a description – gives the deceived party an option to annul the contract even if no loss (ghabn) accompanies it, because his objective may be that attribute which was deceptively suggested in the subject matter; it is an attribute considered at the time of contracting, so its absence gives the deceived contracting party an option like the absence of a stipulated attribute. (See: al-Mughni and al-Sharh al-Kabir 4/80-81) (3). (1) Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim in the Book of Sales: He said: 'Do not practice tasriyah on camels and sheep; whoever buys them has the choice after milking them: if he wishes he may keep them, and if he wishes he may return them along with a sa' of dates.' (2) Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim in the Book of Sales: He said: 'Whoever buys a muhaffalah and returns it must return with it a sa' (of dates).' Al-muhaffalah (with a shaddah on the fa') is a passive participle from al-tahfil, meaning tasriyah and accumulation; hence gatherings of people are called mahaafil. (3) Regarding the case of the musarrah sheep, there is disagreement and lengthy discussion in the fiqh of the independent schools. It is narrated in the Two Sahihs from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Do not practice tasriyah on camels and sheep; whoever buys them has the choice after milking them: if he wishes he may keep them, and if he wishes he may return them and a sa' of dates.' – The legal opinions differed on this: some obligated the return along with a sa' of dates absolutely without considering the quantity of milk obtained, acting upon the apparent meaning of the hadith. Abu Hanifa held that it should not be returned; instead the buyer gets a reduction in price if the value is less than what he paid. Abu Yusuf – one of Abu Hanifa's companions – held in one narration that the buyer returns the value of the milk obtained, not with a sa' of dates, because it may be less or more in value. (See: Radd al-Muhtar 4/96-97). Abu Yusuf interprets the hadith's requirement of a sa' of dates as meaning that the Prophet (peace be upon him) considered it equivalent to the milk obtained in that particular incident, and dates were the readily available commodity at that time, so he ruled accordingly, but the obligatory principle for equivalence is value. The opinion of Abu Yusuf in this matter, as you see, is the most just and the most juridically sound, and it is the one that must be relied upon, and it is better than the statement of his imam Abu Hanifa requiring only a reduction in the value of the musarrah animal without rescission of the contract, because the buyer's object may be the missing attribute that was deceptively suggested to exist in the sale.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific form of deception called al-khilabah or al-tasriyah – artificially retaining milk in an animal's udder to give a false impression of high milk yield. The author begins by citing the Prophetic prohibition and the hadith that grants the buyer a choice after milking: to keep the animal or return it with a sa' of dates. He then presents the majority view that such factual deception gives the buyer an option to annul the contract even without financial loss, because the missing attribute (high milk yield) was implicitly intended. The main discussion is the ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams. Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer cannot return the animal; he only gets compensation for the reduction in value if the animal is worth less. Abu Yusuf, in a narration, holds that the buyer returns the value of the milk – not a sa' of dates – because equivalency should be based on monetary value, not a fixed measure of dates, which may be more or less valuable. The author clearly prefers Abu Yusuf's view as the most just and sound, arguing that it aligns with the underlying rationale of the hadith and gives the buyer full protection of his interest in the attribute.

Key Terms
الخلابة (al-khilabah): Deception by creating a false impression of a desirable attribute in the subject matter of a contract.
التصرية (al-tasriyah): Tying up the udder of a milking animal to accumulate milk and deceive the buyer about its yield.
المحفلة (al-muhaffalah): The animal on which tasriyah has been practiced; a passive participle meaning 'the one whose milk has been retained'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the opinions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the remedy for buying a musarrah animal?
A) Abu Hanifa allows rescission; Abu Yusuf only allows compensation.
B) Abu Hanifa only allows compensation for reduction in value; Abu Yusuf allows returning the value of the milk.
C) Both require returning the animal with a sa' of dates.
D) Abu Hanifa requires returning the animal; Abu Yusuf allows keeping it with compensation.

Key Takeaway
The operative Hanafi fatwa for tasriyah is to return the monetary value of the expected milk, based on the qawida that equivalence is by value.

General Ruling on Deception (Khilabah) in Contracts
Summarizing the general ruling on deception affecting consent in contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida on khilabah to distinguish when ghabn fahish is required and when it is not, and identify the exception for trust sales.

Core Idea
Deception voids a contract only with gross loss, except in trust sales where any lie suffices.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm distills a general qawida governing deception in contracts. Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti to correctly advise on khiyar options. The key is the distinction between deception aimed at increasing price and deception in trust-based sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Recognize that all forms of deception corrupt consent and affect the binding force of the contract, giving the deceived party an option to annul.
This is the baseline ruling.
2
Distinguish the two categories
Separate cases where the aim is to increase the price (requiring ghabn fahish) from cases of betrayal in trust sales (where no loss is needed).
The aim (ghayah) determines the requirement.
3
Apply to the furuat
Test the rule against the given examples: najash and verbal misrepresentation require ghabn fahish; betrayal in murabahah gives option regardless of loss.
Use the furuat to internalize the distinction.
4
Note the istithna'
Identify the exception for trust sales (buyu' al-amanah) and understand the reason: the contract is based on the agreed capital cost.
The istithna' is based on the nature of the contract.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
Conclude that the mufta bihi is the general qawida: ghabn fahish is required except in trust sales.
This is the operative rule for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦/٣٥ - خلاصة حكم الخلابة بوجه عام : بالنظر في جميع صور الخلابة التي نقلناها من المذاهب الاجتهادية في الفقه الإسلامي (الخيانة والتناجش ،والتغرير وتدليس العيب) وفي صورها الأخرى المماثلة التي لم ننقلها وتُرى في مصادرها الفقهية من كتب المذاهب يمكن أن نستخلص للخلابة حكماً عاماً في الشريعة الإسلامية نقرر فيه التفصيل التالي : إن الخلابة بجميع صورها المتقدمة ،البيان، وأمثالها من الصور والأساليب الخادعة التي يلجأ إليها أحد العاقدين تعيب رضا العاقد الآخر وتؤثر في القوة الملزمة للعقد، فيثبت بها شرعاً للعاقد المخلوب خيار يمنح بمقتضاه حق إبطال العقد في فقه المذاهب. ولكن تارة يشترط مع الخلابة وجود غبن للعاقد المخلوب، وتارة لا يشترط الغبن، وذلك بحسب الغاية المقصودة من الخلابة : أ - فإذا كان هدف الخلابة زيادة السعر على المخلوب يشترط عندئذ في الخلابة لكي تعيب الرضا وتسوّغ إبطال العقد أن تكون مصحوبة بغبن فاحش. وذلك كما في صور التناجش، والتغرير القولي في السعر. ففي كل هذه الحالات وأمثالها إنما يقصد الخالب استزادة البدل. فإذا أدت إلى غبن فاحش أوجبت عندئذ للمخلوب الخيار وإلا فلا. ويستثنى من ذلك الخيانة في بيوع الأمانة المرابحة وأخواتها فإنها وإن كان هدفها استزادة البدل - توجب للمشتري على كل حال حق إبطال العقد، أو حق الحط من الثمن بمقدار الخيانة (على اختلاف الآراء الاجتهادية في حكم الخيانة كما سبق بيانه في بحثها ف (٢/٣٥) ولو لم يحصل للمشتري معها غبن في سعر المبيع (۱) . وذلك لأن البيع في المرابحة وأخواتها يقوم على أساس رأس المال باتفاق الطرفين. فكل كذب في بيان رأس المال يؤدي إلى خلاف المتفق عليه بين العاقدين ولو لم يكن غبن. (1) كما لو باع شيئاً بعشرة على أن رأس ماله ثمانية، ثم تبين أن رأس ماله سبعة، والشيء المبيع تساوي قيمته في السوق عشرة أو أكثر، فلا غبن على المشتري في هذه الحال رغم خيانة البائع، ومع ذلك يحق للمشتري إبطال العقد.
6/35 – Summary of the ruling on khilabah in general: Considering all the forms of khilabah that we have quoted from the ijtihad schools in Islamic jurisprudence (betrayal, najash, misrepresentation, and concealment of defect) and other similar forms that we have not quoted and are seen in their fiqh sources from the books of the schools, we can extract a general ruling on khilabah in Islamic law, in which we determine the following details: Indeed, khilabah in all its aforementioned forms and the like of deceptive forms and methods resorted to by one of the contracting parties corrupts the consent of the other party and affects the binding force of the contract. Therefore, according to Islamic law, the deceived party is given an option (khiyar) by which he has the right to annul the contract in the fiqh of the schools. But sometimes khilabah requires the existence of loss (ghabn) to the deceived party, and sometimes it does not require loss, depending on the intended purpose of the khilabah. (a) If the aim of the khilabah is to increase the price on the deceived party, then it is required that the khilabah, in order to corrupt consent and justify annulment of the contract, be accompanied by gross fraud (ghabn fahish). This is as in the forms of najash and verbal misrepresentation regarding the price. In all these cases and the like, the deceiver intends to seek extra compensation. So if it leads to gross fraud, it gives the deceived party the option; otherwise, no. Excluded from this is betrayal (khiyanah) in trust sales (buyu al-amanah) like murabahah and its sisters, for even though its aim is to seek extra compensation, it gives the buyer in any case the right to annul the contract or the right to reduce the price by the amount of betrayal (according to different ijtihad views on the ruling of betrayal as previously explained in its discussion in (2/35)), even if the buyer does not suffer loss in the price of the sold item (1). This is because the sale in murabahah and its sisters is based on the capital cost by agreement of both parties. So any lie in stating the capital cost leads to a departure from what was agreed between the contracting parties, even if there is no loss. (1) For example, if someone sells an item for ten, stating that his capital is eight, then it becomes clear that his capital was seven, and the sold item's market value is ten or more, so there is no loss to the buyer in this case despite the seller's betrayal, yet the buyer still has the right to annul the contract.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm provides a general ruling (hukm 'amm) on khilabah (deception) in contracts. The author distills from the various forms of deception across the schools a two-part rule. First, if the deception aims solely at increasing the price (e.g., najash, false statements about price), then the deceived party only gets the option to rescind if there is gross fraud (ghabn fahish). Second, if the deception involves a breach of trust in trust-based sales (murabahah etc.), the deceived party gets the right to annul or reduce price regardless of loss, because the agreement is based on the stated capital cost. This is a qawida with two categories. The exception is the trust sales, which have their own stricter rule.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Gross fraud or excessive disparity in value that is considered unconscionable.
بيوع الأمانة (buyu' al-amanah): Trust sales where the seller discloses the cost, such as murabahah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which type of deception does the deceived party have the right to annul the contract even if there is no loss (ghabn)?
A) Najash (collusive bidding)
B) Verbal misrepresentation about price
C) Betrayal in trust sales (murabahah)
D) Concealment of defect

Key Takeaway
Deception in contracts gives an option to annul only if accompanied by gross fraud, except in trust sales where any lie suffices.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
