Deception in Contracts: When Does It Give the Right to Rescind?
General ruling on deception affecting consent and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida on khalaba to determine when a deceived party has the right to rescind a contract.

Core Idea
Deception gives the right to rescind only with gross overpayment if the aim is to raise the price, but always if the aim is to deceive about the object's quality, except in trust sales.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi maxim that distinguishes between two types of deception in sales: one that targets the price and one that targets the quality of the object. The ruling hinges on the purpose (qasd) of the deceiver. We will work through the maxim, its applications, and the important exception for trust-based sales like murabaha.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida on khalaba
Articulate the general rule: deception that aims to increase the price gives the right of rescission only when accompanied by gross ghubn (fahish); deception that aims to deceive about a quality gives rescission even without ghubn; trust-based sales (murabaha) are an exception.
2
Distinguish the two purposes of deception
Identify whether the deceiver's goal was to raise the price (e.g., collusive bidding, verbal misrepresentation about price) or to misrepresent a quality of the object (e.g., hiding a defect or exaggerating quality).
3
Apply the qawida to furuat from the text
Work through the given examples: tanajush (collusive bidding) with gross overpayment gives khiyar; selling a musarra ewe at fair price gives khiyar (deception in attribute); selling fruit with good face hiding bad quality gives khiyar even at fair price.
4
Identify the exception for trust-based sales
Note that in murabaha and similar sales, any lie about the capital cost gives the buyer the right to rescind or reduce the price, even without ghubn, because the contract is built on trust regarding the cost.
5
Distinguish pure ghubn from deception
Understand that mere gross disparity without any deception does not give the right to rescind, as it indicates the buyer's negligence, not the seller's fraud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه الإسلامي (الخيانة والتناجش ،والتغرير وتدليس العيب) وفي صورها الأخرى المماثلة التي لم ننقلها وتُرى في مصادرها الفقهية من كتب المذاهب يمكن أن نستخلص للخلابة حكماً عاماً في الشريعة الإسلامية نقرر فيه التفصيل التالي : إن الخلابة بجميع صورها المتقدمة ،البيان، وأمثالها من الصور والأساليب الخادعة التي يلجأ إليها أحد العاقدين تعيب رضا العاقد الآخر وتؤثر في القوة الملزمة للعقد، فيثبت بها شرعاً للعاقد المخلوب خيار يمنح بمقتضاه حق إبطال العقد في فقه المذاهب. ولكن تارة يشترط مع الخلابة وجود غبن للعاقد المخلوب، وتارة لا يشترط الغبن، وذلك بحسب الغاية المقصودة من الخلابة : أ - فإذا كان هدف الخلابة زيادة السعر على المخلوب يشترط عندئذ في الخلابة لكي تعيب الرضا وتسوّغ إبطال العقد أن تكون مصحوبة بغبن فاحش. وذلك كما في صور التناجش، والتغرير القولي في السعر. ففي كل هذه الحالات وأمثالها إنما يقصد الخالب استزادة البدل. فإذا أدت إلى غبن فاحش أوجبت عندئذ للمخلوب الخيار وإلا فلا. ويستثنى من ذلك الخيانة في بيوع الأمانة المرابحة وأخواتها فإنها . وإن كان هدفها استزادة البدل - توجب للمشتري على كل حال حق إبطال العقد، أو حق الحط من الثمن بمقدار الخيانة (على اختلاف الآراء الاجتهادية في حكم الخيانة كما سبق بيانه في بحثها ف (٢/٣٥) ولو لم يحصل للمشتري معها غبن في سعر المبيع (۱) . وذلك لأن البيع في المرابحة وأخواتها يقوم على أساس رأس المال باتفاق الطرفين. فكل كذب في بيان رأس المال يؤدي إلى خلاف المتفق عليه بين العاقدين ولو لم يكن غبن. (1) كما لو باع شيئاً بعشرة على أن رأس ماله ثمانية، ثم تبين أن رأس ماله سبعة، والشيء المبيع تساوي قيمته في السوق عشرة أو أكثر، فلا غبن على المشتري في هذه الحال رغم خيانة البائع، ومع ذلك يحق للمشتري إبطال العقد. ب - وأما إذا كان هدف الخلابة خداع العاقد المخلوب في صفة المعقود عليه كإيهامه بالجودة أو بأي وصف آخر، فإنها تعيب الرضا وتسوّغ إبطال العقد، ولو لم يصحبها غبن في السعر، كما في الشاة المصراة إذا بيعت بثمن لا يزيد عن قيمتها غير لبون، وكالفاكهة الموجهة إذا بیعت بثمن لا يزيد عن سعر الرديء الذي تحت وجهها الجيد، لما تقدَّم أنَّ العاقد المخلوب قد يكون مقصوده الصفة الموهم بها، ففواتها يوجب له الخيار (ر : المغني والشرح الكبير ٦٩/٤، ۸۰، ۹۰، ۱۱۳، ورد المحتار ٩٦/٤ ، ۱۳۱ ، ۱۱۵۵). حكم الغبن المجرد ومستثنياته ٧/٣٥ - يتضح مما تقدم أن الغبن الفاحش وحده في العقود لا يعيب الرضا ما لم يصاحبه شيء من الخلابة. وعلى هذا استقرت معظم الاجتهادات . ذلك لأنَّ الغبن المجرَّد عن كل خديعة لا يدل إلا على تقصير العاقد المغبون في تحري الأسعار ومعرفة حد البدل العادل؛ ولا يدل على مكر من العاقد الآخر. ولكل إنسان أن يطلب المزيد من المنفعة بالطريق الحر المشروع دون غش واحتيال، ما لم يضر بالجماعة، فحينئذ يحد من حريته وتحدد منفعته كما في حال احتكار المواد الضرورية وحالة تحكم. * (۱) ملاحظة : الخلابة بمعناها الذي تقدم شرحه وبصورها المختلفة يقابلها تماماً في الفقه الأجنبي باللغة الفرنجية لفظ : Le dol) ويترجمها القانونيون العرب في مصر بكلمة (التدليس). والتدليس كلمة استعملها فقهاء الإسلام قديماً في المذاهب الثلاثة المالكي والشافعي، والحنبلي، تارة بهذا المعنى تماماً كما فعل القانونيون المعاصرون، وتارة بمعنى كتمان البائع عيباً خفياً في المبيع، وهو المعنى الأصلي للتدليس. وقد رجحنا هنا استعمال لفظ «الخلابة» لأنه أدل على معنى الخديعة المقصودة هنا، ولأنه ورد كما رأيت في الحديث النبوي الذي احتج به جميع فقهاء المذاهب، وهو النص التشريعي في الموضوع نفسه. فأصبح لفظ التدليس مقصوراً على معناه الأصلي، وهو كتمان عيب المبيع، فيكون تدليس العيب في المبيع ونحوه صورة من صور الخلابة بمعناها العام .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Islamic Jurisprudence (Treachery, Collusive Bidding, Misrepresentation, Concealing Defects). From these and similar forms that we have not quoted and are seen in their jurisprudential sources from the books of the schools, we can extract for khalaba (deception) a general ruling in Islamic law in which we determine the following detail: Indeed, khalaba in all its aforementioned forms, [including] those mentioned, and similar deceptive images and methods that one of the contracting parties resorts to, impair the consent of the other contracting party and affect the binding force of the contract, so by it (the deception) the deceived party is legally granted an option (khiyar) by virtue of which he is given the right to annul the contract in the jurisprudence of the schools. But sometimes the existence of ghubn (disparity) for the deceived party is required along with the deception, and sometimes ghubn is not required, according to the intended purpose of the deception: A) If the goal of the deception is to increase the price for the deceived, then it is required in the deception, in order for it to impair consent and justify annulment of the contract, that it be accompanied by gross ghubn (fahish). That is as in the forms of collusive bidding (tanajush) and verbal misrepresentation about the price. So in all these cases and their likes, the deceiver only intends to increase the consideration. So if they lead to gross ghubn, then they give the deceived the option; otherwise not. Excepted from this is treachery in trust-based sales like murabaha and its sisters, for indeed – even if their goal is to increase the consideration – they give the purchaser in any case the right to annul the contract, or the right to reduce the price by the amount of the treachery (according to differences of ijtihadi opinions on the ruling of treachery as previously explained in its discussion in 2/35), even if the purchaser does not suffer ghubn in the price of the sold item (1). That is because the sale in murabaha and its sisters is based on the capital cost by mutual agreement of the parties. So any lie in the statement of the capital cost leads to a violation of what was agreed upon between the contracting parties, even if there is no ghubn. (1) As if he sold something for ten on the basis that his capital was eight, then it becomes clear that his capital was seven, and the value of the sold item in the market is ten or more, so there is no ghubn on the purchaser in this case despite the seller's treachery, yet the purchaser has the right to annul the contract. B) As for if the goal of the deception is to deceive the deceived party regarding the attribute of the object of contract, like giving him the impression of high quality or any other description, then it impairs consent and justifies annulment of the contract, even if not accompanied by ghubn in the price, as with the tied-up (musarra) ewe if sold at a price not exceeding its value when not lactating, and as with the fruit with a good face if sold at a price not exceeding the price of the bad fruit under its good face, because the deceived party may have intended the description that was falsely implied, so its loss gives him the option. Ruling on pure ghubn and its exceptions: 7/35 – It becomes clear from the foregoing that mere gross ghubn in contracts does not impair consent unless accompanied by something of khalaba. On this most ijtihadat have settled. That is because pure ghubn devoid of any fraud does not indicate anything but negligence of the defrauded party in investigating prices and knowing the fair limit of the consideration; and it does not indicate deceit from the other party. And every person may seek additional benefit by the free and permissible path without cheating or fraud, so long as it does not harm the community; then his freedom is limited and his benefit is limited as in the case of hoarding essential materials and the case of monopoly. * (1) Note: Khalaba in the sense previously explained and in its various forms is exactly equivalent in foreign law in the French language to the word 'Le dol', and Arab jurists in Egypt translate it as 'tadlis'. 'Tadlis' is a word used by early Muslim jurists in the three schools – Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali – sometimes in this exact sense as contemporary jurists have done, and sometimes in the sense of the seller concealing a hidden defect in the sold item, which is the original meaning of tadlis. Here we have preferred the use of the word 'khalaba' because it is more indicative of the meaning of fraud intended here, and because it appears, as you have seen, in the prophetic hadith that all jurists of the schools cite, which is the legislative text on the subject itself. So the word 'tadlis' became restricted to its original meaning, which is concealing a defect in the sold item, so concealing a defect in the sold item and the like becomes a form of khalaba in its general sense.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive ruling on the effect of deception (khalaba) in contracts within Hanafi fiqh as part of the general theory of contracts. The passage type is a legal rule (qawida) with specific applications (furuat). The author first establishes that deception, in all its forms, impairs consent and grants the deceived party the right to rescind the contract. However, the right to rescind is conditional depending on the purpose of the deception. If the deception aims to increase the price (as in collusive bidding or verbal misrepresentation about price), the right to rescind exists only if the deception results in gross disadvantage (ghubn fahish). If there is no gross disadvantage, the contract stands, because the deceived party should have been diligent. An exception is made for trust-based sales (murabaha), where any misrepresentation about the capital cost gives the buyer the right to rescind or reduce the price, even without ghubn, because the contract is built on mutual trust regarding the cost. If the deception aims to deceive about a quality of the object (e.g., selling a fruit with a good face hiding poor quality), the right to rescind exists even without ghubn because the buyer's intention may have been that specific quality. The passage then discusses pure ghubn (disadvantage without deception): it does not affect the contract because it indicates negligence on the buyer's part, not fraud. This is a settled position in most schools. The footnote clarifies terminology: khalaba corresponds to 'dol' in French law, and the author prefers 'khalaba' over 'tadlis' because tadlis is better used for concealment of defects, which is a subset of khalaba.

Key Terms
الخلابة (al-khalaba): Deceptive practices and tricks used by a contracting party to impair the consent of the other party.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fāḥish): Gross disparity or overpayment exceeding what is normally tolerable in such transactions.
بيوع الأمانة (buyu' al-amānah): Trust-based sales such as murabaha where the seller discloses the capital cost and the profit is agreed upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following scenarios does a buyer have the right to rescind a contract even if the price paid was fair market value?
A) The seller engaged in collusive bidding (tanajush) that resulted in a grossly inflated price.
B) The seller lied about the cost price in a murabaha sale, but the actual market value of the item is equal to or higher than the price paid.
C) The seller unknowingly sold an item that later turned out to have a hidden defect, but the buyer paid a fair price.
D) The buyer willingly paid a price that was twice the market value because he liked the item.

Key Takeaway
Deception in contracts impairs consent and gives the right to rescind, but the condition for rescission depends on whether the deception targeted the price (requires gross overpayment) or the quality of the object (no overpayment required), with an exception for trust-based sales.

Exceptions to Ghabn Mujarrad in Hanafi Law
Stating a general rule with three exceptions using a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on ghabn mujarrad to identify when a contract may be rescinded and distinguish the three exceptional cases involving orphan, waqf, and bayt al-mal.

Core Idea
Mere overreaching without fraud does not void a contract, except for orphan, waqf, and public treasury.

This lesson examines a key maxim in Hanafi contract law: pure economic overreaching (ghabn mujarrad) does not invalidate a sale unless it involves one of three specially protected entities. You will learn the general rule, the rationale based on a prophetic hadith, and the limited exceptions where the law intervenes to protect vulnerable interests.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its general ruling
Identify the core principle: ghabn mujarrad without fraud does not affect contract validity. Understand that in normal dealings, the law expects parties to protect themselves.
The maxim is: لا يمنع الغبن المجرد عن الخلابة صحة العقد إلا في حقوق اليتيم والوقف وبيت المال
2
Identify the three exceptions
List the three cases where even pure overreaching invalidates the contract: orphan's property, waqf (endowment), and bayt al-mal (public treasury).
These exceptions are explicitly stated in the text.
3
Understand the rationale for exceptions
These entities need extra protection because their managers may be negligent. The law protects them to prevent exploitation.
The text notes: 'these three entities need additional protection through legislation due to the frequent negligence of those responsible.'
4
Apply the maxim to new cases (furuat)
Given a scenario, determine if the contract can be rescinded. If the overreached party is an orphan, waqf, or bayt al-mal, rescission is allowed. Otherwise, it is not.
Use the maxim as a checklist.
5
Note the istithna' (exclusion) for ordinary individuals
For private individuals without fraud, the general rule applies: no rescission for ghabn mujarrad. The hadith supports non-interference.
This is the default ruling.

(۱) وحجتهم من النصوص قول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: دعوا الناس يرزق الله بعضهم من بعض رواه مسلم في الصحيح في كتاب البيوع (۱۵۲۲) والترمذي (۱۲۲۳) والنسائي (٤٤٩٦) من حديث جابر رضي الله عنه. الله عنه وفي المذهب الحنفي رأيان حول جواز فسخ . العقد بالغبن المجرد، والأرجح عدم الجواز ما لم يصحبه تغرير (فتح القدير ١٠٧/٦ ورد المحتار (١٥٩/٤) .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. The sellers at prices, where the ruler sells monopolized property against its owner's will, and resorts to price-fixing on sellers if they collude and control prices. As for ordinary dealings among people outside cases of monopoly and control, it is not the task of legislation to prevent mere overreaching (taghabun) free from fraud and deceit; rather, its task is to place the contracting parties on equal footing in capacity and freedom. Then every person must open his eyes and protect himself from overreaching. Every person, due to his concern for his own interest, has a sufficient motive to seek what is best for him and avoid what is not. The consequence of negligence must be borne by the negligent. This is the position of the majority of Islamic jurisprudential opinions concerning mere overreaching. Note the following exceptions: 8/35 - The exceptional cases in which mere overreaching is considered a defect in the will: Hanafi jurisprudence and others except three situations in which egregious overreaching is prevented even if it is mere overreaching unaccompanied by deception. These three excepted situations are: the rights of the orphan, the endowment (waqf), and the public treasury (bayt al-mal). Any overreaching that occurs through a contract involving the property of any of these three entities is legally voided against the overreacher, because these three entities need additional protection through legislation due to the frequent negligence of those responsible for safeguarding their rights (see: Al-Majalla, Article 356). (1) Their evidence from texts is the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'Leave people, God provides for some of them through others.' Reported by Muslim in Sahih, Book of Sales (1522), al-Tirmidhi (1223), and al-Nasa'i (4496) from the hadith of Jabir, may God be pleased with him. In the Hanafi school, there are two opinions regarding the permissibility of rescinding a contract due to mere overreaching; the preponderant view is that it is not permissible unless accompanied by deception (Fath al-Qadir 6/107, Radd al-Muhtar 4/159).
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental principle in Hanafi contract law: mere overreaching (ghabn mujarrad) without fraud does not invalidate a contract. The author explains that in normal market conditions, the law does not protect a party from a bad bargain; individuals are expected to exercise due diligence. The justification is a hadith where the Prophet instructed to let people trade freely. However, there are three exceptions where even pure overreaching is considered a defect in consent and gives the right to rescind: contracts involving the property of an orphan, a waqf (endowment), or the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The reason is that these entities are particularly vulnerable to negligence by their managers, so the law provides extra protection. The footnote clarifies that the dominant Hanafi position is that mere ghabn does not allow rescission unless accompanied by deception (taghrir). The exceptions for orphan, waqf, and bayt al-mal are thus special safeguards, not general rules.

Key Terms
الغبن المجرد (al-ghabn al-mujarrad): Pure economic overreaching or disparity in value without any element of fraud, deceit, or misrepresentation
الخلابة (al-khilaba): Fraud or deception that accompanies a transaction, affecting consent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when does mere ghabn (overreaching) without fraud allow a contract to be rescinded?
A) In all cases where the overreaching is gross
B) Only when the overreached party is a merchant
C) Only in contracts involving orphan, waqf, or bayt al-mal property
D) Only when the overreaching is accompanied by deception

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mere overreaching does not invalidate a contract unless the transaction involves the rights of an orphan, a waqf, or the public treasury.

The Mustarsil: Definition and Inter-Madhhab Ruling
Defining the mustarsil and its legal effect with inter-madhhab comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the mustarsil according to Hanbali and Maliki schools, state the hadith evidence, and apply the ruling to identify when an overreached party has the option to annul.

Core Idea
A mustarsil overreached has the option to annul in Hanbali and Maliki, but the definition of 'mustarsil' differs between schools.

The Hanbali school recognizes exceptions to the principle that mere overreaching does not invalidate a contract. One such exception is the mustarsil, a person who submits to the other party in ignorance of the price. This lesson unpacks the definition of mustarsil across madhhabs, the evidence from hadith, and the practical implications for the right to rescind.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanbali rule
Identify the Hanbali position: the mustarsil has khiyar al-ibtal if ghabn occurs, even without deception.
2
Define the mustarsil
Note the Hanbali definition: ignorant of price, submissive, not haggling. Compare with Maliki definition (explicit declaration).
3
Examine the dalil
Review the hadith 'Ghabn al-mustarsil haram' and its chain weakness.
The hadith is weak, which may affect its evidentiary weight.
4
Compare Maliki definition
Maliki requires explicit declaration of ignorance and request for market price; this moves the case closer to taghrir.
5
Contrast with Hanafi position
Hanafi generally does not grant option for mere ghabn; exceptions exist only for orphan, waqf, bayt al-mal.

(۲) للمالكية مباحث مسهبة في غبن المسترسل وفقهاء المذهب المالكي مجمعون على أن غبنه إذا كان فاحشاً فإنه يعيب إرادته ويوجب له خيار الإبطال. • لكنهم يفسرون المسترسل بأنه : هو الذي يستسلم من المتبايعين إلى الآخر ويستأمنه في السعر بأن يعلن له أنه جاهل بالأسعار، ويطلب إليه أن يبيعه أو يشتري منه بسعر السوق. فإذا غبنه كان له الخيار . أما إذا كان الشخص جاهلاً للأسعار لكنه غير مسترسل أي) لم يعلن لصاحبه ذلك) فَغَبَنَهُ، ففي منح المغبون الخيار اختلاف بين فقهاء المذهب. وأما إذا كان عالماً بأسعار السوق فلا خيار له إذا غُبن باتفاق الفقهاء (ر: الشرح الصغير للدردير المالكي في هامش الصاوي (٦٣/٢). وفقهاء الحنابلة لا يفسرون المسترسل بمن يعلن للآخر جهله بالأسعار واستثمانه له، بل يكتفون في تحقق معنى الاسترسال بأن يكون جاهلاً مستسلماً لا يماكس. والواقع أن اشتراط المالكية فيه أن يعلن استثمانه إلى البائع في سعر السوق يلحق الموضوع يبحث التغرير من صور الخلابة، لأنه غرره بقبول مبايعته على أساس سعر السوق حتى اطمأن إليه، ثم اتخذ من ذلك ذريعة إلى غبنه. وهذا هو التغرير القولي في السعر . (۳) رواه الطبراني في الكبير :٨ ١٢٦ (٧٥٧٦) عن أبي أمامة الباهلي، وفيه موسى بن الأعمى وهو ضعيف جداً. وله لفظ آخر عند البيهقي :٥: ٣٤٨: «من استرسل عمیر إلى مؤمن فغَبَنّه كان غبنه ذلك ربا وفيه الرجل نفسه ثم أسنده البيهقي عن جابر وأنس بلفظ : «غين المسترسل ربا) وفيه يعيش بن هشام القرقيسياني. قال البيهقي : هو أضعف من موسى بن عمير. ٤٧١
However, Hanbali jurisprudence also excepts two situations in which mere overreaching free from deception is considered a defect in the consent of the overreached party, allowing rescission of the contract. They are: 1. The case of the mustarsil (naive person). 2. Talaqqi al-rukban (intercepting caravans). (1) As for the mustarsil, he is the one who surrenders himself, as buyer or seller, to the other party while being ignorant of the price. If the other party overreaches him in the price, the mustarsil has the option of annulment (2), due to the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'Overreaching the mustarsil is forbidden (haram)' (3). If he was aware of the price or acted hastily and thus was ignorant where if he had deliberated he would have known, then... (1) Al-rukban (with damma on ra) is the plural of raakib (rider), one who rides a camel. (2) The Malikis have extensive discussions on overreaching the mustarsil. The jurists of the Maliki school are unanimous that if the overreaching is gross, it vitiates his intent and gives him the option of annulment. However, they interpret the mustarsil as: the one who surrenders himself from the two contracting parties to the other and entrusts him regarding the price by declaring his ignorance of prices and asking him to buy or sell at the market price. If he is overreached, he has the option. But if the person is ignorant of prices but is not mustarsil (i.e., he did not declare that to his counterpart), and is overreached, there is disagreement among Maliki jurists on granting the option. If he is aware of market prices, he has no option if overreached, by agreement of the jurists (see: al-Sharh al-Saghir lil-Dardir al-Maliki in the margin of al-Sawi, 2/63). The Hanbali jurists do not interpret the mustarsil as one who declares his ignorance and seeks assurance; rather, they suffice for the realization of the meaning of istirsal that he be ignorant and submissive, not haggling. Indeed, the Maliki condition that he declare his seeking of assurance to the seller regarding the market price attaches the subject to the discussion of taghrir (deception) as a form of khilaba, because he deceived him by accepting his transaction on the basis of the market price until he felt secure, then used that as a means to overreach him. This is verbal deception in price. (3) Reported by al-Tabarani in al-Kabir 8:126 (7576) from Abu Umamah al-Bahili; its chain includes Musa ibn al-A'mash, who is very weak. Another wording from al-Bayhaqi 5:348: 'Whoever a believer entrusts him and he overreaches him, his overreaching is usury (riba).' It includes the same man. Then al-Bayhaqi narrated from Jabir and Anas with the wording: 'Overreaching the mustarsil is usury,' which includes Ya'ish ibn Hisham al-Qarqisiyani. Al-Bayhaqi said: He is weaker than Musa ibn Umair.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanbali exception to the general rule that mere ghabn does not invalidate contracts. The Hanbali school recognizes two specific situations where even pure overreaching (without fraud) gives the overreached party a right to rescind: (1) the mustarsil (a person who is ignorant of prices and relies on the other party), and (2) talaqqi al-rukban (intercepting caravans before they reach market). The text focuses on the mustarsil. It defines a mustarsil as someone who submits to the other party in a sale, being unaware of the current price. If the other party overreaches, the mustarsil has the option to annul the contract (khiyar al-ibtal). The evidence is a hadith: 'Overreaching the mustarsil is haram.' The author then compares the Maliki and Hanbali interpretations of 'mustarsil'. In the Maliki school, the mustarsil must explicitly declare his ignorance and ask the seller to transact at the market price. If he does not declare it, even if ignorant, he is not considered mustarsil and may not have the option. In the Hanbali school, it suffices that he is ignorant and does not haggle; explicit declaration is not required. The author notes that the Maliki condition actually brings the case under taghrir (verbal deception), because the seller assures the buyer that he is transacting at market price, then overreaches. The footnote discusses the weakness of the hadith's chain. Overall, the passage highlights how different madhhabs define the same concept and the legal consequences.

Key Terms
المسترسل (al-mustarsil): A person who submits to the other party in a transaction, being ignorant of the market price and not haggling
خيار الإبطال (khiyar al-ibtal): The option to annul the contract unilaterally

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali school, what is the minimum requirement for a person to be considered mustarsil such that overreaching gives him the option to annul?
A) He must explicitly declare his ignorance and ask to be charged the market price
B) He must be ignorant of the price and not haggle
C) He must be a traveler arriving from outside the city
D) He must have a prior relationship of trust with the seller

Key Takeaway
The definition of mustarsil varies: Hanbali requires ignorance and submission without haggling, Maliki adds explicit declaration, while Hanafi does not grant a specific right.

Shart al-Dalala: Implicit Conditions by Context and Custom
Explains implicit conditions via Hanafi and Maliki examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a condition is implicitly indicated by context or custom (dalala) and apply the Hanafi ruling that such conditions are binding.

Core Idea
Conditions implied by context and custom are as binding as explicit stipulations in Hanafi law.

In contract law, not all conditions need to be spelled out. Hanafi and Maliki jurists recognize that the surrounding circumstances and common practice can make a condition 'implicitly stipulated.' This lesson examines how judges and muftis identify such conditions and the legal effect when they are violated.

Guided Path
1
State the legal maxim
The rule: Conditions implied by context or custom (dalala) are binding even if not explicitly stated. In Arabic: ما دل عليه السياق أو العرف في العقود يعتبر شرطاً.
2
Identify the scope of the maxim
This maxim applies broadly to commutative contracts (عقود المعاوضة), especially sales. It covers qualities that are commonly known or contextually expected.
3
Work through the Hanafi example
Analyze the slave example: a buyer purchases a slave famous for a skill (e.g., writing, baking) without explicit stipulation. If the skill is missing, the buyer may rescind because the custom implies that skill was a condition.
The key is that the skill is 'known' (مشهور) – public knowledge creates the implication.
4
Work through the Maliki example
Analyze the jewelry market example: selling a stone in the jewelry market implies it is a jewel. If it is not, the buyer has the right to rescind (qiyam). But if sold outside that market, no implication arises.
The location of the sale provides context that gives rise to the implied condition.
5
Note the istithnā' (exception)
The maxim does not apply when the context or custom does not clearly indicate the condition. For example, selling a stone outside the jewelry market does not imply it is a jewel. Also, the condition must be based on common knowledge or market practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥/٣٦ - (الحالة الثانية : كون مراد العاقد واضحاً من الدلائل : وأما حالة كون مراد العاقد الغالط مكشوفاً واضحاً بطريق الدلالة دون تصريح منه فأمثلته كثيرة في فقه المذاهب . ففقهاء المذهب الحنفي يذكرون أن من اشترى عبداً مشهوراً بأنه يحسن الكتابة أو الخبز فوجده ناسياً ذلك، أو اشترى جارية كانت معروفة بأنها تحسن الطبخ أو الخياطة فوجدها ناسية ذلك، فإنّ للمشتري فسخ البيع ولو لم يشترط هذا الوصف فيهما عند العقد صراحة، لأن الظاهر أنه إنما اشترى رغبة في الصفة، فصارت مشروطة بطريق الدلالة ( ر ) رد المحتار، الجزء الرابع أواخر خيار الشرط، والبحر الرائق لابن نجيم ٣٦/٦) . وفقهاء المذهب المالكي يصرحون بأنه إذا باع الشخص حجراً في سوق الجواهر دل ذلك على أنه إنما يبيع جوهرة، وإن لم يصرح بجنس الحجر في العقد، فإذا تبين أنه ليس بجوهر كان للمبتاع القيام . وإن باعه في غير سوق الجوهر لم يكن له قيام ( ر ) شرح الحطاب على المختصر .(ETV _ ETT/E) ومما يعتبر من الأوصاف مشروطاً دلالة ولو لم يصرح به في العقد سلامة المعقود عليه من العيوب في عقود المعاوضة. فمن حق المشتري أو (۱) القيام في اصطلاح المالكية في مثل هذا المقام معناه حق العاقد في الادعاء والرجوع على المتعاقد معه.
5/36 – (Second case: When the intent of the contracting party is clear from indications): As for the case where the intent of the mistaken contracting party is disclosed and clear by way of indication without explicit statement, examples of it are numerous in the fiqh of the schools. Hanafi jurists mention that one who buys a slave famous for being skilled in writing or baking, and finds him forgetful of that, or buys a female slave known for being skilled in cooking or sewing, and finds her forgetful of that, then the buyer has the right to rescind the sale even if this description was not explicitly stipulated in the contract, because the apparent situation is that he only bought out of desire for the quality, so it becomes stipulated by way of indication (refer to Radd al-Muhtar, vol. 4, end of Khiyar al-Shart; and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq by Ibn Nujaym, 6/36). And Maliki jurists state that if a person sells a stone in the jewelry market, that indicates that he is only selling a jewel, even if he did not specify the type of stone in the contract. So if it becomes clear that it is not a jewel, the buyer has the right of qiyam. But if he sells it outside the jewelry market, he has no right of qiyam (refer to Sharh al-Hattab 'ala al-Mukhtasar). And among the descriptions considered to be conditionally implied even if not explicitly stated in the contract is the freedom of the subject matter from defects in commutative contracts. So the buyer has the right... (1) Qiyam in Maliki terminology in this context means the right of the contracting party to claim and recourse against the other contracting party.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents the second case of mistake in the context of defects of consent: when the mistaken party's intention is apparent from circumstantial evidence (dalala) rather than explicit wording. The author gives two examples. In the Hanafi school, if a person buys a slave or maid known for a particular skill (writing, baking, cooking, sewing) but the slave turns out to have lost that skill (e.g., forgetfulness), the buyer may rescind the sale even if the skill was not stipulated explicitly, because the buyer's desire for that skill is evident from the context. This is called a condition by indication (shart bil-dalala). In the Maliki school, if someone sells a stone in the jewelry market, the context indicates he is selling a jewel; if it turns out not to be a jewel, the buyer has the right to rescind (qiyam). But if sold elsewhere, the buyer has no such right. The passage concludes by noting that freedom from defects is always implicitly a condition in commutative contracts, even if not mentioned.

Key Terms
دلالة (Dalala): Implicit indication from context or custom that a condition is intended.
القيام (al-Qiyam): In Maliki terminology, the right to claim and rescind the contract due to deception or mistake.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man buys a stone in the jewelry market without specifying its type. It turns out to be a common stone. According to the Maliki view mentioned, what is the buyer's right?
A) He has no right because he did not specify.
B) He has the right to rescind (قيام) because the context indicated it was a jewel.
C) He can only get a price reduction.
D) He must accept the stone without recourse.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditions implied by context and custom are as binding as explicit stipulations, granting the right to rescind if the condition is breached.

The Rule: Description in Present is Irrelevant
Legal maxim with furu' and istithna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-wasf fi al-hadhir laghw' to distinguish when a buyer can or cannot rescind a contract due to description mismatch.

Core Idea
Pointing to a present object overrides any verbal description.

When you buy a specific item after seeing it, your consent is based on the actual object you pointed to, not on what the seller might have described it as. This lesson explains the legal maxim 'description in a present object is irrelevant' and its application in Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the qawida: 'al-wasf fi al-hadhir laghw wa fi al-gha'ib mu'tabar' from the source passage.
This sets the foundational principle.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is pointing stronger than verbal description? Because pointing directly identifies the specific object, while words can be ambiguous.
Pointing is the strongest method of identification.
3
Apply the rule to the present object
If the buyer sees and points to an item, any mismatch between verbal description and reality does not give the buyer a right to rescind.
The contract is binding on the buyer.
4
Distinguish the absent object
If the object is absent, description becomes decisive; mismatch gives the buyer an option to rescind.
This is the exception (istithna') to the rule.
5
Identify practical furu'
Work through examples: buying a specific sheep vs. buying a described sheep not yet seen.
The ruling changes based on presence/absence.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
انعقد العقد لازماً للمشتري لا خيار له في إبطاله، لأنه غير معذور بهذا الغلط بعد المشاهدة والإشارة واختلاف الوصف المسمى عن الواقع لا عبرة له في هذه الحال، لأنَّ الإشارة هنا في الشيء الحاضر أبلغ طرق التعريف وأقواها، فإذا اجتمع معها وخالفها ما هو دونها تعريفاً وهو الوصف الكلامي فالعبرة للإشارة. ويقرّر الفقهاء في هذا المقام قاعدة هي : أنَّ الوصف في الحاضر لغو، وفي الغائب معتبر (المجلة (٦٥) .

٥/٣٦ - (الحالة الثانية : كون مراد العاقد واضحاً من الدلائل : وأما حالة كون مراد العاقد الغالط مكشوفاً واضحاً بطريق الدلالة دون تصريح منه فأمثلته كثيرة في فقه المذاهب .

ففقهاء المذهب الحنفي يذكرون أن من اشترى عبداً مشهوراً بأنه يحسن الكتابة أو الخبز فوجده ناسياً ذلك، أو اشترى جارية كانت معروفة بأنها تحسن الطبخ أو الخياطة فوجدها ناسية ذلك، فإنّ للمشتري فسخ البيع ولو لم يشترط هذا الوصف فيهما عند العقد ،صراحة، لأن الظاهر أنه إنما اشترى رغبة في الصفة، فصارت مشروطة بطريق الدلالة ( ر ) رد المحتار، الجزء الرابع أواخر خيار الشرط، والبحر الرائق لابن نجيم ٣٦/٦) .

وفقهاء المذهب المالكي يصرحون بأنه إذا باع الشخص حجراً في سوق الجواهر دل ذلك على أنه إنما يبيع جوهرة، وإن لم يصرح بجنس الحجر في العقد، فإذا تبين أنه ليس بجوهر كان للمبتاع القيام . وإن باعه في غير سوق الجوهر لم يكن له قيام ( ر ) شرح الحطاب على المختصر .

-
ومما يعتبر من الأوصاف مشروطاً دلالة ولو لم يصرح به في العقد
سلامة المعقود عليه من العيوب في عقود المعاوضة. فمن حق المشتري أو المستأجر مثلاً أن يحسب المبيع أو المأجور خالياً من العيوب؛ ومن واجب البائع أو المؤجر معرفة ذلك وتقديره. فإذا ظهر في المبيع أو المأجور عيب كان المشتري أو المستأجر غالطاً ومعذوراً في غلطه فله الرد. ولا يكون في ذلك مفاجأة للبائع أو للمؤجر، لأنَّ عليهما أن يتوقعا ذلك(1) .

هذه إجمالاً خلاصة الحالات التي يعتبر فيها غلط العاقد من شوائب الإرادة العقدية، لأنَّ غلطه واضح من حيث إن العاقد قد كشف عن إرادته بنفسه صراحة أو أن إرادته مكشوفة بطريق الدلالة. فإذا خالف الواقع مراده المكشوف كان معذوراً في غلطه الذي عاب ،رضاه، لوضوح الغلط بالنسبة إلى العاقد الآخر، فلا يكون فسخ العقد مفاجأة له مخلة باستقرار التعامل.

(1) يقول الأستاذ السنهوري في كتابه الجديد مصادر الحق في الفقه الإسلامي ١٣٠/٢ الطبعة الأولى : سلامة الشيء من العيوب شرط ضمني في العقد تقتضيه طبائع الأشياء. فإذا ظهر عيب في الشيء تبيَّن أن المشتري كان واهماً عندما اعتقد أن الشيء سليم من العيب. ومن هنا كان خيار العيب متصلاً أوثق الاتصال بنظرية الغلط، بل هو ليس إلا صورة من صوره المتعددة .

ويقول الإمام الكاساني من فقهاء الحنفية في كتابه «البدائع في بحث خيار العيب (٥/ ٢٧٤) : ... السلامة مشروطة في العقد دلالة فكانت كالمشروطة نصاً، فإذا فاتت المساواة كان له الخيار . لأن البيع عقد معاوضة والمعاوضات مبناها على المساواة عادة وحقيقة . . . ولأن السلامة لما كانت مرغوب المشتري ولم يحصل فقد اختل رضاه. وهذا يوجب الخيار لأنّ الرضا شرط صحة البيع ... فانعدام الرضا يمنع صحة البيع؛ واختلاله يوجب الخيار فيه إثباتاً للحكم على قدر الدليل».

وهذا البيان من صاحب البدائع كما ترى صريح في أن حكم خيار العيب في الفقه الإسلامي مبني على أساس أن رضا المشتري قد اختل بظهور العيب في المبيع، فهو من قبيل شوائب الإرادة المسماة عيوب الرضا.

وقد قدمنا ف (٥/٣٥ أن تدليس العيب يدخل في صور الخلابة . ونضيف هنا أنَّ العيب الذي لم يدلسه البائع تدليساً على المشتري بل كان يجهله البائع نفسه يمكن عده من قبيل الغلط وسنرى أيضاً أنه يمكن عده من صور اختلال التنفيذ الذي هو العيب الرابع الأخير من عيوب الرضا.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. The contract becomes binding on the buyer with no option to annul it, because he is not excused for this error after seeing and pointing, and the difference between the named description and reality is disregarded in this case, because pointing here at the present object is the most effective and strongest method of identification. So if it coincides with and contradicts what is weaker in identification, namely verbal description, then consideration is given to the pointing. The jurists establish in this context a rule: Description in a present object is irrelevant, while in an absent object it is considered (Majalla Article 65). 5/36 - (Second Situation: The Intention of the Contracting Party Being Clear from Indications: As for the situation where the intention of the mistaken party is revealed and clear by way of indication without explicit statement, its examples are many in the fiqh of the schools. The Hanafi jurists mention that if someone buys a slave famous for knowing writing or baking and finds him forgetting that, or buys a slave girl known for cooking or sewing and finds her forgetting that, then the buyer has the right to rescind the sale even if he did not explicitly stipulate this description at the time of the contract, because it is apparent that he only bought desiring the attribute, so it becomes stipulated by way of indication (Radd al-Muhtar, vol. 4, end of Khiyar al-Shart, and al-Bahr al-Rai'q by Ibn Nujaym 6/36). And the Maliki jurists state that if a person sells a stone in the jewel market, that indicates that he is only selling a jewel, even if he did not specify the type of stone in the contract; so if it becomes clear that it is not a jewel, the buyer has the right to 'qiyam' (claim). But if he sold it outside the jewel market, he has no such right (Sharh al-Hattab 'ala al-Mukhtasar). And among the descriptions considered as stipulated by indication even if not explicitly stated in the contract is the soundness of the subject matter from defects in commutative contracts. So it is the right of the buyer or lessee, for example, to consider the sold or leased item free of defects; and it is the duty of the seller or lessor to know that and take it into account. So if a defect appears in the sold or leased item, the buyer or lessee is mistaken and excused for his mistake, so he has the right of return. And that is not a surprise to the seller or lessor, because both must expect it. This, in summary, is the essence of the situations in which the mistake of the contracting party is considered among the defects of contractual will, because his mistake is clear in that the party has revealed his intention by himself explicitly, or his intention is revealed by way of indication. So if reality contradicts his revealed intention, he is excused for his mistake that tainted his consent, due to the clarity of the mistake relative to the other party, so rescission of the contract is not a surprise to him disrupting the stability of transactions. (1) Professor Sanhuri says in his new book 'Sources of Right in Islamic Fiqh' (2/130, first edition): 'Soundness of the object from defects is an implied condition in the contract required by the nature of things. So if a defect appears in the object, it becomes clear that the buyer was mistaken when he believed the object to be free of defect. Hence the option of defect is most strongly connected to the theory of error; indeed it is but one of its multiple forms.' And Imam al-Kasani, a Hanafi jurist, says in his book 'al-Bada'i' in the discussion of khiyar al-'ayb (5/274): 'Soundness is stipulated in the contract by implication, so it is like being stipulated explicitly. So if equivalence is lost, he has the option. Because sale is a commutative contract, and commutative contracts are based on equivalence by custom and reality. And because soundness was the desire of the buyer and was not achieved, so his consent was impaired. And this necessitates the option because consent is a condition for the validity of the sale. So absence of consent prevents the validity of the sale; and its impairment necessitates the option in it, establishing the ruling to the extent of the evidence.' And this statement from the author of al-Bada'i', as you see, is explicit that the ruling of khiyar al-'ayb in Islamic fiqh is built on the basis that the buyer's consent was impaired by the appearance of the defect in the sold item, so it is among the defects of the will called 'defects of consent'. We have already presented in 5/35 that fraudulent concealment of a defect falls under forms of deception. And we add here that a defect which the seller did not conceal fraudulently from the buyer but rather the seller himself was ignorant of it can be counted as a type of error, and we will see also that it can be counted as a form of defective performance, which is the fourth and last defect of consent.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage discusses error (ghalat) in contracts from the perspective of Islamic jurisprudence, specifically focusing on when a mistaken party is excused. It begins with a rule from the Majalla: in a present object, verbal description is irrelevant if there is a contradiction between description and actual viewing/pointing, because the act of pointing to the specific object is the strongest means of identification. This is a qawida kulliya (major rule) applied in sales of specific goods.

The text then moves to the second situation: where the mistaken party's intention is clear through circumstantial indications (dalala) without explicit stipulation. Examples from the Hanafi school: buying a slave known for a skill who turns out to have forgotten it; here the buyer can rescind because the skill is considered an implied condition (shart bi al-dalala). The Maliki school gives the example of selling a stone in the jewel market — the place of sale indicates it is a jewel, so if it is not, the buyer has recourse.

Finally, the passage addresses the implied condition of freedom from defects in commutative contracts (like sale and lease). The buyer/lessee assumes the item is defect-free; if a defect appears, they are mistaken and excused, having the right to return the item (khiyar al-'ayb). This is linked to the theory of error because the buyer's consent was based on a mistaken belief. Al-Kasani's quote from al-Bada'i' emphasizes that soundness is an implied condition; its absence impairs consent, giving the option of defect. The passage concludes by noting that khiyar al-'ayb is a form of error, whether the defect was concealed or unknown to the seller. All these cases share the characteristic that the error is clear to the other party, so rescission does not disrupt transactions.

Key Terms
الوصف في الحاضر لغو (al-wasf fi al-hadhir laghw): Verbal description of a present, seen object is legally irrelevant if it contradicts what is seen by pointing.
الإشارة (al-ishara): Pointing to the specific object as the method of identification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule, if a buyer sees a specific item and points to it, but the seller describes it differently, what is the effect on the contract?
A) The buyer can rescind the contract because the description was wrong.
B) The contract is binding and the buyer has no option, because the pointing overrides the verbal description.
C) The seller must reduce the price.
D) The contract is void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
In a present object, pointing overrides verbal description; the buyer is bound by what they see.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
8 min
Implied Conditions by Indication (Dalala)
Analysis of implied conditions through contextual indications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa applying the Hanafi concept of 'shart bi al-dalala' (implied condition by indication) to cases where a buyer's intention is clear from custom or context.

Core Idea
Custom and context can create binding implied conditions without explicit words.

Not every contract term needs to be spoken. Sometimes the buyer's intention is so clear from circumstances — like buying a slave known for a skill — that the law treats it as an implied condition. This lesson explores how Hanafi and Maliki jurists identify such conditions through 'dalala' (indication) and when they allow rescission.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of dalala
Dalala means an indication from context, custom, or conduct that reveals the party's intention without explicit words. It is recognized as a source of implied conditions in Hanafi law.
It is based on the maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative).
2
Examine the Hanafi examples
Review the examples of buying a slave skilled in writing/baking or a slave girl skilled in cooking/sewing. The skill is a known attribute; if it is missing, the buyer can rescind because the attribute was desired and thus implied by dalala.
The source references Radd al-Muhtar and al-Bahr al-Rai'q.
3
Consider the Maliki example
Selling a stone in a jewel market implies it is a jewel; otherwise the buyer has recourse. This shows how context (place of sale) creates an implied condition.
The location itself becomes an indication of quality.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Opposing schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require explicit stipulation for such options. The practical difference: Hanafi buyer can rescind even without explicit condition; Shafi'i buyer cannot.
This is a key ikhtilaf in options law.
5
Apply to modern transactions
Consider how market reputation or advertising might create implied conditions: buying a 'certified organic' product implies it meets organic standards.
Ensure the indication is clear and known to both parties.

Source Text

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
انعقد العقد لازماً للمشتري لا خيار له في إبطاله، لأنه غير معذور بهذا الغلط بعد المشاهدة والإشارة واختلاف الوصف المسمى عن الواقع لا عبرة له في هذه الحال، لأنَّ الإشارة هنا في الشيء الحاضر أبلغ طرق التعريف وأقواها، فإذا اجتمع معها وخالفها ما هو دونها تعريفاً وهو الوصف الكلامي فالعبرة للإشارة. ويقرّر الفقهاء في هذا المقام قاعدة هي : أنَّ الوصف في الحاضر لغو، وفي الغائب معتبر (المجلة (٦٥) .

٥/٣٦ - (الحالة الثانية : كون مراد العاقد واضحاً من الدلائل : وأما حالة كون مراد العاقد الغالط مكشوفاً واضحاً بطريق الدلالة دون تصريح منه فأمثلته كثيرة في فقه المذاهب .

ففقهاء المذهب الحنفي يذكرون أن من اشترى عبداً مشهوراً بأنه يحسن الكتابة أو الخبز فوجده ناسياً ذلك، أو اشترى جارية كانت معروفة بأنها تحسن الطبخ أو الخياطة فوجدها ناسية ذلك، فإنّ للمشتري فسخ البيع ولو لم يشترط هذا الوصف فيهما عند العقد ،صراحة، لأن الظاهر أنه إنما اشترى رغبة في الصفة، فصارت مشروطة بطريق الدلالة ( ر ) رد المحتار، الجزء الرابع أواخر خيار الشرط، والبحر الرائق لابن نجيم ٣٦/٦) .

وفقهاء المذهب المالكي يصرحون بأنه إذا باع الشخص حجراً في سوق الجواهر دل ذلك على أنه إنما يبيع جوهرة، وإن لم يصرح بجنس الحجر في العقد، فإذا تبين أنه ليس بجوهر كان للمبتاع القيام . وإن باعه في غير سوق الجوهر لم يكن له قيام ( ر ) شرح الحطاب على المختصر .

-
ومما يعتبر من الأوصاف مشروطاً دلالة ولو لم يصرح به في العقد
سلامة المعقود عليه من العيوب في عقود المعاوضة. فمن حق المشتري أو المستأجر مثلاً أن يحسب المبيع أو المأجور خالياً من العيوب؛ ومن واجب البائع أو المؤجر معرفة ذلك وتقديره. فإذا ظهر في المبيع أو المأجور عيب كان المشتري أو المستأجر غالطاً ومعذوراً في غلطه فله الرد. ولا يكون في ذلك مفاجأة للبائع أو للمؤجر، لأنَّ عليهما أن يتوقعا ذلك(1) .

هذه إجمالاً خلاصة الحالات التي يعتبر فيها غلط العاقد من شوائب الإرادة العقدية، لأنَّ غلطه واضح من حيث إن العاقد قد كشف عن إرادته بنفسه صراحة أو أن إرادته مكشوفة بطريق الدلالة. فإذا خالف الواقع مراده المكشوف كان معذوراً في غلطه الذي عاب ،رضاه، لوضوح الغلط بالنسبة إلى العاقد الآخر، فلا يكون فسخ العقد مفاجأة له مخلة باستقرار التعامل.

(1) يقول الأستاذ السنهوري في كتابه الجديد مصادر الحق في الفقه الإسلامي ١٣٠/٢ الطبعة الأولى : سلامة الشيء من العيوب شرط ضمني في العقد تقتضيه طبائع الأشياء. فإذا ظهر عيب في الشيء تبيَّن أن المشتري كان واهماً عندما اعتقد أن الشيء سليم من العيب. ومن هنا كان خيار العيب متصلاً أوثق الاتصال بنظرية الغلط، بل هو ليس إلا صورة من صوره المتعددة .

ويقول الإمام الكاساني من فقهاء الحنفية في كتابه «البدائع في بحث خيار العيب (٥/ ٢٧٤) : ... السلامة مشروطة في العقد دلالة فكانت كالمشروطة نصاً، فإذا فاتت المساواة كان له الخيار . لأن البيع عقد معاوضة والمعاوضات مبناها على المساواة عادة وحقيقة . . . ولأن السلامة لما كانت مرغوب المشتري ولم يحصل فقد اختل رضاه. وهذا يوجب الخيار لأنّ الرضا شرط صحة البيع ... فانعدام الرضا يمنع صحة البيع؛ واختلاله يوجب الخيار فيه إثباتاً للحكم على قدر الدليل».

وهذا البيان من صاحب البدائع كما ترى صريح في أن حكم خيار العيب في الفقه الإسلامي مبني على أساس أن رضا المشتري قد اختل بظهور العيب في المبيع، فهو من قبيل شوائب الإرادة المسماة عيوب الرضا.

وقد قدمنا ف (٥/٣٥ أن تدليس العيب يدخل في صور الخلابة . ونضيف هنا أنَّ العيب الذي لم يدلسه البائع تدليساً على المشتري بل كان يجهله البائع نفسه يمكن عده من قبيل الغلط وسنرى أيضاً أنه يمكن عده من صور اختلال التنفيذ الذي هو العيب الرابع الأخير من عيوب الرضا.

Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage discusses error (ghalat) in contracts from the perspective of Islamic jurisprudence, specifically focusing on when a mistaken party is excused. It begins with a rule from the Majalla: in a present object, verbal description is irrelevant if there is a contradiction between description and actual viewing/pointing, because the act of pointing to the specific object is the strongest means of identification. This is a qawida kulliya (major rule) applied in sales of specific goods.

The text then moves to the second situation: where the mistaken party's intention is clear through circumstantial indications (dalala) without explicit stipulation. Examples from the Hanafi school: buying a slave known for a skill who turns out to have forgotten it; here the buyer can rescind because the skill is considered an implied condition (shart bi al-dalala). The Maliki school gives the example of selling a stone in the jewel market — the place of sale indicates it is a jewel, so if it is not, the buyer has recourse.

Finally, the passage addresses the implied condition of freedom from defects in commutative contracts (like sale and lease). The buyer/lessee assumes the item is defect-free; if a defect appears, they are mistaken and excused, having the right to return the item (khiyar al-'ayb). This is linked to the theory of error because the buyer's consent was based on a mistaken belief. Al-Kasani's quote from al-Bada'i' emphasizes that soundness is an implied condition; its absence impairs consent, giving the option of defect. The passage concludes by noting that khiyar al-'ayb is a form of error, whether the defect was concealed or unknown to the seller. All these cases share the characteristic that the error is clear to the other party, so rescission does not disrupt transactions.

Key Terms
بطريق الدلالة (bi-tariq al-dalala): An implicit indication from context, custom, or conduct that reveals the party's intention without explicit words.
الظاهر (al-zahir): What is apparent from the circumstances; here, it is used to indicate that the buyer's desire for the attribute is evident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi example, if someone buys a slave known for baking and finds he has forgotten how, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void because the slave is defective.
B) The buyer can rescind because the skill was an implied condition by indication.
C) The buyer cannot rescind because he did not stipulate the skill explicitly.
D) The seller must train the slave again.

Key Takeaway
Known attributes desired by the buyer become implied conditions through dalala, entitling the buyer to rescind if those attributes are absent.

Khiyar al-'Ayb as a Form of Error
Doctrinal analysis of khiyar al-'ayb as an instance of error

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) is rooted in the theory of error (ghalat) and apply its ruling in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-'ayb is an error-based remedy because the buyer's consent was impaired by a mistaken belief.

When you buy something and later discover a hidden defect, your consent was based on a false assumption. This lesson shows how classical Hanafi jurists, especially al-Kasani, connected khiyar al-'ayb to the theory of error: the buyer's will was vitiated by the defect, giving them the right to return the item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implied condition
Freedom from defects is an implied condition (shart bi al-dalala) in commutative contracts. The buyer assumes the item is sound.
This is a general principle not requiring explicit stipulation.
2
Recognize the error
When a defect appears, it becomes clear that the buyer was mistaken about the condition of the item. This error impairs consent (rida).
Al-Kasani: 'If soundness was the buyer's desire and it was not achieved, his consent is impaired.'
3
Apply the consequences
The impaired consent gives the buyer the right to rescind the contract (khiyar al-'ayb). The seller is not surprised because such defects are expected.
Both buyer and seller bear the risk of unknown defects.
4
Distinguish from other errors
Errors can arise from fraud (tadlis) or from the seller's own ignorance. Both give the buyer the right of return.
The passage also mentions defective performance as a fourth type of consent defect.
5
Connect to modern law
Implied warranty of merchantability in modern contract law parallels this Hanafi principle.
The rule protects the reasonable expectations of the buyer.

انعقد العقد لازماً للمشتري لا خيار له في إبطاله، لأنه غير معذور بهذا الغلط بعد المشاهدة والإشارة واختلاف الوصف المسمى عن الواقع لا عبرة له في هذه الحال، لأنَّ الإشارة هنا في الشيء الحاضر أبلغ طرق التعريف وأقواها، فإذا اجتمع معها وخالفها ما هو دونها تعريفاً وهو الوصف الكلامي فالعبرة للإشارة. ويقرّر الفقهاء في هذا المقام قاعدة هي : أنَّ الوصف في الحاضر لغو، وفي الغائب معتبر (المجلة (٦٥) .

٥/٣٦ - (الحالة الثانية : كون مراد العاقد واضحاً من الدلائل : وأما حالة كون مراد العاقد الغالط مكشوفاً واضحاً بطريق الدلالة دون تصريح منه فأمثلته كثيرة في فقه المذاهب .

ففقهاء المذهب الحنفي يذكرون أن من اشترى عبداً مشهوراً بأنه يحسن الكتابة أو الخبز فوجده ناسياً ذلك، أو اشترى جارية كانت معروفة بأنها تحسن الطبخ أو الخياطة فوجدها ناسية ذلك، فإنّ للمشتري فسخ البيع ولو لم يشترط هذا الوصف فيهما عند العقد ،صراحة، لأن الظاهر أنه إنما اشترى رغبة في الصفة، فصارت مشروطة بطريق الدلالة ( ر ) رد المحتار، الجزء الرابع أواخر خيار الشرط، والبحر الرائق لابن نجيم ٣٦/٦) .

وفقهاء المذهب المالكي يصرحون بأنه إذا باع الشخص حجراً في سوق الجواهر دل ذلك على أنه إنما يبيع جوهرة، وإن لم يصرح بجنس الحجر في العقد، فإذا تبين أنه ليس بجوهر كان للمبتاع القيام . وإن باعه في غير سوق الجوهر لم يكن له قيام ( ر ) شرح الحطاب على المختصر .

-
ومما يعتبر من الأوصاف مشروطاً دلالة ولو لم يصرح به في العقد
سلامة المعقود عليه من العيوب في عقود المعاوضة. فمن حق المشتري أو المستأجر مثلاً أن يحسب المبيع أو المأجور خالياً من العيوب؛ ومن واجب البائع أو المؤجر معرفة ذلك وتقديره. فإذا ظهر في المبيع أو المأجور عيب كان المشتري أو المستأجر غالطاً ومعذوراً في غلطه فله الرد. ولا يكون في ذلك مفاجأة للبائع أو للمؤجر، لأنَّ عليهما أن يتوقعا ذلك(1) .

هذه إجمالاً خلاصة الحالات التي يعتبر فيها غلط العاقد من شوائب الإرادة العقدية، لأنَّ غلطه واضح من حيث إن العاقد قد كشف عن إرادته بنفسه صراحة أو أن إرادته مكشوفة بطريق الدلالة. فإذا خالف الواقع مراده المكشوف كان معذوراً في غلطه الذي عاب ،رضاه، لوضوح الغلط بالنسبة إلى العاقد الآخر، فلا يكون فسخ العقد مفاجأة له مخلة باستقرار التعامل.

(1) يقول الأستاذ السنهوري في كتابه الجديد مصادر الحق في الفقه الإسلامي ١٣٠/٢ الطبعة الأولى : سلامة الشيء من العيوب شرط ضمني في العقد تقتضيه طبائع الأشياء. فإذا ظهر عيب في الشيء تبيَّن أن المشتري كان واهماً عندما اعتقد أن الشيء سليم من العيب. ومن هنا كان خيار العيب متصلاً أوثق الاتصال بنظرية الغلط، بل هو ليس إلا صورة من صوره المتعددة .

ويقول الإمام الكاساني من فقهاء الحنفية في كتابه «البدائع في بحث خيار العيب (٥/ ٢٧٤) : ... السلامة مشروطة في العقد دلالة فكانت كالمشروطة نصاً، فإذا فاتت المساواة كان له الخيار . لأن البيع عقد معاوضة والمعاوضات مبناها على المساواة عادة وحقيقة . . . ولأن السلامة لما كانت مرغوب المشتري ولم يحصل فقد اختل رضاه. وهذا يوجب الخيار لأنّ الرضا شرط صحة البيع ... فانعدام الرضا يمنع صحة البيع؛ واختلاله يوجب الخيار فيه إثباتاً للحكم على قدر الدليل».

وهذا البيان من صاحب البدائع كما ترى صريح في أن حكم خيار العيب في الفقه الإسلامي مبني على أساس أن رضا المشتري قد اختل بظهور العيب في المبيع، فهو من قبيل شوائب الإرادة المسماة عيوب الرضا.

وقد قدمنا ف (٥/٣٥ أن تدليس العيب يدخل في صور الخلابة . ونضيف هنا أنَّ العيب الذي لم يدلسه البائع تدليساً على المشتري بل كان يجهله البائع نفسه يمكن عده من قبيل الغلط وسنرى أيضاً أنه يمكن عده من صور اختلال التنفيذ الذي هو العيب الرابع الأخير من عيوب الرضا.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. The contract becomes binding on the buyer with no option to annul it, because he is not excused for this error after seeing and pointing, and the difference between the named description and reality is disregarded in this case, because pointing here at the present object is the most effective and strongest method of identification. So if it coincides with and contradicts what is weaker in identification, namely verbal description, then consideration is given to the pointing. The jurists establish in this context a rule: Description in a present object is irrelevant, while in an absent object it is considered (Majalla Article 65). 5/36 - (Second Situation: The Intention of the Contracting Party Being Clear from Indications: As for the situation where the intention of the mistaken party is revealed and clear by way of indication without explicit statement, its examples are many in the fiqh of the schools. The Hanafi jurists mention that if someone buys a slave famous for knowing writing or baking and finds him forgetting that, or buys a slave girl known for cooking or sewing and finds her forgetting that, then the buyer has the right to rescind the sale even if he did not explicitly stipulate this description at the time of the contract, because it is apparent that he only bought desiring the attribute, so it becomes stipulated by way of indication (Radd al-Muhtar, vol. 4, end of Khiyar al-Shart, and al-Bahr al-Rai'q by Ibn Nujaym 6/36). And the Maliki jurists state that if a person sells a stone in the jewel market, that indicates that he is only selling a jewel, even if he did not specify the type of stone in the contract; so if it becomes clear that it is not a jewel, the buyer has the right to 'qiyam' (claim). But if he sold it outside the jewel market, he has no such right (Sharh al-Hattab 'ala al-Mukhtasar). And among the descriptions considered as stipulated by indication even if not explicitly stated in the contract is the soundness of the subject matter from defects in commutative contracts. So it is the right of the buyer or lessee, for example, to consider the sold or leased item free of defects; and it is the duty of the seller or lessor to know that and take it into account. So if a defect appears in the sold or leased item, the buyer or lessee is mistaken and excused for his mistake, so he has the right of return. And that is not a surprise to the seller or lessor, because both must expect it. This, in summary, is the essence of the situations in which the mistake of the contracting party is considered among the defects of contractual will, because his mistake is clear in that the party has revealed his intention by himself explicitly, or his intention is revealed by way of indication. So if reality contradicts his revealed intention, he is excused for his mistake that tainted his consent, due to the clarity of the mistake relative to the other party, so rescission of the contract is not a surprise to him disrupting the stability of transactions. (1) Professor Sanhuri says in his new book 'Sources of Right in Islamic Fiqh' (2/130, first edition): 'Soundness of the object from defects is an implied condition in the contract required by the nature of things. So if a defect appears in the object, it becomes clear that the buyer was mistaken when he believed the object to be free of defect. Hence the option of defect is most strongly connected to the theory of error; indeed it is but one of its multiple forms.' And Imam al-Kasani, a Hanafi jurist, says in his book 'al-Bada'i' in the discussion of khiyar al-'ayb (5/274): 'Soundness is stipulated in the contract by implication, so it is like being stipulated explicitly. So if equivalence is lost, he has the option. Because sale is a commutative contract, and commutative contracts are based on equivalence by custom and reality. And because soundness was the desire of the buyer and was not achieved, so his consent was impaired. And this necessitates the option because consent is a condition for the validity of the sale. So absence of consent prevents the validity of the sale; and its impairment necessitates the option in it, establishing the ruling to the extent of the evidence.' And this statement from the author of al-Bada'i', as you see, is explicit that the ruling of khiyar al-'ayb in Islamic fiqh is built on the basis that the buyer's consent was impaired by the appearance of the defect in the sold item, so it is among the defects of the will called 'defects of consent'. We have already presented in 5/35 that fraudulent concealment of a defect falls under forms of deception. And we add here that a defect which the seller did not conceal fraudulently from the buyer but rather the seller himself was ignorant of it can be counted as a type of error, and we will see also that it can be counted as a form of defective performance, which is the fourth and last defect of consent.

Commentary
This passage discusses error (ghalat) in contracts from the perspective of Islamic jurisprudence, specifically focusing on when a mistaken party is excused. It begins with a rule from the Majalla: in a present object, verbal description is irrelevant if there is a contradiction between description and actual viewing/pointing, because the act of pointing to the specific object is the strongest means of identification. This is a qawida kulliya (major rule) applied in sales of specific goods.

The text then moves to the second situation: where the mistaken party's intention is clear through circumstantial indications (dalala) without explicit stipulation. Examples from the Hanafi school: buying a slave known for a skill who turns out to have forgotten it; here the buyer can rescind because the skill is considered an implied condition (shart bi al-dalala). The Maliki school gives the example of selling a stone in the jewel market — the place of sale indicates it is a jewel, so if it is not, the buyer has recourse.

Finally, the passage addresses the implied condition of freedom from defects in commutative contracts (like sale and lease). The buyer/lessee assumes the item is defect-free; if a defect appears, they are mistaken and excused, having the right to return the item (khiyar al-'ayb). The passage concludes by noting that khiyar al-'ayb is a form of error, whether the defect was concealed or unknown to the seller. All these cases share the characteristic that the error is clear to the other party, so rescission does not disrupt transactions.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return the sold item due to a pre-existing defect that impairs the value or utility.
شوائب الإرادة العقدية (shawā'ib al-irāda al-'aqdiyya): Defects of contractual will, such as error, fraud, and duress, that vitiate consent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Kasani, why does khiyar al-'ayb exist?
A) Because the buyer was cheated by the seller.
B) Because soundness is an implied condition and its absence impairs the buyer's consent.
C) Because the contract is void due to defect.
D) Because the law wants to punish the seller.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-'ayb is directly rooted in the theory of error: the buyer's mistaken belief in soundness impairs consent, allowing rescission.

The Principle of Non-Obvious Error in Contracts
Explaining the ruling on non-obvious error in contracts with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-ghalat ghayr al-wadih la yu'tad bihi' to determine whether an error in a contract is obvious or hidden and state the resulting ruling (binding or option).

Core Idea
Only an obvious error that is disclosed by stipulation or circumstantial evidence vitiates consent; hidden errors do not affect contract validity.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi-Maliki principle: the law prioritizes transactional stability over correcting hidden errors. You will learn how to distinguish between a non-obvious error that leaves the contract binding, and an obvious error that gives rise to a khiyar (option). The examples from classical fiqh will sharpen your ability to apply this qawida in real fatwa scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the error
First, determine whether the error is hidden (the party's true intent was not disclosed) or obvious (the intent was expressed through stipulation or circumstances).
Hidden = no mention of specific quality; obvious = condition or circumstantial indication.
2
Check for stipulation or circumstantial evidence
If the buyer stipulated a specific quality (e.g., 'silk'), the error becomes obvious and the contract is voidable. If circumstantial evidence clearly shows intent (e.g., buying a camel for milk), the error is also obvious.
Without such indicators, the error is non-obvious.
3
Apply the qawida with its exceptions
The default rule is: non-obvious error does not affect the contract. However, exceptions apply when: (a) the error is obvious through stipulation, (b) circumstantial evidence reveals true intent, or (c) the error was unavoidable (the party could not have known).
Each exception has its own governing sub-maxim.
4
Derive the ruling
If the error is non-obvious: the contract is binding (lazim), no option. If the error is obvious: the aggrieved party has a khiyar (option to rescind).
For the unavoidable error case, the seller may rescind upon oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٦/٣٦ - الغلط غير الواضح
فإذا كان الغلط خفياً غير واضح بأن لم يكشف العاقد عن مراده، ولم يكن مكشوفاً من الدلائل والقرائن، فإنّ الفقه الإسلامي لا يعتد عندئذ بغلط العاقد، لأنّ العبرة للإرادة الظاهرة من الإيجاب والقبول ما دامت الإرادة الحقيقية في طي الخفاء، حرصاً على استقرار التعامل؛ لأن التضحية بمصلحة العاقد الشخصية الجزئية عندئذ أولى من التضحية باستقرار المعاملات وباطمئنان المتعاقدين إلى ثبات نتائجها وثقتهم بها .
وعلى هذا الأساس يقرر الفقهاء أنَّ من باع مصلى (سجادة) دون أن يذكر نوعه، وهو يظنه من قطن أو كتان فتبيَّن أنه من الخز فليس له خيار. وكذا لو اشتراه على ظن أنه خز فظهر خلافه، فهو ملزم به ولا خيار له نقل ذلك الحطاب عن مالك، (وهذا بخلاف ما لو اشترط المشتري في العقد شرطاً أنه من الخز فظهر من القطن، حيث يكون له خيار الوصف فيحق له فسخ العقد إن شاء، لأنّ إرادته عندئذ مكشوفة بالشرط، فيكون غلطه معذوراً يعيب رضاه). وكذا من باع أو اشترى بعيراً(۱) وهو يظنه ناقة فإذا هو جمل. إلا أن يكون ظاهر حاله أنه يشتريها لأجل اللبن فعندئذ يكون غلطه واضحاً لأنَّ إرادته مكشوفة بدلالة الحال (ر ) البحر الرائق ٢٦/٦ ورد المحتار ٤/ أواخر خيار الشرط، وشرح المختصر للحطاب ٤٦٦/٣) .
ومثل ذلك ما نقل الحطاب عن مالك فيمن باع حجراً وسماه هكذا : حجراً) ثم تبين أنه ياقوت وهو يجهل أنه ياقوت، إن البيع لازم ولا خيار له، ولو كان المشتري يعلم أنه ياقوت وذلك لأن الحجر اسم جنس عام يشمل الياقوت وغيره، فقد سماه باسم يصلح له(۲) «أما لو قال المشتري لبائع الثياب : اخرج لي ثوباً مروياً (۱) بدينار ، فأخرج له ثوباً أعطاه إياه، ثم وجده من أثمان أربعة دنانير فالبائع هنا يحلف ويسترد ثوبه .
،
قال الحطاب في تعليل ذلك : قصد الياقوتة جاهلاً، وبين من ووجه تفرقة مالك بين الذي يبيع الياقوتة إخراج ثوب بدينار فأخرج ثوباً بأربعة أنّ الأول قد جهل وقصر إذ لم يسأل من يعلم ما هو؟ والثاني قد غلط غلطاً لا يمكن التوقي منه، فيكون له أن يحلف ويأخذ ثوبه .
وفي هذا التعليل الوجيه للتمييز في الحكم بين المسألتين المتشابهتين تتجلى لك خلاصة المبدأ الفقهي العام الذي استخلصناه وقررناه في الغلط، من حيث كونه غلطاً واضحاً في إرادة مكشوفة فيعيب الرضا إذ لا يُخِلُّ باستقرار المعاملات، أو كونه غلطاً خفياً في إرادة خفية فلا يعتد به. فهنا يعلم كل من البائع والمشتري أن المبيع ثوب مروي بدينار. فإذا أخذ المشتري ثوباً بأربعة دنانير فإن فسخ العقد لا يكون مفاجأة له إذا حلف البائع على غلطه واسترد ثوبه (ر) مصادر الحق للأستاذ السنهوري ص / ۱۲۳).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
6/36 - Non-Obvious Error
If the error is hidden, not obvious, in that the contracting party did not disclose his intent, and it was not apparent from evidence and circumstantial indications, then Islamic fiqh does not consider the error of the contracting party at that time, because consideration is given to the apparent intent from offer and acceptance as long as the true intent is concealed, in order to preserve the stability of transactions; because sacrificing the personal, partial interest of the contracting party at that time is preferable to sacrificing the stability of transactions and the confidence of the contracting parties in the stability of their results and their trust in them.
On this basis, the jurists rule that whoever sells a prayer rug (carpet) without mentioning its type, thinking it is cotton or linen, and it turns out to be silk, has no option. Likewise if he bought it thinking it was silk and it turned out otherwise, he is bound by it and has no option—this is reported by al-Hattab from Malik. (This is contrary to the case where the buyer stipulated in the contract a condition that it is silk and it turned out to be cotton, where he has the option of description and has the right to rescind the contract if he wishes, because his intent at that time is disclosed by the condition, so his error is excused and vitiates his consent.) Likewise, whoever sells or buys a camel thinking it is a female camel and it turns out to be a male camel—unless it is apparent from his situation that he is buying it for milk, in which case his error is obvious because his intent is disclosed by the circumstantial indication. (See: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 6/26, Radd al-Muhtar 4/ near the end of the option of condition, and Sharh al-Mukhtasar by al-Hattab 3/466).
Similar is what al-Hattab transmitted from Malik concerning whoever sold a stone and named it 'stone', and it turned out to be a ruby while he was ignorant that it was a ruby: the sale is binding and he has no option, even if the buyer knew it was a ruby, because 'stone' is a generic noun that includes ruby and others, so he named it with a name that is valid for it. "But if the buyer said to the cloth merchant, 'Take out for me a Marwi cloth for a dinar,' and he took out a cloth and gave it to him, then he found it to be worth four dinars, then the seller here takes an oath and takes back his cloth."
Al-Hattab said in explaining that: "The one who intended a ruby ignorantly, and the one who took out a cloth for a dinar and gave out a cloth worth four—Malik distinguishes: the first was ignorant and negligent as he did not ask someone who knows what it is; the second made an error that could not be avoided, so he may take an oath and take back his cloth."
In this sound reasoning for distinguishing between the two similar cases, the essence of the general fiqh principle that we have extracted and established regarding error becomes clear to you, in terms of whether it is an obvious error in a disclosed intent, which vitiates consent because it does not harm the stability of transactions, or a hidden error in a hidden intent, which is not considered. Here, both the seller and buyer know that the sold item is a Marwi cloth for a dinar. So if the buyer takes a cloth worth four dinars, the rescission of the contract is not a surprise to him if the seller swears to his error and takes back his cloth. (See: Masadir al-Haqq by Professor al-Sanhuri, p. 123).
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the fiqh principle regarding non-obvious error in contracts. The principle is that if a contracting party's error is hidden and not externally manifested, the contract remains valid and binding because the law looks at the apparent intent expressed in offer and acceptance. The rationale is the protection of transactional stability outweighs the individual's personal mistake. The passage provides several examples: selling a carpet thinking it is cotton when it is silk; buying a camel thinking it is female when it is male; selling a stone that turns out to be a ruby. In all these cases, the error is non-obvious because the party did not specify the exact type, so the contract is binding. However, if the error becomes obvious through a stipulation or circumstantial indication (like buying a camel for milk), then the party has an option. The passage also discusses a case from Malik where a buyer asks for a specific quality cloth but receives a different one; there the seller's error is unavoidable, so he can swear and rescind. The overall principle is that only obvious error vitiates consent.

Key Terms
الغلط غير الواضح (al-ghalat ghayr al-wadih): An error that is hidden and not apparent from the external expressions or circumstances of the contract.
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-irada al-zahira): The intent that is expressed through offer and acceptance or other clear indicators.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The right to cancel or confirm a contract due to a defect, condition, or other reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone sells a stone thinking it is ordinary but it turns out to be a ruby, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void because of mutual mistake.
B) The sale is binding and the seller has no option.
C) The seller can rescind because of the error.
D) The buyer has the option to return the ruby.

Key Takeaway
Non-obvious error does not invalidate a contract; only error disclosed by stipulation or circumstantial evidence gives rise to an option.
