The Binding Nature of the Pawn Contract (al-Rahn)
Explains the asymmetry in bindingness of the pawn contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the binding rule of the pawn contract for each party and apply the fatwa hierarchy when a creditor wishes to renounce the pawn.

Core Idea
Pawn binds the pledgor but not the pledgee—security for a right can be renounced.

In Hanafi fiqh, the pawn contract (al-rahn) has a unique asymmetry: the debtor (pledgor) cannot unilaterally reclaim the pawned item, but the creditor (pledgee) may return it anytime. This lesson unpacks this rule, its legal reasoning (ta‘lil), and its implications for issuing fatwas. Understanding this distinction prevents a common error of assuming contracts are always binding on both parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Recognize who is the pledgor (rāhin, debtor) and who is the pledgee (murtahin, creditor). The pawned item (rahn) is held by the pledgee as security.
The parties are clearly defined: one owes a debt, the other holds collateral.
2
Determine the ruling for each party
Note that the contract is binding (lāzim) on the pledgor: he cannot demand the pawn back while the debt exists. It is non-binding (ghayr lāzim) on the pledgee: he may renounce (yatakhalla) and return the pawn at any time.
This is the central rule of this passage.
3
Understand the ta‘līl (ratio legis)
The reason given: the pawn is a security (tawthīq) for the pledgee’s right. Since it is for his benefit, he may choose to give it up. The pledgor has already received the debt and cannot revoke the security unilaterally.
The illah (cause) is that the contract is a beneficence for the creditor.
4
Derive the governing qawida
From this rule, formulate a sub-maxim: 'The pawn contract is binding on the pledgor and non-binding on the pledgee.' Contrast with the general principle that contracts are binding (al-‘aqd lāzim).
This qawida is a sughrā in the chapter of rahn.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
Recognize that this ruling comes from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the muftā bihī position. The common error is assuming both sides are bound; the mufti must clarify the asymmetry.
No ikhtilāf on this rule within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - الرهن :
فإنه لازم في حق الراهن المدين غير لازم في حق المرتهن الدائن لأنه توثيق لحقه فله أن يتخلى عنه ،ويتنازل فيعاد المرهون إلى الراهن (ر ف٩/٤٧).
4 - Pawn:
It is binding with respect to the pledgor-debtor, but non-binding with respect to the pledgee-creditor, because it is a security for his right, so he may relinquish it and renounce, and then the pawned item is returned to the pledgor (see paragraph 9/47).
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the pawn contract (al-rahn). The author explains that pawn is binding (lazim) only on the pledgor (al-rahin) — the debtor — meaning he cannot unilaterally reclaim the pawned item while the debt exists. However, it is non-binding on the pledgee (al-murtahin) — the creditor — because the pledgee holds it as security for his right. Therefore, the pledgee may choose to give up the pawn and release the item back to the pledgor. This asymmetry is based on the purpose of the contract: to secure the creditor's right, so the creditor is not compelled to hold the pawn.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is given as security for a debt, remaining in the creditor's possession.
راهن (rāhin): The debtor who pawns his property.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who receives the pawned property as security.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the pawn contract non-binding on the pledgee?
A) Because the pledgee has full ownership
B) Because it is a security for his right, and he may relinquish it
C) Because the contract is invalid
D) Because the debtor can demand it back anytime

Key Takeaway
The pawn contract is binding on the debtor (pledgor) but non-binding on the creditor (pledgee), who may renounce the security at will.

Surety Contract: Binding on the Surety, Non-Binding on the Creditor
States a rule of surety contract: binding on surety, non-binding on creditor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on surety and apply it to determine whether a surety or creditor can unilaterally terminate the contract.

Core Idea
Surety binds the guarantor but frees the creditor — the right is the creditor's to waive.

The Hanafi school treats the surety contract as analogous to pawn (rahn): the surety assumes a binding obligation, but the creditor holds a right that he may waive at any time. This passage records a concise rule — 'lazim bihaqq al-kafil, ghayr lazim bil-nisba li al-da'in'. Understanding this asymmetry is essential for issuing fatwas on guarantees, as it determines who can withdraw and when.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
The surety (kafil) is the guarantor; the creditor (da'in) is the one owed the debt. The text contrasts their legal positions.
2
Note the binding status for each
The surety is bound (lazim) — he cannot unilaterally withdraw. The creditor is not bound (ghayr lazim) — he may release the surety at will.
3
Understand the analogy to pawn
Like a pawn (rahn) where the pawner is bound to keep the pawn but the pawnee may return it, here the surety is bound but the creditor may cancel the guarantee.
4
Recognize the practical implication
The creditor can terminate the surety's obligation without the surety's or debtor's consent, but the surety cannot abandon his guarantee unilaterally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه - الكفالة :
فالكفالة كالرهن الازمة بحق الكفيل، وغير لازمة بالنسبة إلى الدائن
5 - Surety:
Thus surety is like pawn: binding with respect to the surety, and non-binding with respect to the creditor.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the surety contract (al-kafalah). The author analogizes it to pawn: it is binding on the surety (al-kafil) — meaning he cannot unilaterally withdraw from his guarantee — but non-binding on the creditor (al-da'in), who can release the surety from the obligation at any time. This is because the surety contract is a form of security for the creditor, and the creditor may waive that security. The Hanafi position is that the surety assumes a binding obligation, unlike the creditor, who holds the right.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A contract where a person guarantees to fulfill an obligation of another if the latter defaults.
كفيل (kafīl): The person who gives the guarantee (surety).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the legal status of a surety contract for the creditor?
A) Binding, so the creditor cannot release the surety
B) Non-binding, so the creditor may release the surety at will
C) Binding only if the debtor defaults
D) Non-binding, so the creditor must always enforce the guarantee

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a surety contract is binding on the guarantor but the creditor may release him at any time.

Contracts Originally Non-binding That Become Binding
Classifies contracts with original revocability that become binding under conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four contracts that are originally non-binding (ghayr lazim) and state the condition under which each becomes binding (lazim), applying the qawida to new cases.

Core Idea
Four contracts are inherently revocable, but each becomes binding when a specific condition—attachment of a third-party right, issuance of judgment, death, or impediment to revocation—is met.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi mu'amalat: certain contracts are designed with flexibility—they can be rescinded freely by either party. However, the Shari'ah recognizes that this flexibility must yield to finality and protection of rights under specific circumstances. Mustafa al-Zarqa identifies four such contracts and the conditions that transform them from revocable to binding. Understanding this qawida helps a mufti determine when a contract can be rescinded and when it is final.

Guided Path
1
State the general qawida
Identify the rule as expressed by al-Zarqa: 'Contracts whose original principle is non-bindingness, but they become binding in some circumstances.' This is a wusta-level universal maxim in Hanafi contract law.
Memorize the Arabic: عقود الأصل فيها عدم اللزوم، ولكنها تلزم في بعض الأحوال.
2
Enumerate the four contracts
List the four contracts: agency (wakalah), arbitration (tahkim), will (wasiyyah), and gift (hibah). Each has a default revocable nature.
These are the only contracts in this category; the passage is exhaustive.
3
Identify the default ruling for each
Confirm that each contract is originally non-binding: both parties can rescind agency and arbitration before a specific event; the testator can revoke the will during life; the donor can revoke the gift unless an impediment exists.
Default revocability is the 'asl' (original principle) of these contracts.
4
Identify the condition that triggers bindingness
For agency: when a third party's right attaches (e.g., creditor's right in a pledge). For arbitration: when the arbitrator issues a judgment. For will: upon the testator's death. For gift: when one of the seven impediments exists (e.g., spouse, mahram, destruction of property).
Each condition marks a transition from non-binding to binding (luzum).
5
Apply the qawida to a new case
Given a new scenario, identify which of the four contracts is involved, assess whether the binding condition is present, and state whether revocation is permissible.
Practice this step with the tasks below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٤٠) آثار العقد - اللزوم.
المكفول له كما بينا في الرهن بالنسبة إلى المرتهن.
٦/٤٠ - النوع الثاني : عقود الأصل فيها عدم اللزوم، ولكنها تلزم في بعض الأحوال.
وجملة عقود هذا النوع أربعة هي : الوكالة التحكيم، الوصية، الهبة.
١ - الوكالة :
وهي عقد يفوض به الإنسان غيره ونيابته عن نفسه في التصرف.
فلكل من الطرفين أن يفسخ عقدها متى شاء. فالوكيل يستطيع عزل نفسه، كما أن للموكل أن يعزله، لأنَّ الوكالة استعانة وإعانة، فللموكل أن يكف عن الاستعانة، كما أنّ للوكيل أن يكف عن الإعانة.
ويلحظ أنه لا يسري حكم العزل إلا من تاريخ علم الطرف الآخر (المجلة ١٥٢٣ - ١٥٢٤).
ولكن الوكالة إذا تعلق بها حق شخص ثالث تصبح لازمة ويمتنع فيها العزل والفسخ بلا رضى ذلك الشخص، وذلك في عدة حالات:
فمن ذلك ما لو رهن المدين مالاً ووكل وكيلاً بناء على طلب الدائن لبيع الرهن إذا لم يؤد الدين عند حلوله؛ فلا يملك الموكل ولا الوكيل فسخ هذه الوكالة دون موافقة الدائن المرتهن لتعلق حقه بهذه الوكالة تسهيلاً لاستيفاء دينه .
وكذا لو أراد المدين السفر فطلب الدائن من الحاكم منعه أو يوكل وكيلاً عنه ليتمكن من مخاصمته فوكل المدين وكيلاً بالخصومة بناء على هذا الطلب من الدائن، وسافر فليس له عزل هذا الوكيل. (ر: المجلة ١٥٢١).

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٢ - التحكيم :
وهو أن يلجأ طرفان مختصمان إلى شخص يختارانه برضاهما ليفصل في الخلاف الذي بينهما، بدلاً من القاضي.
فلكل من الطرفين أن يفسخ عقد التحكيم ويعزل المحكم قبل أن يحكم.
أما بعد صدور الحكم منه فلا، بل يصبح التحكيم لازماً (ر: المجلة / ١٨٤٧).
٣ - الوصية :
وهذه من تصرفات الإرادة المنفردة فيجوز للموصي فسخها والرجوع فيها ما دام حياً. فإذا مات انقلبت لازمة في حدود ثلث ماله؛ ولا ينتقل حق الرجوع إلى ورثته.
٤ - الهبة :
ففي الهبة شيء من معنى عدم اللزوم، إذ يجوز الرجوع فيها واسترداد الموهوب ما لم يمنع منه أحد موانع سبعة معروفة في بابها. فإذا وجد شيء من موانع الرجوع هذه تصبح الهبة معه لازمة لا يسوغ للواهب الرجوع فيها.
ومن تلك الموانع التي تصبح معها الهبة لازمة كون الموهوب له زوجاً للواهب أو من أقاربه المحارم؛ وكذا هلاك المال الموهوب عند الموهوب له أو خروجه عن ملكه أو بغيره من الأسباب الناقلة للملكية ببيع.
وعلى كل يتوقف الرجوع في الهبة على القضاء أو الرضاء، فلا تعود
Chapter (40): Effects of the Contract - Bindingness.
The person for whom the guarantee is made is as we explained in pledge with respect to the mortgagee.
6/40 - The second type: Contracts whose original principle is non-bindingness, but they become binding in some circumstances.
The total number of contracts of this type is four: agency, arbitration, will, gift.
1 - Agency:
It is a contract by which a person delegates another and appoints him as his representative in disposition.
Each of the two parties may rescind the contract whenever they wish. The agent can dismiss himself, just as the principal can dismiss him, because agency is seeking help and providing help. So the principal may refrain from seeking help, and the agent may refrain from providing help.
It is noted that the effect of dismissal does not take effect except from the date the other party learns of it (Majalla 1523-1524).
But if a third party's right becomes attached to the agency, it becomes binding, and dismissal and rescission are prohibited without that person's consent. This occurs in several cases:
Among them: if a debtor pledges property and appoints an agent at the creditor's request to sell the pledge if the debt is not paid at its due time; then neither the principal nor the agent may rescind this agency without the consent of the creditor-mortgagee, because his right is attached to this agency to facilitate the collection of his debt.
Similarly, if a debtor wants to travel and the creditor asks the judge to prevent him or to appoint an agent to be able to litigate with him, and the debtor appoints an agent for litigation based on this request from the creditor, and then travels, he may not dismiss this agent. (Ref: Majalla 1521).

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
2 - Arbitration:
It is that two disputing parties resort to a person they choose by mutual consent to decide the dispute between them, instead of a judge.
Each of the two parties may rescind the arbitration contract and dismiss the arbitrator before he gives judgment.
But after he issues his judgment, no, rather the arbitration becomes binding (Ref: Majalla 1847).
3 - Will:
This is one of the acts of unilateral will; the testator may rescind it and revoke it as long as he is alive. When he dies, it becomes binding within the limit of one-third of his wealth; the right of revocation does not pass to his heirs.
4 - Gift:
In the gift there is an element of non-bindingness, since revocation and recovery of the gifted item are permissible unless prevented by one of seven known impediments in its chapter. If any of these impediments to revocation exist, the gift becomes binding and the donor may not revoke it.
Among those impediments that make the gift binding are: the donee being the donor's spouse or among his mahram relatives; likewise the destruction of the gifted property while in the donee's possession, or its leaving his ownership through other transferring causes of ownership such as sale.
In any case, revocation of a gift depends on a court order or mutual consent, so it does not return...
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a classification and analysis of a specific category of contracts: those that are originally revocable (non-binding) but become binding under certain conditions. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating the general rule: 'Contracts whose original principle is non-bindingness, but they become binding in some circumstances.' He then enumerates four contracts that fall under this type: agency, arbitration, will, and gift.

For each contract, he explains the default rule and the conditions that transform it into a binding contract.

1. Agency (al-Wakalah): By default, both principal and agent can rescind the agency at any time, since it is based on mutual assistance. However, if a third party's right becomes attached—such as a creditor's right in a pledge arrangement—the agency becomes binding and cannot be revoked without that party's consent. The author gives two examples: a debtor appointing an agent to sell pledged property at the creditor's request, and a debtor appointing an agent for litigation at the creditor's request to enable the creditor to sue.

2. Arbitration (al-Tahkim): The parties may rescind the arbitration agreement before the arbitrator issues a judgment. Once the judgment is rendered, however, the arbitration becomes binding and cannot be revoked.

3. Will (al-Wasiyyah): A will is a unilateral disposition; the testator may revoke it at any time during his life. Upon death, it becomes binding within the limit of one-third of the estate (the maximum allowed for bequests to non-heirs). The right to revoke does not pass to the heirs.

4. Gift (al-Hibah): A gift is generally revocable unless one of seven impediments exists. The author mentions two examples: the donee being the donor's spouse or a mahram relative, or the gifted property being destroyed or transferred out of the donee's ownership (e.g., sold). When such an impediment exists, the gift becomes binding. Revocation of a gift requires either a court order or mutual consent.

The passage draws heavily on the Ottoman Majalla (Mejelle) articles, indicating its codificatory approach. The principle that these contracts are originally non-binding but become binding is a practical rule in Hanafi mu'amalat, balancing freedom of contract with the protection of third-party rights and finality of transactions.

Key Terms
لزوم (luzūm): Bindingness; a contract becomes binding when neither party can unilaterally rescind it.
وكالة (wakālah): A contract whereby a person delegates another to act on his behalf in legal dispositions.
تحكيم (taḥkīm): A contract where disputing parties agree to refer their dispute to a private arbitrator.
وصية (waṣiyyah): A unilateral disposition of property to take effect after death, limited to one-third of the estate.
هبة (hibah): A gratuitous transfer of ownership of property during the donor's lifetime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does an arbitration contract become binding such that neither party can rescind it?
A) From the moment the arbitrator is appointed
B) After the arbitrator issues his judgment
C) When the dispute arises
D) Never, it is always revocable

Key Takeaway
The rule that four contracts—agency, arbitration, will, and gift—are originally non-binding but become binding under specific conditions is a foundational principle in Hanafi contract law, balancing flexibility with the need for finality and protection of rights.

Contracts Binding in Origin with Rescission for Supervening Excuses: Ijarah and Muzara'ah
Describes the third category of contracts and the legal principles governing flexibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that contracts binding in origin may be rescinded due to supervening excuses and identify the requirement of compensation for losses incurred.

Core Idea
A binding contract may be rescinded when a genuine excuse makes enforcement wasteful, but compensation is due.

This lesson introduces a third category of contracts in Hanafi fiqh: those that are binding in origin but permit rescission under limited circumstances due to supervening excuses. Unlike absolutely binding contracts, these allow flexibility to prevent harm. The primary examples are ijarah (hire) and muzara'ah (sharecropping). As a mufti, understanding this maxim helps you evaluate when a party may exit a contract without penalty beyond compensating for actual losses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
State the Arabic qawida and its English translation. This is the anchor for the entire category.
Maxim: عقود الأصل فيها اللزوم ولكن في طبيعتها شيئاً من عدم اللزوم في ظروف محدودة.
2
List the two contracts
Name the two contracts that fall under this category: Ijarah (hire) and Muzara'ah (sharecropping). Briefly define each.
Ijarah: hire of services or usufruct. Muzara'ah: partnership in cultivation.
3
Apply the condition for rescission
Recognize that rescission is only allowed due to a supervening excuse ('udhr tari') that makes execution harmful or wasteful. If no genuine excuse, the contract remains binding.
Examples from the text: canceling a trip after hiring an animal, or a wedding after hiring a cook.
4
Determine compensation
Explain that the rescinding party must compensate the other for losses incurred in preparation. This is a requirement of fairness and causation (tasabbub).
Compensation covers costs already incurred, such as time, money, or disruption.

وذلك كما لو استأجر الإنسان دابة للسفر ثم عدل عن السفر، أو استأجر طباخاً لوليمة ثم صرف النظر عنها، أو استأجر طبيباً لقلع ضرس أو لإجراء عملية جراحية ثم سكن ألمه فعدل عن إجرائها.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. 7/40 - The Third Type: Contracts whose origin is binding, but in their nature there is something of non-bindingness in limited circumstances. This type is the opposite of the second type; the total number of its contracts is two: Ijarah (hire) and Muzara'ah (sharecropping). 1 - Ijarah: Indeed, the origin in it is bindingness with respect to the two contracting parties, so both parties are compelled to execute it; however, our jurists have established in it the permissibility of withdrawal and rescission due to supervening excuses, because executing the contract at that time would be a waste of money or unreasonable harm. That is like if a person hires an animal for travel and then refrains from traveling, or hires a cook for a wedding feast and then cancels it, or hires a doctor to extract a tooth or perform surgery and then the pain subsides and he refrains from undergoing it. However, it is rationally sound in fiqh to oblige the hirer in such cases to compensate the other party for the harm if the latter is surprised by the rescission after having incurred costs of preparation in money, time, or disruption; this is required by modern legal thought, and the rules of causation (tasabbub) in our fiqh do not reject it, even if our jurists did not explicitly state it. 2 - Muzara'ah: It is a type of partnership for cultivating land, where the land is from one party and the work from another (Majalla, 1431).
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage defines the third category of contracts under the Hanafi classification of bindingness. These contracts are binding in origin, meaning the parties must fulfill them, but they allow rescission when a genuine excuse arises that would make enforcement wasteful or harmful. Examples from ijarah include hiring for a trip then canceling, or hiring a doctor then no longer needing the treatment. The author notes that compensation is due for any loss caused to the other party. The second contract is muzara'ah (sharecropping), a partnership for cultivation. Both contracts share this flexible characteristic.

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A contract for hire of services or usufruct of property in exchange for rent.
المزارعة (al-muzara'ah): A partnership where one party provides land and the other provides labor for cultivation, sharing the produce.
العذر الطارئ (al-'udhr al-tari'): A supervening excuse that makes execution of the contract harmful or wasteful.

D) The hirer can rescind without any compensation because it is a supervening excuse.

Key Takeaway
Contracts like ijarah and muzara'ah are binding in origin but allow rescission with compensation when a genuine supervening excuse makes execution harmful or wasteful.

Binding Contracts with Excuse-Based Rescission: The Hanafi Rule for Ijarah
Introduces the third contract category and details ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ijarah is fundamentally binding but may be rescinded for valid, unforeseen excuses, and you will be able to identify when compensation is required to the other party.

Core Idea
Ijarah binds both parties—but when an unforeseen excuse makes performance harmful, rescission is permitted with compensation.

In Hanafi contract theory, most contracts are either binding or non-binding from the outset. However, a third category exists: contracts that are binding but contain a latent flexibility. Ijarah (lease/hire) is the prime example. This lesson unpacks the rule that ijarah can be rescinded for valid excuses, and explains the compensation principle that protects the other party's interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract Category
Understand that this passage introduces the third type of contracts: those that are binding in principle but allow rescission in limited circumstances. Ijarah is the primary example.
2
State the General Rule of Bindingness
Ijarah is binding on both parties from formation. Both must fulfill their obligations unless an unforeseen excuse arises.
Bindingness (luzum) is the default.
3
Recognize the Exception: Unforeseen Excuses
If after contract formation an unforeseen circumstance makes performance wasteful or harmful, the contract may be rescinded. Examples: canceling travel after renting an animal, canceling a banquet after hiring a cook, canceling a medical procedure after pain subsides.
The excuse must be genuine and not anticipated.
4
Apply the Compensation Principle
The party who rescinds may be required to compensate the other for expenses incurred in preparation. This is based on causation (tasabbub) principles in Hanafi fiqh.
Compensation is not automatic; it depends on actual harm.
5
Understand the Legal Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The rule balances bindingness with preventing unreasonable hardship. It reflects the maqasid of avoiding waste and harm, in line with the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar'.
This is a qawida-like rule in contract theory.

The Rule of Non-Binding Contracts: Rescission Without Retroactive Effect
Explains muzara'ah's binding status and the maxim on rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that rescission in non-binding contracts has no retroactive effect, and identify when a rescission is prevented due to illegitimate motive.

Core Idea
Rescission in non-binding contracts is permitted but does not undo past actions.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi contractual maxim using muzara'ah as the anchor. You'll learn when a contract becomes binding, the limits of rescission, and the role of motive in preventing cancellation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that muzara'ah (sharecropping) is a non-binding (ja'iz) contract before the seed is sown. The seed provider has the right to rescind.
Non-binding means either party can cancel without penalty, unless a condition makes it binding.
2
Determine the moment of binding
Once the seed is sown, the contract becomes binding (lazim) even for the seed provider. The act of sowing creates a commitment that cannot be undone.
The rationale: forcing continuation would risk the seed owner's property.
3
Apply the maxim of rescission
Rescission in non-binding contracts does not have retroactive effect. Actions taken before rescission remain valid.
Examples: dismissal of an agent does not void his past acts; return of a gifted animal does not require return of its offspring.
4
Check the motive
The seed provider cannot rescind if the motive is to replace the sharecropper with a cheaper worker or to work himself. Only a genuine change of mind allows rescission.
This is based on the legitimacy of the motive (mashru'iyyat al-ba'ith).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - المزارعة :
وهي نوع شركة على استثمار الأرض بالزرع، تكون فيها الأرض من طرف والعمل من طرف آخر (المجلة/ ١٤٣١).
فبعد تقرير حكم المزارعة في الباب السابق، نذكر هنا أن المزارعة تلحق بهذا النوع الثالث، فهي أيضاً غير لازمة في حق الطرف الذي عليه البذر قبل إلقائه في الأرض.
ففيجوز للطرف الذي عليه تقديم البذر أن يعدل عن المزارعة ويفسخ عقدها، ولكن ذلك قبل إلقاء البذر في الأرض.
وملحظ الفقهاء في هذا أن إجباره على الاستمرار في المزارعة يقتضي إلزامه بإتلاف ماله وهو البذر، ولا يدري هل ينتج أو لا ينتج؟ فصار كما لو استأجر الإنسان عاملاً لهدم داره ثم بدا له العدول عن الهدم، فإن له ذلك.
أما بعد إلقاء البذر في الأرض فتصبح المزارعة لازمة حتى في حق صاحب البذر. (الدر المختار ورد المحتار ١٧٧/٥)
ويلاحظ أن الفسخ في هذه العقود غير اللازمة ليس له استناد (أي تأثير رجعي) فعزل الوكيل لا ينقض تصرفاته السابقة، ورجوع الواهب بعد ولادة الدابة الموهوبة لا يوجب للواهب أخذ ولدها معها بل يبقى للموهوب له لأنه حَصَل في ملكه. (ر: المجلة/ ٨٦٩).
وهكذا يلحظ في بقية المسائل.
تلك العقود الأحد عشر التي ينتفي فيها اللزوم انتفاء مطلقاً أو مقيداً على اختلاف درجاته فيها.
وهناك عقد ثاني عشر نفرده بالذكر في هذه المناسبة، كان يعتبر في أصل المذهب الحنفي غير لازم فأصبح لدينا بمقتضى نصوص المجلة لازماً (۲) وهو : الاستصناع .
(۱) يلحظ هنا أن صاحب البذر إذا أراد الفسخ قبل أن يبذره، لا عدولاً منه عن المزارعة، أو لأنه عاملاً أرخص من المزارع الذي تعاقد معه، وجد يريد أن يعمل بنفسه؛ فليس له الفسخ بل يلزم بعقده مع المزارع إلى المدة المتفق عليها . ومثل ذلك يلحظ في مسألة استئجار العامل لهدم بناء ونحو ذلك إذا أراد المستأجر فسخ الإجارة: فلو عدولاً منه عن العمل جاز، ولو تبديلاً للعامل لم يجز. (ر: رد المحتار في المحل المذكور). وذلك بالنظر إلى مشروعية الباعث وعدمها كما تقدم ر ف ٤/٢٩ الحاشية).
(۲) وكذا بمقتضى أحكام القانون المدني الذي حل محل المجلة.
٥٣١
2 - Muzara'ah: It is a type of partnership for cultivating land, where the land is from one party and the work from another (Majalla/1431). After establishing the ruling of muzara'ah in the previous chapter, we mention here that muzara'ah belongs to this third type, so it is also non-binding for the party who provides the seed before it is sown in the land. So the party who is to provide the seed may withdraw from the muzara'ah and rescind the contract, but that is before the seed is sown. The jurists' observation in this is that forcing him to continue in the muzara'ah would require him to destroy his wealth, which is the seed, and he does not know whether it will produce or not. So it becomes like when a person hires a worker to demolish his house and then decides to cancel the demolition; he may do so. After the seed is sown, the muzara'ah becomes binding even for the seed owner. (al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar 5/177) It is noted that rescission in these non-binding contracts does not have retroactive effect; thus, dismissing an agent does not invalidate his previous actions, and a donor's return after the birth of a gifted animal does not entitle the donor to take its offspring with it, but it remains with the donee because it came into his ownership. (See Majalla/869). And similarly in the rest of the issues. Those are the eleven contracts in which bindingness is negated, absolutely or conditionally, to varying degrees. And there is a twelfth contract that we single out for mention on this occasion: it was considered non-binding in the original Hanafi school, but according to the texts of the Majalla it became binding, namely istisna'. (1) It is noted here that if the seed owner wants to rescind before sowing, not as a withdrawal from the muzara'ah, but because he found a cheaper worker than the sharecropper he contracted with, or he wants to work himself; then he may not rescind, but is bound by his contract with the sharecropper for the agreed period. Similarly, in the case of hiring a worker to demolish a building and the like, if the hirer wants to rescind the ijarah: if it is a withdrawal from the work, it is permitted; if it is to replace the worker, it is not permitted. (See Radd al-Muhtar in the mentioned location). This is based on the legitimacy of the motive or lack thereof, as previously mentioned in the footnote of 4/29. (2) And likewise according to the provisions of the civil law that replaced the Majalla.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the third type of contracts, focusing on muzara'ah (sharecropping). The author explains that muzara'ah belongs to this category: it is non-binding for the party responsible for providing the seed before the seed is sown. The rationale given is that forcing the seed owner to continue would require him to risk his property (the seed) with uncertain return. The author then clarifies that after the seed is sown, the contract becomes binding for all parties. The passage also includes an important note that rescission of non-binding contracts does not have retroactive effect, citing examples of agency and gift. Finally, the author mentions a twelfth contract, istisna' (manufacturing contract), which originally was non-binding in the Hanafi school but became binding according to the Majalla (and later civil law). The footnotes provide additional details: the seed owner cannot rescind if his motive is to use a cheaper worker or to work himself; similarly in ijarah, rescission is permitted only if it is a true withdrawal from the work, not a replacement of the worker. The second footnote notes that istisna' became binding also under modern civil law.

Key Terms
المزارعة (al-muzara'ah): A contract of partnership for cultivation where one party provides land and the other provides work.
البذر (al-badhr): The seed to be sown; its provision is a key determinant of the contract's binding nature.
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract for manufacturing or commissioning a specific item.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Bindingness of a contract; a binding contract cannot be unilaterally rescinded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately upon signing the contract.
B) After the seed is sown in the ground.
C) Once the harvest begins.
D) It never becomes binding for the seed provider.

Key Takeaway
Muzara'ah is non-binding before sowing, but becomes binding after; rescission has no retroactive effect, and the motive for rescission matters.

Classification of Contractual Options and Their Effect on Bindingness
Definition and classification of contractual options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify contractual options (khiyarat) based on their source and need for judicial intervention, and apply the Hanafi rule that options strip bindingness from a contract.

Core Idea
An option (khiyar) makes a contract non-binding until exercised or expired.

This passage defines khiyar and explains its effect on contract bindingness. The Hanafi school distinguishes between options arising from mutual agreement (e.g., khiyar al-shart) and those granted by law to protect a party from defects in consent (e.g., khiyar al-'ayb). A further distinction is made between options requiring a judge's ruling (like khiyar al-'ayb) and those that can be exercised without judicial intervention (like khiyar al-shart). Understanding these classifications is essential for a mufti to advise on contract rescission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida
The passage opens with the rule that an option strips bindingness from a contract from its origin. Recognize this as the foundational maxim governing khiyar.
Qawida: 'ثبوت الخيار يسلب اللزوم'
2
Classify the khiyar by source
Note the two categories: (a) khiyar arising from stipulation (e.g., khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-naqd) and (b) khiyar established by Shari'ah due to defects in consent (e.g., khiyar al-'ayb, khiyar al-ghabn).
The first depends on agreement; the second is granted by law.
3
Sub-classify by judicial need
Distinguish options that require a judge's ruling (khiyar al-'ayb needs expertise) from those that do not (khiyar al-shart can be exercised extrajudicially).
This affects procedural validity of rescission.
4
Recognize bars to khiyar
The footnote introduces a bar: if the buyer causes a new defect after knowing the old defect, the right to rescind for the old defect is lost.
Mani' (preventer) of khiyar.
5
Apply to fatwa
When presented with a case, first determine the type of option, then assess if any bar exists, and finally advise on rescission possibility.
The mufti must classify and check conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩/٤٠ - (ج) حالة الخيارات العقدية :
فإنَّ ثبوت الخيار لأحد العاقدين في فسخ العقد معناه سلب صفة اللزوم من العقد في أصله . ولذا اعتبر الفقهاء من شرائط لزوم العقود اللازمة أن لا يكون فيها خيار لأحد العاقدين (المجلة/ ١١٤ - ١١٦).
وهذه الخيارات منها ما يتوقف ثبوته على اتفاق إرادة العاقدين عليه، فلا يثبت إلا باشتراطهما فيعتبر من قبيل الشروط العقدية، وذلك خيار الشرط وخيار النقد اللذان سلف إيضاح معناهما في بحث القياس (ر: ف ١١/ ثامناً).
ومنها ما يثبت بإيجاب الشرع لمصلحة أحد العاقدين صيانة لحقوقه فيكون نتيجة لبعض عيوب الرضا المتقدّمة في بحث شوائب الإرادة، من خلابة، أو تدليس عيب، أو تجزئة صفقة إلخ ... (ر: ف ١/٣٥ - ١/٣٧).
فيحق لصاحب الخيار فسخ العقد أو إمضاؤه، ما لم يوجد مسقط للخيار أو مانع شرعي يمنع ممارسته (۱) ، مما هو معروف في مواطنه ومباحثه من الأبواب الفقهية، وفي المواد / ۳۰۰ - ٣٦٠ من المجلة.
هذا، وإنَّ بعض تلك الخيارات العقدية يتوقف على القضاء، فلا يثبت لصاحبه ولا يقبل منه الفسخ قبل قضاء القاضي كما في خيار العيب لأنه من المواطن التي تحتاج إلى خبرة وتحقيق قضائي في وجود العيب المسوغ للفسخ .
وبعضها لا يحتاج إلى القضاء، بل يستطيع صاحب الخيار بعد تحقق سببه في الواقع أن يمارس خياره في فسخ العقد، فيكون فسخه نافذاً قبل أن يقضى به، كما في حالة خيار الشرط، وخيار تفرق الصفقة .
(۱) كما لو وجد في المبيع عيب قديم وحدث فيه عند المشتري عيب حادث، فيمتنع عليه فسخ البيع بسبب العيب القديم.
9/40 - (c) The case of contractual options: For the establishment of an option for one of the contracting parties to rescind the contract means stripping the bindingness from the contract in its origin. Therefore, the jurists considered from the conditions of binding contracts that there be no option for either party (Majallah 114-116). These options include those whose establishment depends on the agreement of the wills of the parties, so it is only established by their stipulation, thus it is considered from the types of contractual conditions, such as the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) and the option of cash payment (khiyar al-naqd), whose meanings were previously clarified in the discussion of qiyas (see: F 11/8th). And among them are those that are established by the legislation of the Shari'ah for the benefit of one of the parties to protect his rights, thus it is a result of some defects of consent previously mentioned in the discussion of defects of will, such as deception (khilabah), fraud (tadlis 'ayb), splitting of a deal (tajzi'at safqah), etc. (see: F 1/35 - 1/37). So the holder of the option has the right to rescind the contract or confirm it, unless there is a factor that nullifies the option or a legal impediment preventing its exercise (1), as is known in its places and topics in the fiqh chapters, and in articles 300-360 of the Majallah. And indeed, some of those contractual options depend on the judiciary, so they are not established for the holder and rescission is not accepted from him before the judge's ruling, as in the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), because it is from the matters that require expertise and judicial investigation into the existence of the defect that justifies rescission. And some of them do not require the judiciary; rather, the holder of the option, after the realization of its cause in reality, can exercise his option to rescind the contract, so his rescission is effective before any judicial ruling, as in the case of the option of stipulation and the option of the splitting of the deal. (1) As if there is an old defect in the sold item and a new defect occurs with the buyer, then he is prevented from rescinding the sale due to the old defect.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of contractual options (khiyarat) and their effect on the bindingness of contracts. The author explains that the existence of an option for a party strips the contract of its binding character from the beginning. Hanafi jurists consider the absence of an option as a condition for a contract to be binding. The passage then divides options into two categories: (1) options that arise from the agreement of the parties, such as khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-naqd, which are considered contractual conditions; and (2) options that are established by Shari'ah to protect a party due to defects in consent, such as khiyar al-ghabn (option for fraud) or khiyar al-'ayb (option for defect). Furthermore, it distinguishes options that require judicial intervention (like khiyar al-'ayb because it requires expertise) from those that do not (like khiyar al-shart, where the holder can rescind without a judge). This is a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: some khiyarat are self-executing, while others need court confirmation. The footnote gives an example of a bar to rescission: if a buyer discovers an old defect but also causes a new defect, he loses the right to rescind due to the old defect.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of stipulation: a condition agreed upon by the parties to have the right to rescind within a specified period.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the buyer can rescind if a pre-existing defect is discovered.
خلابة (khilabah): Deception or misrepresentation affecting consent.
تدليس (tadlis): Fraud by concealing a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following options requires a judge's ruling to be effective?
A) Khiyar al-shart
B) Khiyar al-naqd
C) Khiyar al-'ayb
D) Khiyar tafarruq al-safqah

Key Takeaway
The existence of an option strips bindingness from the contract; Hanafi law classifies options by source and procedural requirement, with khiyar al-'ayb requiring judicial confirmation and khiyar al-shart being self-executing.

Retroactive Rescission in Binding Contracts (Al-Faskh al-Mustanid)
Definition and scope of retroactive rescission in Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between retroactive (mustanid) and prospective (muqtasir) rescission in Hanafi contract law, and apply the principle of retroactivity to cases of invalidity (fasad), coercion (ikrah), and option (khiyar).

Core Idea
Rescission in binding contracts due to fasad, ikrah, or khiyar voids the contract from the start — it never existed.

This lesson introduces a key principle in Hanafi muamalat: when a binding contract is rescinded due to invalidity, coercion, or the exercise of an option, the rescission is retroactive. This means the contract is considered as if it never happened. In contrast, rescission in non-binding contracts only ends the contract from the moment of rescission. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct fatwa application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three triggers
Fasad (invalidity), ikrah (coercion), and khiyar (option) are the only cases in which rescission in a binding contract is retroactive.
These are distinct from rescission in non-binding contracts.
2
Understand faskh mustanid vs. muqtasir
Faskh mustanid cancels the contract from its inception; faskh muqtasir only terminates it from the time of rescission.
The term mustanid means 'based on' — the rescission is based on a defect present from the start.
3
Recognize the legal effect
The contract is void ab initio; possession and payments are retroactively invalid, and parties must return to their original positions.
This protects the wronged party.
4
Apply to examples
The text mentions khiyar al-kammiyyah and khiyar kashf al-hal. In both, exercising the option retroactively voids the sale.
These options arise when quantity or measurement is unknown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠/٤ - هذا ويجب أن يلاحظ في هذه الحالات الثلاث التي ينسلخ فيها اللزوم عن العقود اللازمة بسبب الفساد أو الإكراه أو الخيار، أنَّ فسخ العقد فيها يكون مستنداً لا مقتصراً، أي أنه يكون له انعطاف وتأثير رجعي ينسحب فيه الانفساخ على الماضي، فيجعل العقد كأنه لم ينعقد من أصله. وذلك على خلاف حكم الفسخ بسبب عدم اللزوم في العقود غير اللازمة كما تقدم (ر: ف ٧/٤٠).
١١/٤٠ - وهذه الخيارات العقدية التي تنشأ عن ظهور حالات تشوب إرادة العاقد بشائبة من عيوب الرضا، وتسلب اللزوم من العقد اللازم هي كثيرة غير محصورة بما تقدم ذكره في بحث شوائب الإرادة. وقد أوصلها الفقهاء إلى أكثر من عشرين خياراً منها خيار الكمية وخيار كشف الحال وغيرهما (۱) .
وأحكامها مفصَّلة في مظانها ومناسباتها من كتب الفقه (ر: «الدر المختار ورد المحتار أول باب خيار الشرط ٤٥/٤ - ٤٦ ؛ «الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم بحاشية الحموي أول أحكام الفسوخ، ج ۲، الفن ، ص / ١٩٤ - ١٩٥).
وقد تناولت المجلة من الخيارات بالبحث سبعة أساسية هي: خيار الشرط، والوصف والنقد والتعيين ،والرؤية ،والعيب والغبن بالتغرير ؛
(۱) خيار الكمية: كما لو اشترى شيئاً بما في جيبه أو حقيبته من النقود دون بيان مقدارها، فإن للبائع الخيار عندما يعرف مقدارها. وخيار كشف الحال كما في البيع بمكيال أو بوزن حجر لا يعلم مقداره بالوحدات القياسية العرفية، فإن للمشتري الخيار متى علم مقدار سعة المكيال ووزن الحجر. (ر: رد المحتار ٢٢/٤ و (۲۷).
10/4 - And it must be noted in these three cases in which bindingness is stripped from binding contracts due to invalidity (fasad), coercion (ikrah), or option (khiyar), that the rescission of the contract in them is retroactive (mustanid) not merely effective from the time of rescission (muqtasir), i.e., it has a backward effect and retroactive impact such that the rescission extends to the past, making the contract as if it had never been concluded from its origin. This is contrary to the ruling of rescission due to lack of bindingness in non-binding contracts as previously mentioned (see: F 7/40). 11/40 - And these contractual options that arise from the emergence of situations that taint the will of the contracting party with some defect of consent, and strip the bindingness from the binding contract, are many, not limited to what was previously mentioned in the discussion of defects of will. The jurists have counted them as more than twenty, including the option of quantity (khiyar al-kammiyyah) and the option of discovery (khiyar kashf al-hal) and others (1). Their detailed rulings are in their appropriate places in the fiqh books (see: Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, beginning of the chapter on khiyar al-shart 4/45-46; Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym with Hamawi's commentary, beginning of rulings on rescissions, vol. 2, art, pp. 194-195). The Majallah covered seven basic options in its research: the option of stipulation, description, cash, specification, sight, defect, and deception-based fraud (al-ghabn bi al-taghrir). (1) Khiyar al-kammiyyah: As if someone buys something with the money in his pocket or bag without specifying the amount, then the seller has the option when he knows the amount. And khiyar kashf al-hal: as in a sale by a measure or by a stone weight whose quantity is not known in conventional units, then the buyer has the option when he knows the capacity of the measure and the weight of the stone. (See: Radd al-Muhtar 4/22 and (27).)
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage presents two important points. First, it establishes the principle that rescission (faskh) in the three cases of invalidity (fasad), coercion (ikrah), and option (khiyar) is retroactive: it cancels the contract ab initio, as if it never existed. This is contrary to rescission in non-binding contracts (e.g., khiyar al-shart in some views) where rescission only has prospective effect. Second, it lists many types of khiyar beyond those previously discussed, noting that the jurists have identified over twenty, including khiyar al-kammiyyah (option of quantity) and khiyar kashf al-hal (option of discovery of reality). The author references the Majallah's treatment of seven basic options. This shows the richness of Hanafi khiyar law. The footnote gives examples of khiyar al-kammiyyah (where the price is unknown) and khiyar kashf al-hal (where the measurement tool is non-standard). These are rare options but important for understanding the comprehensive nature of when a contract may be non-binding.

Key Terms
فسخ مستند (faskh mustanid): Retroactive rescission that annuls the contract from the beginning.
خيار الكمية (khiyar al-kammiyyah): Option of quantity: when the exact quantity or price is unknown, the party has an option upon knowing.
خيار كشف الحال (khiyar kashf al-hal): Option of discovery: when the measurement tool is non-standard, buyer has option upon discovering the true measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does rescission in cases of fasad, ikrah, and khiyar affect the contract?
A) It only terminates the contract from the time of rescission forward.
B) It makes the contract as if it never existed from the beginning.
C) It renders the contract void only if confirmed by a judge.
D) It has no effect on the contract's past.

Key Takeaway
Rescission due to fasad, ikrah, or khiyar is retroactive, voiding the contract ab initio.

The Hanbali View on Muqtada al-‘Aqd and Its Limits
Explaining the limits of contractual conditions in Hanbali fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanbali approach to contractual conditions from the stricter Hanafi view, and apply the 'essential purpose' test to determine validity.

Core Idea
A condition only invalidates a contract if it defeats the Sharia purpose itself, not just adds a benefit.

This passage explores the Hanbali school's nuanced position on the contract's inherent requirement (muqtada al-‘aqd). While Hanbalis do use this concept to restrict conditions, they interpret it more broadly than other schools. They focus on whether the condition undermines the fundamental Sharia objective of the contract, rather than every stipulation that goes beyond the default terms. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and contrasts it with the Hanafi approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concept
Read the passage and locate the term 'muqtada al-‘aqd'. Understand that it refers to the essential legal effects a contract is meant to produce, beyond which conditions are problematic.
Note: The Hanbali definition is narrower than the Hanafi one.
2
Determine the Hanbali criterion
Identify the key phrase: 'the Sharia purpose of the contract (al-ghaya al-shar‘iyya)'. The Hanbalis only invalidate conditions that contradict this purpose, not merely any extra benefit.
They consider the party's benefit as part of the contract's benefit if legitimate.
3
Analyze the two marriage examples
Study the two cases: (1) stipulating that no marital pleasure is lawful (voids the contract); (2) stipulating non-practice of enjoyment (condition void, contract valid). Ask: why does one void the contract and the other not?
The first negates the purpose entirely; the second contradicts an essential effect but the purpose remains.
4
Contrast with the Hanafi view
Recall the Hanafi rule: any condition that adds a benefit not inherent in the contract is batil (void) and may render the contract fasid if it conflicts with muqtada. Compare with Hanbali flexibility.
Hanafis often invalidate conditions that impose extra obligations (e.g., penalty clauses).
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Consider a sale contract where the buyer stipulates free delivery. Under Hanbali view, this does not defeat the purpose of sale, so it is valid as long as not prohibited. Under Hanafi, it may be valid if not gharar.
Focus on the 'essential purpose' test.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (42) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
ثم أورد رحمه الله نصوص الشريعة الدالة على هذا المبدأ مما أوردناه في أول البحث (ر) فتاوى ابن تيمية 239/3).

42/6 - موقف الاجتهاد الحنبلي من نظرية مقتضى العقد :
هذا، وإن الفقه الحنبلي لم يهمل نظرية مقتضى العقد التي تمسك بها من ضيق على نفسه من رجال المذاهب الأخرى. فكثيراً ما يمنع فقهاء الحنابلة بعض الشروط في بعض العقود ويعللون بمخالفة ذلك الشرط لمقتضى العقد.
ولكنهم أوسع نظراً في تفسير مقتضى العقد وحدوده. فهم لا يعتبرون كغيرهم كل مصلحة يشترطها أحد العاقدين لنفسه مما لا يوجبها العقد بذاته تكون منافية لمقتضاه ؛ بل يعتبرون مصلحة العاقد من مصلحة العقد نفسه ما دامت مشروعة، أي غير محرَّمة شرعاً. فلا يعتبر الشرط منافياً لمقتضى العقد في نظر المحققين من الحنابلة إلا في النواحي الأساسية التي إذا شرط خلافها تعطلت الغاية الشرعية من العقد فعندئذ قد يبطل العقد بالشرط، وقد يبطل الشرط ويصح العقد، وذلك على حسب العقد، وذلك على حسب حال الشرط ودرجة مناقضته لغاية العقد.
وعلى هذا الأساس قالوا : لو شرط في عقد النكاح أن لا تحل به المتعة الزوجية، يبطل العقد بهذا الشرط، إذ لا يبقى معه للزواج معنى بل يكون كالعقد الصوري.
- أما لو شرط فيه عدم ممارسة الاستمتاع الزوجي فإن النكاح يصح ويلغو الشرط لأنه ينافي مقتضى العقد، إذ لا يعقل أن يكون الاستمتاع حلالاً بالعقد وممنوعاً بالشرط. وإنما لم يبطل العقد هنا لأن غايته الشرعية، وهي حل المتعة، حاصلة ومتفق بين الطرفين على ثبوتها والشرط المذكور أمر زائد غير جائز فيلغو هو دون العقد.
Chapter (42): Ijtihads on the Authority of the Contractual Will. Then he (Ibn Taymiyya, may Allah have mercy on him) cited the texts of Sharia indicating this principle from what we mentioned at the beginning of the research (refer to Ibn Taymiyya's Fatawa 3/239). 42/6 - The position of Hanbali ijtihad on the theory of the contract's requirement (muqtada al-‘aqd): Indeed, Hanbali fiqh did not neglect the theory of the contract's requirement that those who restricted themselves from other schools adhered to. Hanbali jurists often prevent some conditions in some contracts and justify it by that condition contradicting the contract's requirement. However, they are broader in their interpretation of the contract's requirement and its limits. They do not consider, like others, every benefit that one of the contracting parties stipulates for himself which the contract itself does not necessitate to be contrary to its requirement; rather, they consider the benefit of the contracting party as part of the benefit of the contract itself as long as it is legitimate, i.e., not prohibited by Sharia. Therefore, in the view of verified Hanbali scholars, a condition is not considered contrary to the contract's requirement except in fundamental aspects where if the opposite is stipulated, the Sharia purpose of the contract is defeated; then the contract may be invalidated by the condition, or the condition may be invalidated and the contract valid, depending on the contract and depending on the condition and its degree of contradiction to the purpose of the contract. On this basis they said: If one stipulates in a marriage contract that marital pleasure will not be lawful by it, the contract is invalidated by this condition, because no meaning of marriage remains with it; rather it becomes like a formal contract. As for if one stipulates in it the non-practice of marital enjoyment, then the marriage is valid and the condition is void because it contradicts the contract's requirement, since it is inconceivable that enjoyment be lawful by the contract and prohibited by the condition. The contract is not invalidated here because its Sharia purpose, which is the lawfulness of pleasure, is achieved and agreed upon by both parties; the mentioned condition is an extra impermissible matter, so it is void without the contract.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter 42, which discusses various ijtihads regarding the authority of the will in contracts. The author focuses on the Hanbali school's approach to the concept of 'muqtada al-‘aqd' (the contract's requirement). He notes that Hanbali jurists do use this theory to invalidate certain conditions, but their interpretation is broader than that of other schools. They distinguish between conditions that defeat the essential Sharia purpose of the contract (e.g., a condition denying marital pleasure altogether) and conditions that are merely external benefits for one party. In the former case, the contract is void; in the latter, the condition is void but the contract remains valid. Two examples from marriage are given: (1) stipulating that no marital pleasure is lawful makes the marriage void because it negates the purpose; (2) stipulating non-practice of enjoyment does not void the marriage because the essential purpose (lawfulness of pleasure) remains intact; only the extra condition is void. This reflects a nuanced approach that balances contractual freedom with the preservation of Sharia objectives.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtaḍā al-ʿaqd): The essential legal requirement inherent in a contract that cannot be contravened without defeating its Sharia purpose
الغاية الشرعية من العقد (al-ghāya al-sharʿiyya min al-ʿaqd): The Sharia purpose of the contract, which if defeated by a condition, invalidates the contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali school, what is the consequence if a condition in a marriage contract stipulates that marital pleasure is not lawful?
A) The condition is void but the contract is valid
B) Both contract and condition are void
C) The contract is void but the condition is valid
D) The contract is valid only if both parties agree

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali school uses a purpose-based test for contractual conditions, voiding only those that fundamentally undermine the Sharia objective of the contract.

Invalid Conditions in Rahn and the General Rule
Specific invalid conditions in pledge and the general rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qa'ida that conditions contradicting the muqtada of a contract are void, and identify invalid conditions in pledge contracts.

Core Idea
Conditions that defeat the essential purpose of a contract are invalid, even if mutually agreed.

This lesson examines specific invalid conditions in pledge (rahn) contracts and extracts the general legal maxim that conditions contradicting the inherent purpose of a contract are void. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti evaluating conditions in modern financial contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific invalid conditions in rahn
List the conditions mentioned: no sale of pledge at maturity, no recovery of debt from its price, option for pledgor, and time limit for pledge.
These are examples from the Hanbali school, but the principle is universal.
2
Understand why each condition is fasid
Each condition defeats the creditor's essential right to recover the debt from the pledge. The purpose (muqtada) of rahn is to secure the debt.
If the condition negates the purpose, it is invalid by definition.
3
Note the internal Hanbali ikhtilaf
Some Hanbali scholars say the whole contract becomes void; others say only the condition is void. This affects the practical outcome.
The Hanafi position is generally that the condition alone is void unless it is fundamental.
4
Generalize to the qa'ida
The underlying rule: 'Conditions that contradict the muqtada of the contract are invalid.' This applies across all contracts.
Muqtada refers to the legal effects and objectives inherent in the contract's definition.
5
Apply the qa'ida to other contracts
Think of a sale contract: a condition that the buyer cannot resell the item would be invalid because it contradicts the purpose of ownership.
The test is whether the condition undermines the very reason the contract exists.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصرحوا في الرهن بأنه لا يصح فيه اشتراط أن لا يباع الرهن عند حلول الدين، أو أن لا يستوفي الدين من ثمنه. وكذا لا يصح اشتراط الخيار للراهن، ولا توقيت الرهن وعللوا ذلك بأنَّ هذه كلها شروط فاسدة لمنافاتها مقتضى العقد، فإن المقصود بعقد الرهن مع الوفاء بهذه الشروط، مفقود. واختلفوا هل يبطل الرهن بهذه الشروط، أو يلغو الشرط فقط. (ر : المغني ٤٢٩/٤) . وهكذا في معظم العقود يميزون في كل منها بين الشروط الصحيحة والشروط الفاسدة، ويعللون فسادها بأنها تنافي مقتضى العقد بهذا المعنى الذي بيناه . (۱) هنا قيد ملحوظ أي من مصلحة العاقد المشروعة، وهي التي لم يحرمها الشرع ..
And they stated explicitly regarding the pledge (rahn) that it is not valid to stipulate in it that the pledge should not be sold when the debt is due, or that the debt should not be recovered from its price. Likewise, it is not valid to stipulate an option for the pledger, nor to fix a time limit for the pledge. They justified this by saying that all these are invalid conditions because they contradict the requirements of the contract; indeed, the purpose of the pledge contract when these conditions are met is lost. And they differed whether the pledge becomes invalid due to these conditions or whether only the condition is void. (Reference: al-Mughni 4/429) And similarly in most contracts, they distinguish between valid and invalid conditions, and they explain the invalidity by saying that they contradict the requirements of the contract in this sense that we have explained. (1) Here there is a notable restriction: the legitimate interest of the contracting party, which the Sharia has not prohibited.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of invalid conditions, focusing on the pledge (rahn) contract. The Hanbali school lists specific conditions that are invalid because they defeat the very purpose of the pledge: securing a debt. For example, stipulating that the pledge cannot be sold at maturity or that the debt cannot be recovered from its sale price negates the creditor's right to recover the debt. Similarly, an option for the pledger or a time limit contradicts the nature of the pledge as a security. The passage then notes an internal disagreement within the Hanbali school: whether such invalid conditions nullify the entire contract or just become void while the contract remains valid. Finally, it generalizes this principle to all contracts: conditions that contradict the contract's 'muqtada' (inherent requirements) are invalid. The footnote reiterates that the interest must be legitimate.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or collateral given to secure a debt; a contract that creates a right of retention.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): An invalid condition that either contradicts the contract's nature or is prohibited by Sharia.
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-aqd): The inherent legal requirements and purpose of a contract, established by Sharia, which parties cannot change by agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the conditions listed in the rahn contract considered fasid?
A) Because they are explicitly prohibited by the Quran.
B) Because they contradict the muqtada (requirements) of the contract.
C) Because they are not customary in commercial practice.
D) Because they make the contract ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The core principle: any condition that defeats the essential purpose of a contract is void, even if mutually agreed; the contract itself remains valid unless the condition is fundamental.

Non-Binding Conditions in Hanbali Law: Right of Rescission
Defines a Hanbali qawida on non-binding conditions with rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanbali principle of non-binding conditions and apply it to determine the right of rescission in marriage contracts.

Core Idea
A valid condition may be non-binding; its breach gives the right to rescind, not compel performance.

The Hanbali school introduces a distinctive principle regarding contractual conditions: some conditions are valid and stipulable but not legally binding on the other party. The key consequence is not enforcement of the condition itself, but a right of rescission for the stipulating party if the condition is breached. This lesson unpacks this qawida through examples from marriage contracts and examines an internal Hanbali dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Locate the statement in the passage that defines the new Hanbali principle: a condition may be valid (sahih) but non-binding (ghayr mulzim).
2
Understand the Legal Effect
The key effect is not enforcement but a right of rescission (faskh al-'aqd) for the stipulating party upon non-fulfillment.
3
Apply to Marriage Examples
Analyze the examples: a woman stipulating that her husband not travel or not marry another. The husband is not compelled, but if he violates, she can annul the marriage.
4
Note the Internal Ikhtilaf
Identify the dispute about the condition where a second wife stipulates that the husband divorce his first wife. The majority holds it valid, but Imam Ibn Qudamah holds it invalid.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
According to the tarjih principles of the Hanbali madhhab, Ibn Qudamah's view (invalidity) is preponderant, so such a condition does not even give a right of rescission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤/٤٢ - الناحية الثانية :
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
وقد تفرع عن تلك الناحية الأولى أن جاءنا الاجتهاد الحنبلي بمبدأ جديد هام في قاعدة الشروط العقدية، وهو أن الشرط قد يكون في ذاته وبحسب طبيعته غير ملزم شرعاً للمشروط عليه فهو في الشروط كالعقد ذلك يصح اشتراطه ولا يلغو فتكون ثمرة غير اللازم في العقود، ومع صحة اشتراطه، رغم عدم لزومه، أنَّ الطرف المشروط لمصلحته يحق له فسخ العقد عند عدم وفاء الطرف الآخر بالشرط.
•
ففي هذا النوع من الشروط يكون الطرفان مخيرين: فكما أن الطرف المشروط عليه مخيَّر في الوفاء بالشرط وعدمه يكون الطرف المشروط له مخيراً بين الاستمرار وفسخ العقد في حالة عدم الوفاء بالشرط.
وذلك كما لو اشترطت المرأة في عقد النكاح على الرجل أن لا يسافر أو لا يتزوج عليها»، فإنَّ الرجل حينئذ إنما يستحسن له شرعاً أن يفي بهذه الشروط ما استطاع لكن لا يجب عليه الوفاء ولا يجبر عليه قضاء، لأن الشرع أطلق له حرية السعي والسفر والانتقال، إذ هو أعرف بمواطن رزقه وتتبع حاجاته، كما أطلق له حق التزوج بحدود رسمها الشرع. ولكنه إذا لم يف بالشرط فسافر أو تزوج غيرها كان لها فسخ النكاح بينها وبينه . - حتى إن الحنابلة جوزوا على هذه الطريقة شروطاً مكروهة شرعاً تمس غير العاقدين، ما دامت النتيجة في هذا النوع من الشروط ليست هي الإلزام بتنفيذ الشرط، بل هي حق الفسخ عند عدم تحققه.
وذلك كما لو اشترطت المرأة الثانية في عقد النكاح على زوجها أن يطلق امرأته الأولى. فقد اختلفوا في صحة هذا الشرط، والمحققون على صحته (۱)، (۱) لأنه من مصلحة المرأة الشارطة وإن لم يُلزم به الزوج، فيحق
(۱) إن الإمام موفق الدين بن قدامة المقدسي نص على عدم صحة هذا الشرط، لكن أكثر أصحاب المذهب الحنبلي على الصحة وقواعد الترجيح في المذهب تعتبر قول الشيخ الموفق عند الاختلاف هو الراجح الصحيح (ر: تصحيح الفروع ٥٦/٣).
14/42 - The Second Aspect: Chapter (42) Ijtihads on the Authority of Contractual Will. And from that first aspect branches a new important principle from Hanbali ijtihad in the rule of contractual conditions: that a condition may be, in itself and by its nature, non-binding legally for the person on whom it is stipulated; it is in conditions like a contract in that its stipulation is valid and not null, so it yields the consequence of a non-binding element in contracts. And with the validity of its stipulation, despite its non-bindingness, is that the party for whose benefit it is stipulated has the right to rescind the contract upon the other party's failure to fulfill the condition. So in this type of condition, both parties have options: just as the party on whom the condition is stipulated has the choice to fulfill or not, the stipulating party has the choice between continuing or rescinding the contract in case of non-fulfillment. For example, if a woman stipulates in the marriage contract that the man not travel or not marry another woman, then it is commendable for the man to fulfill these conditions as much as possible, but he is not obligated to fulfill nor compelled by the judiciary, because the Law has given him freedom of effort, travel, and movement, as he knows best his means of livelihood and pursuit of needs, just as it has given him the right to marry within the limits set by the Law. But if he does not fulfill the condition and travels or marries another, she has the right to annul the marriage between her and him. Indeed, the Hanbalis permitted in this way conditions that are legally disliked, affecting non-contracting parties, as long as the result in this type of condition is not the enforcement of the condition, but rather the right of rescission upon its non-realization. For example, if the second woman stipulates in the marriage contract that her husband divorce his first wife. They differed on the validity of this condition; the investigators hold it valid (1), because it is in the interest of the stipulating woman even if the husband is not bound by it, so she has the right... (1) Imam Muwaffaq al-Din Ibn Qudamah al-Maqdisi textually held the invalidity of this condition, but most Hanbali scholars hold it valid, and the principles of weighting in the madhhab consider the statement of Shaykh al-Muwaffaq in case of disagreement to be the preponderant correct one (see Tashih al-Furu' 3/56).
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage defines a new Hanbali legal principle regarding conditions in contracts, specifically marriage. It explains that some conditions are valid but not legally binding on the other party; however, the stipulating party has the right to annul the contract if the condition is breached. The author provides examples: a woman stipulating that her husband not travel or not marry another; the husband is not legally compelled to comply, but if he violates, she can annul the marriage. This extends even to conditions that are religiously disliked, like a second wife stipulating that the husband divorce the first. The passage notes an internal Hanbali dispute: Ibn Qudamah rejects such a condition, but the majority accept it, and the tarjih (preponderance) favors the majority view. The fatwa rule is that the right of rescission is the key effect, not enforcement of the condition itself.

Key Terms
شرط غير ملزم (shart ghayr mulzim): A condition that is valid and stipulable but not legally binding; failure to fulfill gives the other party the right to rescind the contract.
فسخ العقد (faskh al-‘aqd): Annulment or rescission of the contract, which is the remedy for breach of a non-binding condition in Hanbali jurisprudence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal effect of a non-binding condition in Hanbali law according to this passage?
A) The condition is void and ignored.
B) The condition is binding and enforceable by the judge.
C) The condition is valid and gives the stipulating party the right to rescind if breached.
D) The condition is recommended but has no legal effect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbali qawida distinguishes between validity and bindingness: a valid condition can be non-binding, and its breach gives a right of rescission, not enforcement.

Non-Binding Conditions (Sharṭ Ghayr Lāzim) and Right of Rescission in Hanbali Fiqh
Explains Hanbali concept of non-binding conditions with marriage examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-binding condition (sharṭ ghayr lāzim) in the Hanbali school and apply the rule that its breach gives the beneficiary the right of rescission, not enforcement.

Core Idea
A condition can be valid but non-binding; the remedy is rescission, not compulsion.

The Hanbali school introduces a nuanced category of contractual conditions that are valid to stipulate but not legally enforceable. This lesson explores that concept through marriage contract examples, contrasting it with the Hanafi position that such conditions are void.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept
Identify the key feature: a condition in itself is not legally binding (ghayr lāzim) but is valid to stipulate. The party for whose benefit it is made has the right to rescind (faskh) the contract if breached.
This is distinct from void conditions (bāṭil) which are invalid from the start.
2
Apply to marriage examples
Wife stipulates husband not travel or not marry another; he does. Hanbali: condition valid, wife may rescind marriage. Hanafi: condition void, wife cannot rescind for this reason.
The Hanbali view gives a remedy even though the condition is not enforceable.
3
Examine the second example
Second wife stipulates husband divorce first wife; he refuses. Hanbali majority: condition valid (though makrūh) because it gives rescission right, not enforcement.
The condition affects a third party, but since it only grants rescission, it is tolerated.
4
Contrast with Hanafi principle
Hanafis reject this category: conditions that conflict with the nature of the contract (muqtada al-ʿaqd) or absolute rights are void. The effects of ownership and marriage are fixed by Sharīʿa, not parties.
This stems from different views on party autonomy (sulṭān al-irādah).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In a Hanafi context, the wife has no remedy; in a Hanbali context, she may rescind. A mufti must know which school applies.
The dispute is legitimate ikhtilāf; both positions have strength within their own usul.

Source Text
