١٤/٤٢ - الناحية الثانية :
الفصل (٤٢) الاجتهادات في سلطان الإرادة العقدية
وقد تفرع عن تلك الناحية الأولى أن جاءنا الاجتهاد الحنبلي بمبدأ جديد هام في قاعدة الشروط العقدية، وهو أن الشرط قد يكون في ذاته وبحسب طبيعته غير ملزم شرعاً للمشروط عليه فهو في الشروط كالعقد ذلك يصح اشتراطه ولا يلغو فتكون ثمرة غير اللازم في العقود، ومع صحة اشتراطه، رغم عدم لزومه، أنَّ الطرف المشروط لمصلحته يحق له فسخ العقد عند عدم وفاء الطرف الآخر بالشرط.
•
ففي هذا النوع من الشروط يكون الطرفان مخيرين: فكما أن الطرف المشروط عليه مخيَّر في الوفاء بالشرط وعدمه يكون الطرف المشروط له مخيراً بين الاستمرار وفسخ العقد في حالة عدم الوفاء بالشرط.
وذلك كما لو اشترطت المرأة في عقد النكاح على الرجل أن لا يسافر أو لا يتزوج عليها»، فإنَّ الرجل حينئذ إنما يستحسن له شرعاً أن يفي بهذه الشروط ما استطاع لكن لا يجب عليه الوفاء ولا يجبر عليه قضاء، لأن الشرع أطلق له حرية السعي والسفر والانتقال، إذ هو أعرف بمواطن رزقه وتتبع حاجاته، كما أطلق له حق التزوج بحدود رسمها الشرع. ولكنه إذا لم يف بالشرط فسافر أو تزوج غيرها كان لها فسخ النكاح بينها وبينه . - حتى إن الحنابلة جوزوا على هذه الطريقة شروطاً مكروهة شرعاً تمس غير العاقدين، ما دامت النتيجة في هذا النوع من الشروط ليست هي الإلزام بتنفيذ الشرط، بل هي حق الفسخ عند عدم تحققه.
وذلك كما لو اشترطت المرأة الثانية في عقد النكاح على زوجها أن يطلق امرأته الأولى. فقد اختلفوا في صحة هذا الشرط، والمحققون على صحته (۱)، (۱) لأنه من مصلحة المرأة الشارطة وإن لم يُلزم به الزوج، فيحق لها فسخ النكاح إذا لم يف الزوج بتطليق ضرتها كما شرط (ر: كشاف القناع شرح الإقناع بحث الشروط الصحيحة في النكاح).
١٥/٤ - الناحية الثالثة :
إنَّ الاجتهاد الحنبلي سوّغ تقييد الملك المطلق عند مباشرة عقده بشروط تحفظية تمنع بعض تصرفات المالك، أو تقيد طرق الانتفاع، أو تستثني بعض الحقوق، أو توجب على المالك بعض الواجبات .
- فجوز لبائع الأمة الرقيقة أن يشترط على المشتري عدم بيعها، بل يختصها بالتسري والاستيلاد، لأنها قد تكون عزيزة على البائع فيريد أن يحفظ لها الصيانة والكرامة .
- وكذلك جوز اشتراط البائع على المشتري أنه إذا أراد بيع المبيع فالبائع أحق باسترداده بثمنه فليس للمشتري عندئذ أن يبيعه على غير بائعه.
- وجوّز أيضاً بيع الشيء مع شرط احتفاظ البائع بمنفعته مدة معينة.
- وجوز أيضاً بيع العقار على شرط أن يقفه المشتري، وبيع العبد على شرط أن يعتقه، ونحو ذلك.
وهذه الشروط وأمثالها لا تقبلها الاجتهادات الأخرى غير المذهب الحنبلي، لأنها عندهم تعارض ما يقتضيه عقد البيع، وما يستلزمه الملك المطلق من حرية تصرف المشتري فيما اشتراه فإنَّ ثمرات الملك وحقوقه لا يرتبها العاقد ؛ وإنما يرتبها الشرع ترتيباً يمنع تجاوز الإنسان فيها على حقوق غيره.
ولكن نظر الاجتهاد الحنبلي في ذلك أن هذه الشروط الإرادية لها تأثير في تحديد آثار العقد بسلطة منحها الشرع العاقدين، وفوضهما بمقتضاها هذا التحديد بحسب مصالحهما. فلا يكون الملك المنتقل بعقد البيع مع هذه الشروط ملكاً مطلقاً بل مقيداً.
14/42 - The Second Aspect: Chapter (42) Ijtihadat on the Authority of Contractual Will. And from that first aspect branched the Hanbali ijtihad bringing an important new principle in the rule of contractual conditions: that a condition may be in itself and by its nature not legally binding upon the one on whom it is placed; it is like the contract in that it is valid to stipulate it and it is not void, so its effect is that of non-bindingness in contracts. And despite the validity of stipulating it, despite its non-bindingness, the party for whose benefit the condition was made has the right to rescind the contract when the other party does not fulfill the condition. So in this type of condition, both parties are given a choice: just as the party upon whom the condition is placed is free to fulfill it or not, the party for whom the condition is made is free between continuing and rescinding the contract in case of non-fulfillment of the condition. For example, if a woman stipulates in the marriage contract upon the man that he not travel or not marry another woman, then it is recommended for the man legally to fulfill these conditions as much as he can, but he is not obligated to fulfill them nor is he compelled by the judiciary, because the law has granted him freedom to seek livelihood, travel and movement, as he knows better the places of his sustenance and pursuit of his needs, and also granted him the right to marry within the limits set by the law. But if he does not fulfill the condition and travels or marries another, she has the right to rescind the marriage between them. Indeed, the Hanbalis permitted by this method conditions that are legally disliked which affect third parties, as long as the result in this type of condition is not the enforcement of the condition, but rather the right of rescission when it is not fulfilled. For example, if the second wife stipulates in the marriage contract upon her husband that he divorce his first wife. They differed on the validity of this condition, and the verified position is its validity (1), (1) because it is in the interest of the stipulating woman even if the husband is not bound by it, so she has the right to rescind the marriage if the husband does not divorce her co-wife as stipulated (see: Kashshaf al-Qina' sharh al-Iqna', section on valid conditions in marriage).
13/4 - The Third Aspect: The Hanbali ijtihad permitted restricting absolute ownership at the time of concluding its contract by protective conditions that prevent some actions of the owner, or restrict methods of usufruct, or exclude some rights, or impose some obligations on the owner. So they permitted a seller of a slavemaid to stipulate on the buyer not to sell her, but rather to keep her for concubinage and bearing children, because she may be dear to the seller and he wants to preserve her dignity and honor. Likewise, they permitted the seller's stipulation on the buyer that if he wants to sell the purchased item, the seller has the priority to repurchase it at its price; then the buyer may not sell it to anyone else. They also permitted selling a thing with the condition that the seller retains its usufruct for a specified period. They also permitted selling real estate on condition that the buyer endow it (waqf), and selling a slave on condition that the buyer emancipate him, and similar conditions. Such conditions are not accepted by other ijtihadat besides the Hanbali school, because according to them they contradict what the contract of sale requires, and what absolute ownership entails of the buyer's freedom of disposition over what he purchased; for the effects of ownership and its rights are not arranged by the contracting parties; rather, the Law arranges them in a way that prevents a person from transgressing upon the rights of others. But the view of the Hanbali ijtihad is that these voluntary conditions have an effect in determining the effects of the contract by an authority granted by the Law to the contracting parties, and it delegated to them this determination according to their interests. So the ownership transferred by a sale contract with these conditions is not absolute but restricted.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage presents a comparison between the Hanbali madhhab and other schools (primarily the Hanafi school) regarding the scope of party autonomy in contracts. The author discusses two aspects of Hanbali ijtihad:

First aspect (al-nāhīyah al-thāniyah): The Hanbali school introduces the concept of non-binding conditions (shurūṭ ghayr lāzima). These are conditions that are valid to stipulate and do not render the contract void, but they are not legally enforceable; fulfillment is commendable but not obligatory. However, the party for whose benefit the condition is stipulated has the right to rescind the contract if the condition is not fulfilled. This gives a choice to both parties: the one bound by the condition may choose to fulfill it or not, and the beneficiary may choose to continue or cancel. Examples are given from marriage contracts: a woman may stipulate that her husband not travel or not marry another wife; if he does, she may seek dissolution of the marriage. Similarly, a second wife may stipulate that the husband divorce his first wife; although this condition is disliked (makrūh) because it harms a third party, the Hanbalis consider it valid because it only gives the right to rescind, not to compel the husband. The footnote mentions a difference of opinion within the Hanbali school, with Ibn Qudāmah holding invalidity but the majority accepting validity.

Second aspect (al-nāhīyah al-thālithah): The Hanbali school permits restricting absolute ownership (al-milk al-muṭlaq) through conditions in the contract of sale. These conditions may limit the buyer's freedom to dispose of the property, such as prohibiting resale, requiring the buyer to keep the item for a specific purpose, granting the seller a right of preemption in case of resale, retaining usufruct for a period, or requiring the buyer to make waqf or emancipate a slave. Other madhhabs (including the Hanafis) reject these conditions because they oppose the inherent effects of a sale contract, which grant the buyer absolute ownership. They argue that the effects of ownership are determined by the Law, not by the parties. The Hanbali position, however, is based on the principle that the Law has given the contracting parties authority to customize the effects of their contract according to their interests, as long as no specific prohibition is violated. Therefore, ownership under such conditions is not absolute but restricted.

The text highlights the underlying legal philosophy: the Hanafi school (and others) see ownership rights as fixed by the Law and not subject to private modification, while the Hanbali school allows greater contractual freedom to tailor ownership rights to the parties' needs. This is part of a broader discussion on the authority of contractual will (sulṭān al-irādah al-ʿaqdīyah).

Key Terms
شرط غير لازم (sharṭ ghayr lāzim): A condition that is valid to stipulate but not legally binding; fulfillment is recommended but not enforceable; non-fulfillment gives the beneficiary the right to rescind the contract.
سلطان الإرادة العقدية (sulṭān al-irādah al-ʿaqdīyah): The extent of party autonomy in shaping contractual effects beyond the default legal consequences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanbali school, if a second wife stipulates in her marriage contract that her husband must divorce his first wife, what is the ruling?
A) The condition is void because it harms a third party.
B) The condition is valid but disliked; if the husband does not comply, she may rescind the marriage.
C) The husband is legally obligated to divorce the first wife.
D) The condition is null and the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanbali school, non-binding conditions are valid; their breach gives the beneficiary a right to rescind, not enforce.

Pages p. 531 · Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
